Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Suspending Kafalah on a Condition: The Role of Custom
Resolving an intra-Hanafi dispute on suspending kafalah using custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on suspending kafalah on a condition and apply the school's reliance on custom to determine validity.

Core Idea
Suspending kafalah on a condition is valid only if the condition is customary.

The passage addresses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding whether a kafalah contract can be suspended (mu'allaq) on a condition, such as 'If I meet you tomorrow, I am free from the guarantee.' There are two narrations: one prohibits if the condition is non-customary, the other permits if it is customary. The commentator resolves this by relying on 'urf (custom), showing how Hanafi fiqh uses custom to reconcile apparently conflicting texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The issue is whether a guarantor can condition his release from liability on a future event, e.g., meeting the creditor tomorrow.
This is ta'leeq al-kafalah (suspending the guarantee).
2
State the two narrations
One narration forbids suspension if the condition is not customary; another permits it if customary. Recognize that the conflict is resolved by custom.
Both narrations are attributed to the Hanafi school, possibly Sahibayn vs. Abu Hanifa.
3
Understand the role of custom
The commentator explains that the condition referred to in the general prohibition is the non-customary condition. Customary conditions are considered valid because custom ('urf) is a recognized legal source.
This is a common Hanafi methodology: specifying general texts by custom.
4
Apply the resolution
For example, if it is customary in the marketplace for guarantors to suspend liability until a certain event, it is valid. If not, the suspension is void and the guarantor remains liable.
The thamarah al-khilaf: whether the condition is effective or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها
ولا الكفالة بما تعدَّر استيفاؤه من الكفيل، كالحدودِ والقصاص، وبالمبيع بخلاف الثَّمَنِ
قال: (ولا الكفالة بما تعدَّر استيفاؤه من الكفيل، كالحدود والقصاص، وبالمبيع بخلاف الثَّمن) اعلم أن الكفالة بتسليم المبيع تصح. أما إذا كان متعارفاً فإنّه يجوزُ كما في تعليق الكفالة، كما في «الإيضاح»: الكفيل بالمال أو النفس لو قال : إن وافيتُك غداً فأنا بريء من المال ، فوافاه غدا يبرؤ من المال. فقد جوز تعليق البراءةِ عن الكفالة بالمال، وكذا إذا علق البراءة باستيفاء البعض يجوز، أو علق البراءة عن البعض بتعجيل البعض يجوز، ذكره في «مبسوط شيخ الإسلام». فعلم أنَّ المراد بالشرط الشرط : الغير متعارف، واختلاف الروايتين في صحة التعليق محمول على هذا، فرواية عدم الجواز فيما إذا كان غير متعارف، ورواية الجواز فيما إذا كان متعارفا. انتهى، وهاهنا بحث نفيس مذكورٌ في ردّ المحتار) 
[١) قوله : ولا كفالة ؛ لأنَّ الكفالة إنّما تصح بمضمون تجري النيابة في إيفاءه، ولا تجري النيابة في العقوبات ؛ لأنَّ المقصود من شرعها جزاء المفسدين عن الفساد، فلا يمكن إقامتها على غير الجاني، ولأنَّ شرط الكفالة كون المكفول به مقدور التسليم من الكفيل، والحد والقصاص ليسا كذلك. [٢]قوله : كالحدود والقصاص ؛ أي كنفس الحدودِ ونفس القصاص، فإنَّ الكفالة بنفس من عليه الحدود والقصاص تجوز، صرَّحَ به في «البناية)، وغيرها. [٣] قوله : بخلاف الثمن ؛ فإنّه يصحُ الكفالة به عن المشتري ؛ لأنه دين كسائر الديون. (٤) قوله: اعلم ؛ لما كان قول المصنف : وبالمبيع يحتمل احتمالاً تصح الكفالة فيه، واحتمالاً لم تصح، فوجب أن يصرح الشارحُ له بما هو المقصود هاهنا، فقال: واعلم أن الكفالة ... الخ. وتوضيحه : إنَّ الأعيان إمَّا مضمونةٌ بنفسها : وهي ما يجب قيمتها عند الهلاك ، أو بغيرها : وهي ما لا يجب قيمتها، أو أمانة، والأوَّلُ تصح الكفاله به ولا تصح بالثاني والثالث ؛ لفقدان شرطها، وهو أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل، لا يخرج عنه إلا بدفع عينه أو بدله.
(۱) رد المحتار» (٤ : ٣٧٧).
(۲) «البناية» (٦ : ٧٦٠).

وبالمرهون
لكن لو هلكَ لا يَحِبُ على الكفيل شيء ، فمراد «المتن» الكفالة بمالية المبيع ؛ وذلك لأنَّ ماليته غير مضمونة على الأصيل، فإنَّه لو هَلَكَ ينفسخ البيعُ ، ويَحِمُ ردُّ الثَّمَنِ. (وبالمرهون ) : أي بماليته لكن يصح بتسليم المرهون
وتوضيحه : إنَّ الأعيان إما مضمونة بنفسها : وهى ما يجب قيمتها عند الهلاك ، أو بغيرها : وهي ما لا يجب قيمتها ، أو أمانة، والأوَّلُ تصح الكفاله به ولا تصح بالثاني والثالث ؛ لفقدان شرطها، وهو أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل، لا يخرج عنه إلا بدفع عينه أو بدله. وصورة الكفالة بالمبيع أن يقول للمشتري : إن ملك المبيع فعلي بدله أو قيمته ، فمعنى قول المصنف الله : بالمبيع، بماليته ؛ وذلك لأنَّ ماليتَهُ غير مضمونةٍ على الأصيل: فإنه لو هلك المبيع قبل القبض في يد البائع لا يجب على البائع شيء، وإنّما يسقط حقه في الثمن.
معنى وإذا كان المبيع مضموناً على البائع بسقوط حقِّهِ فِي الثَّمَنِ لا بنفسه لا يمكن تحقَّقُ الكفالة، ة، إذ هي ضمُّ الذمَّةِ إلى الذمَّةِ في المطالبة ، ولا يتحقق الضم بين المختلفين، فإنّما ثبت على الأصيل وهو سقوط حقه في الثمن لا يمكن إثباتُهُ في حق الكفيل. وما أمكن إثباته على الكفيل من كونه مضموناً عليه بالقيمة لا يمكن إثباته على الأصيل، بخلاف الكفالة بتسليم المبيع، حيث يصح لتحقق معنى الضم فيها، ونظير الكفالة ببدل الكتابة، حيث لا يمكن الإيجاب على الكفيل بمثل ما وجبَ الكفالة بالمبيع على الأصيل، كذا في «البحر» (١) ، و«الكفاية»(۲)، وغيرهما. [١] قوله : لا يجب على الكفيل شيء ؛ لأنَّ العقد قد انفسخ، ووجب على البائع رد الثمن، والكفيل لم يضمن الثمن. [٢] قوله : وبالمرهون ؛ أي ولا تصح الكفالة بماليَّة المرهون ؛ لأنَّ المرهون غيرُ مضمون على المرتهن بنفسه، وإنما يسقط دينه إذا هلك ، فلا يمكن إيجابُ الصَّمَانِ على الكفيل بما لا يجب على الأصيل.
Chapter of Kafalah: Its definition, conditions, and rulings. There is no kafalah for what is impossible to exact from the guarantor, such as hudud and qisas, and for the sold item, contrary to the price. He said: (There is no kafalah for what is impossible to exact from the guarantor, like hudud and qisas, and for the sold item, contrary to the price). Know that kafalah for delivery of the sold item is valid. As for if it is customary, then it is permissible as in suspending kafalah, as in Al-Idah: the guarantor for money or himself if he says: If I meet you tomorrow then I am free from the money, and he meets him tomorrow, he becomes free from the money. He allowed suspending the release from kafalah for money, and likewise if he suspends the release upon exacting part, it is permissible, or if he suspends the release of part upon accelerating part, it is permissible, mentioned it in Mabsut Shaykh al-Islam. So it is known that what is meant by condition is the non-customary condition, and the difference of the two narrations regarding the validity of suspension is based on this: the narration of impermissibility [applies] to when it is non-customary, and the narration of permissibility [applies] to when it is customary. End. And here is an exquisite investigation mentioned in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His statement: There is no kafalah; because kafalah is only valid for something guaranteed in which substitution in its fulfillment is permissible, and substitution is not permissible in corporal punishments because the purpose of legislating them is to punish the corrupters for corruption, so it cannot be imposed on other than the offender, and because the condition of kafalah is that the object of guarantee be deliverable by the guarantor, and hudud and qisas are not such. [2] His statement: Like hudud and qisas; i.e., the same hudud and the same qisas, for kafalah for the person upon whom are hudud and qisas is permissible, as stated in Al-Binayah and others. [3] His statement: Contrary to the price; for kafalah for it from the buyer is valid because it is a debt like other debts. (4) His statement: Know; since the statement of the author 'by the sold item' carries a possibility that kafalah is valid in it and a possibility that it is not, it was necessary for the commentator to clarify what is intended here, so he said: and know that kafalah... etc. And its clarification: that corporeal properties are either guaranteed in themselves: i.e., what requires its value upon destruction, or by other than themselves: i.e., what does not require its value, or trust property. The first: kafalah for it is valid, and it is not valid for the second and third due to the lack of its condition, which is that it be guaranteed on the principal, not removed from him except by payment of its original or its substitute. 

And with the mortgaged property: but if it perishes, nothing is due from the guarantor. So the intention of the Matn is kafalah for the monetary value of the sold item; and that is because its monetary value is not guaranteed on the principal, for if the sold item perishes, the sale is annulled and the price must be returned. (And with the mortgaged): i.e., its monetary value, but it is valid for delivery of the mortgaged. And its clarification: that corporeal properties are either guaranteed in themselves: i.e., what requires its value upon destruction, or by other than themselves: i.e., what does not require its value, or trust property. The first: kafalah for it is valid, and it is not valid for the second and third due to the lack of its condition, which is that it be guaranteed on the principal, not removed from him except by payment of its original or its substitute. And the form of kafalah for the sold item is that he says to the buyer: 'If you own the sold item, then upon me is its substitute or its value.' So the meaning of the author's statement 'by the sold item' is by its monetary value; and that is because its monetary value is not guaranteed on the principal: for if the sold item perishes before possession in the hands of the seller, nothing is due from the seller, but only his right in the price falls. The meaning: And if the sold item is guaranteed on the seller by the fall of his right in the price, not in itself, kafalah cannot be realized, for it is joining a liability to a liability in demand, and joining cannot occur between two different things. For what is established on the principal—namely the fall of his right in the price—cannot be established against the guarantor. And what can be established against the guarantor—namely that he is responsible for the value—cannot be established against the principal. Contrary to kafalah for delivery of the sold item, where it is valid because the meaning of joining is realized. And analogous is kafalah for the substitute of Kitabah, where it is not possible to impose on the guarantor the same as what is due on the principal. So it is in Al-Bahr, Al-Kifayah, and others. [1] His statement: Nothing is due from the guarantor; because the contract has been annulled and the seller must return the price, and the guarantor did not guarantee the price. [2] His statement: And with the mortgaged; i.e., kafalah for the monetary value of the mortgaged is not valid; because the mortgaged is not guaranteed on the mortgagee in itself, but only his debt falls if it perishes, so it is not possible to impose liability on the guarantor for what is not due from the principal.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship/guarantee) from a Hanafi fiqh text. It begins by stating the general rule: kafalah is not valid for anything that cannot be exacted from the guarantor, such as hadd punishments (fixed corporal punishments) and qisas (retributive justice). This is because kafalah only applies to financial obligations where substitution by a guarantor is permissible. Corporal punishments are personal and cannot be delegated; the purpose of such punishments is to punish the actual offender. Additionally, the condition of kafalah is that the object of guarantee must be deliverable by the guarantor, which is not the case for hudud and qisas. However, kafalah for the person himself who owes hudud or qisas is valid—i.e., guaranteeing that he will appear. The text then contrasts the sold item (mabee') with the price (thaman): kafalah for the price is valid because it is a debt like any other debt, but kafalah for the sold item requires clarification. The sold item, before delivery, is not guaranteed on the seller in the sense of liability for its value; if it perishes, the sale is annulled and only the price is returned. Therefore, kafalah for its monetary value is not valid because the principal is not liable for that value. However, kafalah for delivery of the sold item is valid, as it involves joining a liability to another in demand, which is possible. The commentary also discusses the issue of suspending kafalah on a condition, noting that there are two narrations in the Hanafi school: one prohibits it when the condition is non-customary, and the other permits it when it is customary. Finally, the text states that kafalah for the mortgaged item (marhoon) is also not valid for its monetary value, because the mortgagee is not liable for the value of the mortgaged item; if it perishes, his debt merely falls. Thus, the condition that the object be guaranteed on the principal is not met.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'leeq): Suspending the effect of a contract on a future condition.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom; a recognized source of law in Hanafi fiqh.
متعارف (muta'arif): Customary; commonly practiced in a particular community.
الرواية (al-riwayah): A narrated opinion from the Hanafi school (usually from the Sahibayn or Abu Hanifa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what determines the validity of a condition suspending kafalah?
A) The intention of the guarantor.
B) Whether the condition is customary.
C) The amount of the debt.
D) The presence of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Suspending kafalah on a condition is valid only if the condition is customary; custom resolves the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
12 min
Kafalah for Sold, Pawned, and Trust Items
Rulings on validity of kafalah for specific types of property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for valid kafalah and determine whether kafalah is valid for sold items, pawned items, and trusts.

Core Idea
Kafalah is only valid when the principal is directly liable for the item itself; financial value alone does not suffice.

Kafalah (suretyship) is typically associated with debts, but can it cover specific items like a sold car before delivery or a pawned watch? The Hanafi school answers with a nuanced condition: the principal must be directly liable for the item. This lesson unpacks that condition across three categories of property and explains why the Shafi'i school disagrees.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for valid kafalah
The text states that kafalah requires the subject to be 'guaranteed against the principal' (maḍmūn ʿalā al-aṣīl) such that the principal cannot exit liability except by paying the item or its substitute. Identify this core condition.
This condition distinguishes Hanafi kafalah from a simple promise.
2
Categorize items into three types
Items are either self-guaranteed (maḍmūna bi-nafsihā, e.g., usurped goods), guaranteed by other than itself (maḍmūna bi-ghairihā, e.g., sold item), or trust (amāna). Determine which type meets the condition.
Self-guaranteed items create direct liability for their value.
3
Apply to sold and pawned items
Examine why kafalah for the financial value of a sold item or pawned item fails: the principal (seller/mortgagee) is not directly liable for the item's value; liability is merely a fall of right or debt.
For a sold item before delivery, the seller's liability is only loss of the price, not the item's value.
4
Apply to trusts and the Shafi'i dispute
Trusts are not guaranteed, so kafalah is invalid unless delivery is obligatory (e.g., hired item). Then contrast the Shafi'i position that rejects kafalah for any item because they require the guarantor to be able to fulfill from his own assets.
The Hanafi response: kafalah is joining in claim, not necessarily direct fulfillment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالمرهون ) : أي بماليته لكن يصح بتسليم المرهون وتوضيحه : إنَّ الأعيان إما مضمونة بنفسها: وهى ما يجب قيمتها عند الهلاك ، أو بغيرها : وهي ما لا يجب قيمتها ، أو أمانة، والأوَّلُ تصح الكفاله به ولا تصح بالثاني والثالث ؛ لفقدان شرطها، وهو أن يكون مضموناً على الأصيل، لا يخرج عنه إلا بدفع عينه أو بدله. وصورة الكفالة بالمبيع أن يقول للمشتري : إن ملك المبيع فعلي بدله أو قيمته ، فمعنى قول المصنف الله : بالمبيع، بماليته ؛ وذلك لأنَّ ماليتَهُ غير مضمونةٍ على الأصيل: فإنه لو هلك المبيع قبل القبض في يد البائع لا يجب على البائع شيء، وإنّما يسقط حقه في الثمن. وإذا كان المبيع مضموناً على البائع بسقوط حقِّهِ فِي الثَّمَنِ لا بنفسه لا يمكن تحقَّقُ الكفالة، ة، إذ هي ضمُّ الذمَّةِ إلى الذمَّةِ في المطالبة ، ولا يتحقق الضم بين المختلفين ، فإنّما ثبت على الأصيل وهو سقوط حقه في الثمن لا يمكن إثباتُهُ في حق الكفيل. وما أمكن إثباته على الكفيل من كونه مضموناً عليه بالقيمة لا يمكن إثباته على الأصيل، بخلاف الكفالة بتسليم المبيع، حيث يصح لتحقق معنى الضم فيها، ونظير الكفالة ببدل الكتابة، حيث لا يمكن الإيجاب على الكفيل بمثل ما وجبَ على الأصيل، كذا في «البحر» (١) ، و«الكفاية»(۲)، وغيرهما. [١] قوله : لا يجب على الكفيل شيء ؛ لأنَّ العقد قد انفسخ، ووجب على البائع رد الثمن، والكفيل لم يضمن الثمن. [٢] قوله : وبالمرهون ؛ أي ولا تصح الكفالة بماليَّة المرهون ؛ لأنَّ المرهون غيرُ مضمون على المرتهن بنفسه، وإنما يسقط دينه إذا هلك ، فلا يمكن إيجابُ الصَّمَانِ على الكفيل، وهو ليس واجباً على الأصيل. وشرط صحة الكفالة أن يكون المكفول به بحيث لا يمكن للأصيل أن يخرج عنه إلا بدفعه أو دفع بدله ، فإنَّ الكفالة التزام المطالبة بما هو على الأصيل، فلا بُدَّ أن يكونَ المكفول به واجباً على الأصيل ومضموناً عليه. [١] قوله : لا يجب عليه شيء ؛ فإنَّ عين المرهون إن كان بمقدار الدين أو زائداً عليه، والزيادة عليه من ماليته كان أمانة في يد المرتهن، ولا ضمان فيها. [٢] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي الله ؛ إذ الشَّافعيّة منعوا الكفالة بالأعيان مطلقاً، بناءً على أصلِهِ أنَّ موجب الكفالة التزام أصل الدين في الذمة، فكان محلها هو الدِّينُ دون العين، وإن شرط صحتها قدرة الكفيل على الإيفاء من عنده، وذلك يتصوَّرُ في الديون دون الأعيان. وقلنا بناءً على أصلنا : إِنَّ الكفالة ضمُّ دَمَّةٍ إلى ذمَّةٍ في المطالبة، والمطالبة تقتضي أن يكون المطلوب مضموناً على الأصيل لا محالة، كذا في «العناية»(۲)، وغيرها. [٣] قوله : وبالأمانة، ما كان أمانة فإن كان غير واجب التسليم كالوديعة ومال المضاربة والشركة، فإنَّ الواجب عدم المنع عند الطلب لا التسليم، ولا يجوز الكفالة بتسليمه لعدم وجوبه ، كما لا يجوز بيعها، وإن كان واجب التسليم كالمستأجر إذا ضَمِنَ رجل بتسليمه إلى المستأجر ، كمَن استأجر دابَّة وعجَّل الأجر ، ولم يقبضها وكفل له بذلك كفيل صحت الكفالة وعلى الكفيل تسليمها ما دامت حية.
And with the pawned item (i.e., its financial value) but it is valid with delivery of the pawn. Explanation: Items are either guaranteed by themselves—those for which value is obligatory at destruction—or by other than themselves—those for which value is not obligatory—or trust. The first: kafalah is valid with it, and it is not valid with the second and third due to the lack of its condition, which is that it must be guaranteed against the principal, not exiting except by paying its very self or its substitute. The form of kafalah with the sold item is that he says to the buyer: 'If the ownership of the sold item is lost, then its substitute or value is upon me.' So the meaning of the author's statement 'with the sold item' is 'with its financial value'; because its financial value is not guaranteed against the principal. For if the sold item perishes before taking possession in the seller's hand, nothing is obligatory upon the seller; rather his right to the price falls. And when the sold item is guaranteed against the seller by the fall of his right to the price, not by itself, the realization of kafalah is impossible, because it is the joining of a liability to a liability in the claim, and joining between two different things is not realized. What is established upon the principal—the fall of his right to the price—cannot be established regarding the guarantor, and what can be established upon the guarantor—being guaranteed by the value—cannot be established upon the principal. Unlike kafalah for delivery of the sold item, where it is valid because the meaning of joining is realized. Similarly, kafalah for the substitute of kitabah (contract of manumission) cannot make obligatory upon the guarantor the like of what is obligatory upon the principal. Thus in al-Bahr, al-Kifayah, and others. [1] His statement: 'Nothing is obligatory upon the guarantor' because the contract has been rescinded and returning the price is obligatory upon the seller, and the guarantor did not guarantee the price. [2] His statement: 'And with the pawned item' i.e., kafalah with the financial value of the pawn is not valid because the pawned item is not guaranteed against the mortgagee by itself; his debt merely falls if it perishes. So imposing guarantee upon the guarantor is not possible, while it is not obligatory upon the principal. The condition for validity of kafalah is that the guaranteed matter be such that the principal cannot exit from it except by paying its very self or its substitute. For kafalah is undertaking of claim for what is upon the principal, so it is necessary that the guaranteed matter be obligatory upon the principal and guaranteed against him. [1] His statement: 'Nothing is obligatory upon him' because the very pawn, if equal to or exceeding the debt, and the excess over it from its financial value, is a trust in the mortgagee's hand, with no guarantee. [2] His statement: 'Contrary to al-Shafiʿi' (may Allah have mercy on him) because the Shafiʿis prohibited kafalah for specific items absolutely, based on their principle that the operative cause of kafalah is undertaking the principal of the debt in the liability, so its subject is debt, not the item. And among its conditions is the guarantor's ability to fulfill from his side, which is conceivable in debts, not in items. And we said, based on our principle: kafalah is joining a liability to a liability in the claim, and the claim necessitates that the claimed matter be guaranteed against the principal without fail. Thus in al-ʿInayah and others. [3] His statement: 'And with the trust' — what is a trust, if it is not obligatory to deliver like a deposit, partnership property, and mudarabah capital, then the obligation is to not prevent at request, not delivery. Kafalah for its delivery is not valid because of its non-obligation, just as its sale is not valid. But if delivery is obligatory, like a hired item: if a man guarantees its delivery to the lessee — for example, someone hired an animal and paid the rent in advance, and did not take possession, and a guarantor guaranteed that for him — the kafalah is valid, and the guarantor is obliged to deliver it as long as it is alive.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage examines the validity of kafalah (suretyship) when the subject matter is not a debt but a specific item or its financial value. The author distinguishes three categories: items guaranteed by themselves (e.g., usurped goods), items guaranteed by other than themselves (e.g., sold item before delivery), and trusts. Only the first category qualifies for valid kafalah in Hanafi law, because the condition is that the principal must be directly liable for the item or its substitute. For sold items before delivery, if it perishes, the seller's liability is merely the loss of the right to the price, not a financial obligation. The commentary also explains the Shafiʿi objection: they require the guarantor to be able to fulfill from his own assets, which is only possible for debts, not items. The Hanafi response is that kafalah is about joining liabilities in a claim, not necessarily about direct fulfillment. Finally, the passage addresses trusts: for deposits, partnership assets, and mudarabah capital, there is no obligation to deliver, so kafalah is invalid; but for hired items where delivery is obligatory, kafalah is valid.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): A contract where a guarantor (kafil) joins his liability to the principal's in a claim, making both liable for the same obligation.
مضمونة بنفسها (maḍmūnatun bi-nafsihā): Items that are inherently guaranteed, meaning their value is obligatory upon the holder if destroyed.
مضمونة بغيرها (maḍmūnatun bi-ghairihā): Items that are not directly guaranteed; only the principal's right or debt is affected if they perish.
أمانة (amānah): Items held in trust without liability for destruction unless negligent; includes deposits, partnership assets, and mudarabah capital.
المبيع (al-mabīʿ): The sold item before it is delivered to the buyer; it is not guaranteed by itself against the seller.
المرهون (al-marhūn): The pawned item; it is not guaranteed by itself against the mortgagee; the debt is reduced if it perishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a valid kafalah?
A) A guarantor guarantees the financial value of a sold item before delivery.
B) A guarantor guarantees the delivery of a hired animal that the lessee has paid for but not taken.
C) A guarantor guarantees the financial value of a pawned item.
D) A guarantor guarantees the return of a deposit.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is valid only when the principal is directly liable for the item; financial value alone does not create the necessary liability.

Suretyship for Specific Items: Hanafi Position vs. Shafi'i Objection
Ruling that suretyship is valid only for self-guaranteed items, with Shafi'i objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates suretyship for self-guaranteed items and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Suretyship joins liability for demand; if the item is guaranteed on the principal, suretyship is valid.

This lesson unpacks a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding suretyship (kafalah). While both agree that suretyship is permissible in principle, they differ sharply on its scope: can a person guarantee delivery of a specific item, or only a debt? The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi position and responds to the Shafi'i objection.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Suretyship is valid for items that are legally guaranteed by themselves (e.g., maghsab, fasid sale, sum al-shira'), but invalid for items guaranteed by something else.
2
Identify the Shafi'i view and its basis
Shafi'is forbid suretyship for specific items absolutely; they argue kafalah is undertaking a debt in the dhimmah, so its locus is debt, not 'ayn, and require the surety to be able to fulfill from his own pocket.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
The Hanafi definition is joining dhimmah for demand (mutalabah), not assuming the debt. Demand requires the item be guaranteed on the principal, not that the surety can deliver it himself.
4
Determine the practical consequence
Hanafis allow suretyship for delivering a specific item (e.g., a usurped item); Shafi'is require the surety to guarantee the value instead.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon position is the Hanafi view, as affirmed in al-'Inayah and other sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحاصل أن الكفالة بمالية الأعيان المضمونة بالغير لا تصح، فأما بالأعيان المضمونة بنفسها تصح عندنا خلافاً للشافعي، وذلك مثل: المبيع بيعاً فاسداً، والمغصوب والمقبوض على سوم الشراء، فإنَّه مضمون بالقيمة. وشرط صحة الكفالة أن يكون المكفول به بحيث لا يمكن للأصيل أن يخرج عنه إلا بدفعه أو دفع بدله، فإنَّ الكفالة التزام المطالبة بما هو على الأصيل، فلا بُدَّ أن يكونَ المكفول به واجباً على الأصيل ومضموناً عليه. [٢] قوله : خلافاً للشافعي الله؛ إذ الشَّافعيّة منعوا الكفالة بالأعيان مطلقاً، بناءً على أصلِهِ أنَّ موجب الكفالة التزام أصل الدين في الذمة، فكان محلها هو الدِّينُ دون العين، وإن شرط صحتها قدرة الكفيل على الإيفاء من عنده، وذلك يتصوَّرُ في الديون دون الأعيان. وقلنا بناءً على أصلنا: إِنَّ الكفالة ضمُّ دَمَّةٍ إلى ذمَّةٍ في المطالبة، والمطالبة تقتضي أن يكون المطلوب مضموناً على الأصيل لا محالة، كذا في «العناية»(۲)، وغيرها.
So the conclusion is that suretyship for the monetary value of items that are guaranteed by something else is not valid. As for items that are guaranteed by themselves, it is valid according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. Examples include: an item sold in a corrupt sale, a usurped item, and an item taken on the basis of a purchase proposal, for these are guaranteed by value. And the condition for validity of suretyship is that the object of suretyship is such that the principal cannot discharge it except by delivering it or paying its substitute. For suretyship is an undertaking to demand what is upon the principal, so the object of suretyship must be obligatory upon the principal and guaranteed against him. [2] His statement: 'Contrary to al-Shafi'i' — because the Shafi'ites forbid suretyship for specific items absolutely, based on their principle that the effect of suretyship is undertaking the original debt in the dhimmah, so its locus is debt, not specific items. And the condition for its validity is the ability of the surety to pay from his own side, which is conceivable for debts, not for specific items. And we said based on our principle that suretyship is the joining of a dhimmah to a dhimmah for the purpose of demand, and demand necessitates that what is demanded is guaranteed against the principal inevitably, as in al-'Inayah and others.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position is that suretyship (kafalah) for specific items (a'yan) is valid when those items are legally guaranteed (mamun) on the principal — meaning the principal is liable to pay compensation if they are destroyed. Examples include items sold in a fasid (corrupt) sale, usurped items (maghsab), and items taken under a purchase proposal (sum al-shira'), all of which are guaranteed by their value. The Shafi'is oppose suretyship for specific items entirely. Their reasoning: the obligation of suretyship is the assumption of a debt in the dhimmah (a liability), so its proper locus is debt, not physical items. They also require the surety to be able to fulfill from his own pocket, which is only possible for debts. The Hanafi response: suretyship is essentially joining one liability (dhimmah) to another for the purpose of demanding payment (mutalabah). Demand requires that the claimed item be guaranteed on the principal, period. The Shafi'i condition of ability to pay is not a requirement. The commentator cites al-'Inayah for this principle.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafala): The undertaking to demand what is owed by the principal; joining a dhimmah to another for demand.
المضمونة بنفسها (al-madmunat bi-nafsiha): Items that are legally guaranteed by their own value, such as maghsab or items from a fasid sale.
ضم ذمة إلى ذمة (damm dhimmah ila dhimmah): Joining one liability (of the surety) to another (of the principal) for the purpose of demanding payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the Shafi'is consider suretyship for specific items invalid?
A) Because specific items are not guaranteed on the principal.
B) Because suretyship, in their view, requires assuming a debt in the dhimmah, and specific items are not debts.
C) Because the surety must be able to deliver the item physically.
D) Because the principal must be able to deliver the item.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates suretyship for specific items only when they are legally guaranteed on the principal, while the Shafi'i school rejects it entirely based on the nature of suretyship as debt assumption.

Suretyship for Trust Items: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Loans
Intra-Hanafi dispute on suretyship for loans

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for validity of suretyship for trust items and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding loans (ariyah) by identifying the majority and minority positions and their proofs.

Core Idea
Suretyship to deliver a trust item is valid only when the principal is obliged to deliver; for loans, the majority rules its validity despite Sarakhsi's objection.

This passage examines the validity of suretyship (kafalah) for trust items (amanat) in Hanafi fiqh. The key is whether the principal is obligated to physically deliver the item or merely to enable the owner to take it. Here, we delve into an intra-Hanafi dispute on loans (ariyah), where Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi holds that suretyship for delivery is invalid, while the majority of Hanafi authorities declare it valid. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of kafalah in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Classify trust items by obligation of delivery
Identify which trust items (deposit, loan, rental, mudarabah capital) impose a duty to deliver upon demand and which only require non-prevention.
Deposits and partnership capital are not obligatorily deliverable; the principal's duty is only takhliyah (enabling).
2
Determine the ruling for each category
For non-obligatory delivery, suretyship is invalid. For obligatory delivery (e.g., a rented item after payment), suretyship is valid and the surety must deliver the item if it still exists.
The surety is only liable for the specific item, not its value if it perishes.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on loans
Locate the disagreement: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi holds that suretyship for delivery of a loan (ariyah) is invalid, while other authorities (al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Mabsut, al-Quduri, al-Tuhfah) consider it valid.
The dispute centers on whether the obligation to return a loan is to deliver or merely to vacate.
4
Follow the reasoning of Sarakhsi's view
Sarakhsi argues that the principal's duty is takhliyah (vacating), not physical delivery, so suretyship for delivery is not obligatory and thus invalid.
This is a zanni (speculative) reasoning from a qiyas perspective.
5
Understand the majority's refutation and the relied-upon position
The commentator (and majority) respond that the obligation to return (wajib al-radd) includes enabling, and the surety can fulfill by bringing the item and enabling the owner. This view is supported by al-Dhakhirah and Fath al-Qadir.
The mufta bihi position is validity of suretyship for delivery of loans and all trust items where delivery is obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالأمانة : كالوديعة ، والمستعار، والمستأجر ، ومال المضاربة، والشركة. [٣] قوله : وبالأمانة، ما كان أمانة فإن كان غير واجب التسليم كالوديعة ومال المضاربة والشركة، فإنَّ الواجب عدم المنع عند الطلب لا التسليم، ولا يجوز الكفالة بتسليمه لعدم وجوبه ، كما لا يجوز بيعها، وإن كان واجب التسليم كالمستأجر إذا ضَمِنَ رجل بتسليمه إلى المستأجر ، كمَن استأجر دابَّة وعجَّل الأجر ، ولم يقبضها وكفل له بذلك كفيل صحت الكفالة وعلى الكفيل تسليمها ما دامت حية، فإن هلكت ليس على الكفيل شيء؛ لأنَّ الإجازة انفسخت، وخرج الأصلُ عن كونه مطالباً بتسليمها، وإنما عليه ردُّ الأجر، والكفيل ما كفله، كذا في «العناية». قالوا الكفالة بماليَّة الوديعة والعارية لا تصح، أمَّا بتمكين المالك من أخذ الوديعة يصح، وكذا بتسليم العارية. [١) قوله : وكذا بتسليم العارية ; قال في «المنح» : وما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي له من أنَّ الكفالة بتسليم العارية باطلة بأجل، فقد ذكر في «الجامع الصغير»: إنَّ الكفالة بتسليم العارية صحيحة، وكذا في «المبسوط»، ونص القُدُوري أَنَّها بتسليم المبيع جائزة، ونص في «التحفة» على جميع ما ذكرناه من أنَّ الكفالة بالتسليم صحيحة . والوجه عندي أن لا فرق بين الثلاث الأول من الوديعة ومال المضاربة والشركة، وبين العارية وما معها من الأمانات، إذ لا شك في وجوب الرد عند الطلب، فإن قال شمس الأئمة : الواجب التخلية بينه وبينها، لا ردّها إليه. فنقول : فليكن مثل هذا الواجب على الكفيل، وهو أن يحصلها ويخلّي بينه وبينها بعد إحضاره إليها، ونحن نعني بوجوب الرد بما هو الأعم من هذا، ومن حمل المردود إليه، قال في «الذخيرة»: الكفالة بتمكين المودع من الأخذ صحيحة، كذا في «فتح القدير» انتهى.
And regarding trust items: such as deposits, loans, rented items, and capital of mudarabah and shirkah. [3] His statement: 'And regarding trust items' — what is a trust: if its delivery is not obligatory, like a deposit, mudarabah capital, and shirkah share, then the duty is not to prevent when demanded, not to deliver. Suretyship for its delivery is not valid due to lack of obligation, just as selling it is not valid. But if delivery is obligatory, like a rented item — if a person guarantees its delivery to the lessee, e.g., someone rents an animal and pays the hire in advance but does not take possession, and a surety guarantees that, the suretyship is valid, and the surety must deliver it as long as it is alive. If it dies, nothing is owed by the surety because the lease contract is dissolved, and the principal is no longer obliged to deliver; he only has to return the hire, and the surety only guaranteed the delivery, as in al-'Inayah. They said: suretyship for the monetary value of a deposit or loan is not valid. As for enabling the owner to take the deposit, it is valid; similarly, suretyship for delivery of a loan. [1] His statement: 'Similarly, suretyship for delivery of a loan' — In al-Minah: What Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned that suretyship for delivery of a loan is absolutely invalid, but it is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir that suretyship for delivery of a loan is valid, and similarly in al-Mabsut. Quduri stated that suretyship for delivery of a sold item is permissible. And in al-Tuhfah it is stated for all that we have mentioned that suretyship for delivery is valid. The correct view in my opinion is that there is no difference between the first three (deposit, mudarabah, company capital) and the loan and other trust items, since there is no doubt about the obligation to return when demanded. If Shams al-A'imma says: the obligation is to vacate between him and it, not to return it to him, then we say: let such an obligation be upon the surety — that he brings it and vacates between him and it after bringing it. We mean by the obligation to return what is more general than that, including carrying the returned item. In al-Dhakhirah: suretyship by enabling the depositor to take is valid, as in Fath al-Qadir. End.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding suretyship (kafalah) for trust items (amanat). The matn states that trust items include deposits (wadi'ah), loans (ariyah), rented items (mu'ajjar), and mudarabah and shirkah capital. The commentary distinguishes between trust items whose delivery is not obligatory (e.g., deposit) — for these, suretyship to deliver is invalid because the principal is only obliged not to prevent, not to actively deliver. For trust items whose delivery is obligatory (e.g., a rented item after paying the hire), suretyship to deliver is valid. The surety must deliver the specific item if it still exists; if it perishes, the lease dissolves and the surety has no liability. There is a dispute: Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi held that suretyship for delivering a loan (ariyah) is absolutely invalid, but other Hanafi authorities (al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Mabsut, al-Quduri, al-Tuhfah) consider it valid. The commentator (likely the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah) reconciles by noting that the obligation to return is wajib when demanded, and the surety can fulfill by enabling the owner to take possession (takhliyah). The relied-upon position is that suretyship for delivery of loans and other trust items is valid.

Key Terms
الأمانة (al-amanah): Property held in trust, such as deposit (wadi'ah), loan (ariyah), rented item (mu'ajjar), mudarabah and shirkah capital.
التخلية (at-takhliyah): Vacating or enabling the owner to take possession without actively handing over.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Suretyship for trust items is valid only when the principal is obliged to deliver; for loans, the majority holds it valid despite Sarakhsi's objection.

Suretyship for Carrying on a Specific vs. Non-Specific Rented Animal
Ruling on suretyship for carrying on a specific vs. non-specific rented animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between suretyship for a specific and non-specific rented animal, and apply the Hanafi rule that the surety must have the ability to deliver.

Core Idea
Suretyship for a specific animal is invalid because the surety cannot deliver it; for a non-specific animal, it is valid.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi suretyship (kafalah) law: whether the obligation is tied to a specific item or a generic one. The ruling hinges on the surety's ability to perform – a foundational condition in kafalah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is suretyship (kafalah) for carrying goods on a rented animal. Determine whether the animal is specified (mu'ayyanah) or not (ghayr mu'ayyanah).
The ruling changes based on this distinction.
2
Understand the rationale
For a specific animal, the surety cannot deliver that exact animal – only the principal can. For a non-specific animal, the surety can provide any animal that meets the obligation.
Ability (qudra) is a condition for valid suretyship.
3
Apply the consequence if the animal dies
If the specific animal dies, the lease dissolves, the principal must return the hire, and the surety has no liability because he only guaranteed delivery of that specific animal.
This follows from the invalidity of the original kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالحمل على دابة مستأجرة معينة بخلاف غير المعينة. قالوا: الكفالة بماليَّة الوديعة والعارية لا تصح، أمَّا بتمكين المالك من أخذ الوديعة يصح، وكذا بتسليم العارية.
[٢] قوله : وبالحمل على دابة مستأجرة معينة، إذ لا قُدْرَةَ له على تسليم دابة المكفول عنه، بخلاف غير المعيَّنة : فإن المستحق هاهنا الحمل على أي دابة كانت، فالقدرة ثابتة هاهنا. فإن هلكت ليس على الكفيل شيء؛ لأنَّ الإجازة انفسخت، وخرج الأصلُ عن كونه مطالباً بتسليمها، وإنما عليه ردُّ الأجر، والكفيل ما كفل به، كذا في «العناية».
And regarding carrying on a specific rented animal, unlike a non-specific one. They said: suretyship for the monetary value of a deposit or loan is not valid. But suretyship for enabling the owner to take the deposit is valid, and similarly suretyship for delivery of a loan. [2] His statement: 'And regarding carrying on a specific rented animal' — because he has no ability to deliver the animal of the person for whom he is surety, unlike a non-specific one: for here the entitlement is to carry on any animal, so ability is present. If it dies, nothing is owed by the surety because the lease contract is dissolved, and the principal is no longer obliged to deliver it; he only has to return the hire, and the surety only guaranteed that delivery, as in al-'Inayah.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on suretyship for carrying goods on a rented animal. If the rental contract specifies a particular animal (mu'ayyanah), suretyship to carry on that specific animal is invalid because the surety cannot deliver that exact animal—only the principal can. If the rental is non-specific (ghayr mu'ayyanah), suretyship is valid because the surety can fulfill the obligation by providing any animal. The example clarifies: if a person rents a specific horse and a surety guarantees transportation on that horse, the suretyship is void. If the horse dies, the lease dissolves, and the principal is only liable to return the advance payment; the surety has no liability. This is based on the principle that suretyship requires the surety's ability to perform the obligation.

Key Terms
المعينة (al-mu'ayyanah): A specific animal designated in the rental contract; suretyship for its delivery is invalid.
غير المعينة (ghayr al-mu'ayyanah): A non-specific animal where any individual suffices; suretyship is valid because the surety can provide any.

The validity of suretyship for a rented animal depends on whether the animal is specific (invalid) or non-specific (valid), based on the surety's ability to deliver.

Kafalah After Debtor's Death: Dispute of Abu Yusuf
Discussing kafalah validity after debtor dies insolvent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the majority Hanafi position on the validity of kafalah after the debtor dies insolvent and distinguish the view of Abu Yusuf, including the underlying principle of necessity (al-darurah tuqaddaru bi-qadariha).

Core Idea
The debtor's death does not extinguish the guarantee; necessity is limited to the debtor alone.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding kafalah (guarantee) when the original debtor (asil) dies insolvent. The central question is whether the debt drops from the guarantor (kafil) when it drops from the debtor. The text presents the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Abu Yusuf's dissenting opinion, and clarifies the underlying reasoning using the principle that necessity is limited to its extent. Understanding this dispute strengthens your grasp of how Hanafi jurists balance textual evidence with legal maxims.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: The kafalah remains valid after the debtor's death; the debt does not drop from the guarantor because the drop from the debtor is a necessity limited to him.
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's alternative view
Abu Yusuf holds that if the guarantor is informed of the debtor's death and accepts, a new kafalah can be made, implying that the original kafalah may be void if not accepted after death.
This view is restricted to kafalah for both person and property, except for a testator's debt during illness.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil) used by both sides
The hadith where the Prophet refrained from praying over a debtor until someone guaranteed the debt is cited. The majority interpret it as indicating a prior undertaking, while Abu Yusuf sees it as an acknowledgment or promise, not a binding kafalah.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), allowing room for interpretation.
4
Understand the Hanafi response and necessity principle
The majority argue that the debt drops from the debtor only by necessity (due to death), and this necessity does not extend to the guarantor. The principle 'al-darurah tuqaddaru bi-qadariha' is applied.
Similarly, a pledge (rahn) remains valid after the debtor's death.
5
Consider the secondary dispute on Abu Yusuf's ruling
Later scholars differed whether Abu Yusuf's kafalah is suspended until creditor acceptance or effective immediately. The correct view is that it is effective immediately, as kafalah is a unilateral undertaking.
This analogy to a proxy in marriage is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها
وعند أبي يوسف إذا بلغه الخبر، وأجاز جاز، وهذا الخلاف في الكفالة بالنفس والمال معاً، إلا إذا كَفِلَ عن مورثه في مرضه مع غيبة غرمائه) وأما الكفالة فأمر بين الكفيل والأصيل؛ لأنه التزم ما على الأصيل، والحديث يحتمل أن يكون إقراراً بكفالة سابقة، فإنَّ لفظ الإقرار والإنشاء في الكفالة سواء، ولا عموم الحكاية الفعل، ويحتمل أن يكون وعداً لا كفالة، وكان امتناعه من الصَّلاة عليه ليظهرَ طريق قضاء ما عليه، فلما ظهر بالوعدِ صلَّى عليه.
لا يقال: لو سقط الدين لبرئ الكفيل؛ لأنَّ براءته توجب براءة الكفيل، فلمّا لم يبرأ عُلِمَ أنّ عليه ديناً، فيجوز ابتداء الكفالة به؛ لأنا نقول: الكفيل خلف عنه فلا يبرأ، أو نقولُ في حق الطالب: لا يسقط؛ لأنَّ سقوطه ضروري فلا يتعدى المطلوب، كذا قالوا هذا المقام. وقيدت المسألة بالكفالة بعد موته؛ لأنه لو كَفِلَ في حياتِهِ ثُمَّ مَاتَ مُفلِساً لم تبطل الكفالة، وكذا لو كان به رهن ثم مات مفلساً لا يبطلُ الرَّهن؛ لأنَّ سقوط الدِّينِ عنه في أحكام الدنيا ضرورة، فيتقدَّرُ بقدرها، فأبقيناه في حق الكفيل والرهن؛ لعدم الضرورة، ذكره في «البحر» (۱) نقلا عن «المعراج». [۱] قوله: وعند أبي يوسف الخ؛ واختلف المشايخ على قوله: فقيل: عنده تصح بوصف التوقف، حتى إن رضي به الطالب بعد القيام من المجلس نفذ، وإن لم يرض به بطل. وقيل: بوصف النفاذ ورضاء الطالب ليس بشرط عنده، وهو الأصح؛ لأنَّ الكفالة التزام من الكفيل لا إلزام فيه على غيره، فيتم بالملتزم وحده كالإقرار؛ فلهذا تصح مع الجهالة. ومن قال بوصف التوقفِ جعل الكفيل كالفُضُولي في النكاح، إذا زوج امرأةٌ وليس عنها ولي، حيث يتوقَّفُ النّكاحُ عنده على إجازتها فيما وراء المجلس، فكأنه جعل قوله: كفلت لفلان عن فلان بكذا عقداً تاماً، لكنه تصرف لغيره فيتوقف على رضائه. (۱) «البحر الرائق (٦: ٢٥٣).
The book of Kafalah: its definition, conditions, and rulings. And according to Abu Yusuf: if the news reaches him and he accepts, it becomes valid. This disagreement concerns kafalah for both person and property, except if he guaranteed for his testator during his illness in the absence of his creditors. As for kafalah, it is a matter between the guarantor and the principal because he undertook what is upon the principal. The hadith could be an acknowledgment of a prior guarantee, for the wording of acknowledgment and initiation in kafalah are the same, and there is no generality in the narration of an action. It could also be a promise, not a guarantee. His abstaining from praying over him was to show the way of settling what was upon him; when it became apparent through the promise, he prayed over him. It is not said: if the debt is dropped, the guarantor would be released, because his release necessitates the release of the guarantor; since he was not released, it is known that there is a debt upon him, so initiating kafalah for it is permissible. We say: the guarantor is a successor to him, so he is not released. Or we say regarding the claimant: it does not drop because its dropping is a necessity and thus does not extend to the one demanded. Thus they said in this context. The issue is restricted to kafalah after his death, because if he guaranteed during his life and then died insolvent, the kafalah does not become void. Similarly, if there was a pledge with him and then he died insolvent, the pledge does not become void, because the dropping of the debt from him in worldly rulings is a necessity, so it is limited to its extent; we preserve it in regard to the guarantor and the pledge due to absence of necessity. It is mentioned in al-Bahr, quoting from al-Mi'raj. [1] His saying: 'And according to Abu Yusuf...' – the shuyukh differed regarding his statement: some said that according to him it is valid with the description of suspension, such that if the claimant is pleased with it after leaving the session, it takes effect, and if he is not pleased, it becomes void. And some said: with the description of effectiveness, and the claimant's pleasure is not a condition according to him, which is the most correct, because kafalah is an undertaking from the guarantor that does not impose anything on others, so it is completed with the one undertaking alone, like acknowledgement; therefore it is valid even with ignorance. And whoever said it is with the description of suspension made the guarantor like a proxy in marriage when a woman gives herself in marriage and she has no guardian, where the marriage is suspended according to him upon her approval after the session. So it is as if he made his saying 'I guarantee for so-and-so against so-and-so for such amount' a complete contract, but it is a disposition for others, so it is suspended upon their consent.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of kafalah (suretyship) when the original debtor dies insolvent. The issue arises from the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) refrained from praying over a deceased debtor until someone guaranteed the debt. The text presents Abu Yusuf's view that kafalah after death is valid if the guarantor knows and accepts, but the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the debt drops only by necessity and does not affect the guarantor. The commentary explains that the death of the debtor insolvent does not void a prior kafalah or pledge because the dropping of the debt is a necessity limited to the debtor, not extending to the guarantor. Further, a dispute among later scholars about whether Abu Yusuf's kafalah is suspended pending creditor acceptance or effective immediately is resolved in favor of immediate effectiveness, as kafalah is a unilateral undertaking. The analogy to a proxy in marriage is rejected because the guarantor is not acting for the claimant.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of guarantee where one person (kafil) undertakes to fulfill an obligation owed by another (asil) to a third party (talib).
أصيل (asil): The original debtor whose obligation is guaranteed by the kafil.
إقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgement of a pre-existing right or obligation; here, the hadith may be interpreted as an iqrar of a prior kafalah rather than a new one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what happens to a kafalah if the original debtor dies insolvent?
A) The kafalah becomes void because the debt is dropped from the debtor.
B) The kafalah remains valid because the debt does not drop from the guarantor.
C) The kafalah becomes suspended until the guarantor agrees to pay.
D) The kafalah is valid only if the debtor died in debt but not insolvent.

Key Takeaway
The debtor's death insolvent does not void a kafalah; the necessity of the debt drop is limited to the debtor and does not extend to the guarantor.

Sick Person Asking Heir to Guarantee Debt in Creditors' Absence
Ruling on sick person's request to heir to guarantee debt in creditors' absence, with proof via istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a sick person's request for an heir to guarantee a debt in the creditors' absence, and distinguish between the ruling based on qiyas and the overriding istihsan.

Core Idea
A sick person's instruction to his heir to pay his debts is treated as a bequest, not a guarantee, and is valid despite ignorance of the debt.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a terminally ill person asks his heir to guarantee his debts while the creditors are absent. The ruling is validated through istihsan (juridical preference), which overrides the strict qiyas that would require the creditor's presence. We will explore the reasoning of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and why their view became the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Scenario and Immediate Ruling
Identify the exact situation: the sick person says to the heir 'Guarantee me for what I owe', and the heir guarantees. The text initially states 'it is valid'.
The ruling is given as a direct statement; this is the outcome to be explained.
2
Understand the Qiyas Objection
According to qiyas (analogy), a kafalah contract requires the creditor's presence and acceptance. Since the creditors are absent, the contract would normally be invalid. This is the baseline ruling if no special consideration applied.
Qiyas here aligns with the default rules of kafalah.
3
Analyze the Istihsan Counter-Argument
The basis of istihsan is that this is not a true kafalah but a bequest (wasiyyah) from the sick person to the heir to pay the debt. As a wasiyyah, ignorance of the exact amount or creditor does not invalidate it.
The effective cause (illa) shifts the legal classification from kafalah to wasiyyah.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Variance
Identify the different positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (according to one interpretation) held the qiyas view, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) adopted the istihsan view. The text indicates a disagreement.
The passage records that the fatwa is on the statement of 'the second' (al-thani), interpreted as the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Justification
Later Hanafi authorities such as al-Tarsusi, al-Mahbubi, and al-Nasafi preferred the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. This became the relied-upon position for fatwa.
This is the operative ruling in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صورته: أن يقول المريض لوارثِهِ في غيبة الغرماء: تكفل عنِّي بما عليَّ من الدين، فكفل جازَتْ، وإنما تصح. ولهما: أنَّ الكفالة عقد تمليك المطالبة، فلا يتوقف شطره على ما وراء المجلس كسائر العقود، وإنما تبرع على الطالب بالتزام المطالبة، وإنشاء سبب التبرع لا يتم ما لم يقبله المتبرع عليه كالهبة والصدقة، ولا يمكن جعل عبارة الكفيل قائمة مقام عبارة الطالب؛ لعدم ولايته عليه. ذكره العلامةُ السُّمُنِّي. وفي «البَزَّازيّة» و«الدرر»: إنَّ الفتوى على قول الثاني، وقال في «المنح»: قال الطرسوسي في «أنفع الوسائل» بعد أن ذكر الخلاف بين أبي حنيفة ومحمد وبين أبي يوسف والفتوى على قولهما، وفي «صحيح الشيخ قاسم»، والمختار قولهما عند المحبوبي والنسفي وغيرهما. انتهى.
[١]قوله: لوارثه؛ إنّما قال: لوارثه؛ لأنَّ المريض لو قال هذا القول لأجنبي، اختلفت المشايخ فيه؛ فمنهم من قال بجوازه؛ تنزيلاً للمريض منزلة الطالب، ومنهم من قال بعدمه؛ لأنَّ الأجنبي غير مطالب لقضاء دينه بلا التزام، وكان المريض والصحيحُ سواء والأوّل أوجه، كذا في «الفتح»، والتفصيل في «البحر الرائق).
[۲] قوله: وإنما تصح... الخ؛ وكان القياس على قولهما أن لا تصح في هذه المسألة أيضاً؛ لأن الطالب غير حاضر؛ ولأن الصحيح لو قال هذا لوارثه فضمنه لم تصح، فكذلك المريض.
ووجه الاستحسان: إن هذا إنما صح بطريق الوصية من المريض لوارثه بأن يقضي دينه لا بطريق الكفالة عنه؛ ولهذا صح وإن لم يسم المريض الدين، ولا ربَّ الدين؛ لأن الجهالة لا تمنع صحة الوصية.
Its form: that a sick person says to his heir in the absence of creditors: 'Guarantee me for what I owe of debt', and he guarantees, it is valid. And for them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): kafalah is a contract that transfers the right to claim, so its validity does not depend on being in the same session like other contracts. It is a donation to the claimant by undertaking the claim, and the initiation of a cause of donation is not complete unless the donee accepts it, like gift and charity. And the words of the guarantor cannot stand in place of the words of the claimant because he has no authority over him. Mentioned by al-Sumnuni. In al-Bazzaziyya and al-Durar: the fatwa is according to the second statement. And in al-Munah: al-Tarsusi said in Anfa' al-Wasa'il after mentioning the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad and between Abu Yusuf, that the fatwa is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And in Sahih al-Shaykh Qasim: the chosen view is that of the two according to al-Mahbubi and al-Nasafi and others. End. [1] His saying: 'to his heir' – he only said 'to his heir' because if the sick person said this to a stranger, the shuyukh differed; some said it is permissible, making the sick person stand in the place of the claimant; some said it is not, because the stranger is not required to pay his debt without undertaking, and the sick person and the healthy are the same, and the first is more appropriate, as in al-Fath, and the detail is in al-Bahr al-Raiq. [2] His saying: 'and it is valid' etc. – According to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) analogy, it should not be valid in this issue either because the claimant is not present, and because if a healthy person said this to his heir and he guaranteed, it would not be valid, so similarly for the sick person. The basis of istihsan is that this is valid by way of a bequest (wasiyyah) from the sick person to his heir to pay his debt, not by way of kafalah; therefore it is valid even if the sick person did not specify the debt or the creditor, because ignorance does not prevent the validity of a bequest.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from istihsan. It addresses a specific scenario: a terminally ill person tells his heir to guarantee his debts while the creditors are absent. The ruling is that such a guarantee is valid. The commentary explains that according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), kafalah is a contract of transferring the right to claim, so it requires acceptance of the creditor; but here, the validity is based on istihsan, treating it as a bequest (wasiyyah) from the sick person to the heir to pay the debt, not as a kafalah. Since bequests are valid even if the debt amount or creditor is unknown, ignorance does not invalidate. The text also notes that if the sick person addressed a stranger instead of the heir, there is a difference of opinion: some allow it treating the sick person as a claimant, others disallow because a stranger is not obliged to pay without undertaking. The relied-upon position (fatwa) is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest or testamentary instruction given by a person during terminal illness directing payment of debts or distribution of property after death.
استحسان (istihsan): A juridical preference that sets aside a strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling based on a stronger evidence or need.
غيبة الغرماء (ghaybat al-ghuramā'): The absence of the creditors from the location where the kafalah is being made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the heir is obliged to pay his father's debts.
B) Because it is treated as a bequest (wasiyyah), which does not require specification of debt.
C) Because the sick person's illness makes his word binding.
D) Because the creditors have implicitly agreed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a sick person's instruction to his heir to guarantee debts in the absence of creditors by treating it as a bequest (wasiyyah) via istihsan, overriding the qiyas that would require creditor acceptance.

Kafalah — Surety

Sick Person's Kafalah for Heir: Bequest or Suretyship?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on kafalah as bequest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sick person's kafalah for his heir and explain why the Sahibayn's view treats it as a bequest, applying istihsan.

Core Idea
A sick person's kafalah for his heir is valid as a bequest, not as a contract of suretyship.

When a sick person asks his heir to guarantee his debt in the creditors' absence, a normal kafalah would require the creditor's acceptance. However, the Hanafi school diverges: Imam Abu Hanifa insists on acceptance, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule it valid by istihsan, treating it as a bequest. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal issue
Understand the facts: a sick person tells his heir to guarantee his debt while creditors are absent. The core issue is whether the heir's acceptance is required for the kafalah to be valid.
The absence of creditors is key – they cannot accept in the session.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa says the kafalah is invalid without the creditor's acceptance because kafalah is a contract of transferring the right to demand, which requires acceptance in the same session. He rejects any analogy to a bequest.
He treats the action purely as a kafalah contract.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say it is valid even without acceptance because the sick person's statement is effectively a bequest (wasiyyah) – a directive to the heir to pay his debt. Bequests do not require acceptance from the beneficiary.
They apply istihsan over qiyas.
4
Understand the istihsan reasoning
The qiyas would invalidate it, but istihsan allows validity due to the special need to clear the sick person's debt, benefiting the creditors. Ignorance of the debt amount or creditor identity does not harm a bequest.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi method here.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical difference: Abu Hanifa requires creditors to be present; Sahibayn do not. The fatwa is on the Sahibayn's view, as recorded in later texts like Al-Bazzaziyya and Al-Durar.
Tarjih by later authorities solidifies the operative position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Its form: That the sick person says to his heir in the absence of the creditors: 'Guarantee for me what is upon me of debt', and he guarantees, it is valid, and it is only valid. And for them two: That suretyship is a contract of transferring the right to demand, so its validity does not depend on what is beyond the session like other contracts, and it is a donation to the claimant by undertaking the demand, and the initiation of the cause of donation is not completed unless the donee accepts it like gift and charity, and it is not possible to make the statement of the guarantor stand in place of the statement of the claimant due to lack of guardianship over him. Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a sick person's request to his heir to guarantee his debt in the absence of creditors. The author first states the ruling: it is valid. Then he mentions the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) that kafalah is a contract of transferring the right to demand, which requires acceptance in the session, but they make an exception here due to istihsan, treating it as a bequest. The fatwa is on their view. The commentary then explains that the specification to 'heir' is important because if a stranger were involved, there is ikhtilaf. Also, the istihsan is justified because the sick person's statement is effectively a bequest, which does not require naming the debt or creditor.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of transferring the right to demand payment from a debtor to a guarantor.
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest or testamentary disposition made by a person during illness, effective after death from one-third of the estate.
غرماء (ghurama'): Creditors; plural of gharim, one who is owed a debt.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning that deviates from strict analogy for a stronger legal reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is considered a bequest from the sick person to his heir.
B) Because the creditor's acceptance is implied by the sick person's statement.
C) Because suretyship does not require acceptance in any case.
D) Because the heir can be forced to pay the debt.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a sick person's kafalah for his heir is valid as a bequest, not requiring creditor acceptance, per the Sahibayn's view upheld by later authorities.

Kafalah for Kitabah Money: Guaranteeing the Maktab's Debt
Ruling on kafalah for kitabah money with condition and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah for kitabah money, identify the condition for its validity, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding badal al-sa'i.

Core Idea
Kafalah for kitabah money is valid if the maktab has property; but guaranteeing badal al-sa'i is disputed within the school.

This passage addresses whether a person can guarantee the payment owed by a slave who has contracted for his freedom (maktab). The ruling hinges on the nature of the debt and the status of the maktab. We will examine the general permissibility, the condition that the maktab must have property, and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over guaranteeing the substitute service (badal al-sa'i). Finally, we note the exception for blood money (diya).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafalah (suretyship) for the kitabah money owed by a maktab to his master. The ruling is that it is valid (sahih), but with a condition.
The text states: 'وبمال الكتابة حر تكفل به أو عبد' (And with the kitabah money, a free person guarantees it or a slave).
2
Locate the condition for validity
The text explicitly states that kafalah for kitabah money is valid only if the maktab has property (مال). This is a key condition without which the guarantee would not be effective.
The phrase: 'وإنما تصح إذا كان له مال' (it is only valid if he has property).
3
Trace the intra-madhhab dispute on badal al-sa'i
The passage reports a difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Abu Hanifa holds that kafalah for badal al-sa'i is not valid because the debt falls away upon incapacity. The Sahibayn hold it is valid because the maktab is treated as a free man in debt.
The text: 'وحكم بدل السعاية حكمُ حال الكتابة في قول أبي حنيفة فلا تجوز الكفالة به للمولى عنده، وتجوز عندهما؛ لأنه حرّ مديون.'
4
Note the exception for diya (blood money)
The text also mentions that kafalah for blood money is generally not valid, unless explicitly undertaken. This serves as a contrast and shows the scope of the rule.
The text: 'وكذا لا تصح الكفالة بالدِّية.' with an exception from Al-Kafi.

And with the kitabah money, a free person guarantees it or a slave; because it is a debt that is established despite the contravention, and he only said: 'a free person guarantees it or a slave' to remove the misunderstanding that the suretyship of the slave for it should be valid because it is not permissible to establish such a debt upon him because the slave is the subject of kitabah, so he specified it to prevent this misunderstanding. End. And Allah knows best.

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on kafalah for kitabah money (the payment owed by a maktab under a contract of manumission). The author rules that a free person or a slave can guarantee this debt. The specification of 'free or slave' is to prevent the misconception that a slave cannot guarantee such a debt because he cannot have a debt upon himself; however, it is valid if the slave has property. The commentary ties back to the previous discussion of the sick person's statement to his heir, reiterating that it is treated as a bequest. It then addresses the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the kafalah for badal al-sa'i (substitute service): Abu Hanifa holds it invalid, while the Sahibayn hold it valid because the maktab is a free man in debt. Finally, it notes that kafalah for blood money is generally invalid, with an exception if expressly undertaken.

Key Terms
مال الكتابة (mal al-kitabah): The payment agreed upon in a kitabah contract, which the maktab must pay to his master to earn freedom.
مكاتب (maktab): A slave who has contracted with his master for manumission in exchange for a specified payment.
بدل السعاية (badal al-sa'i): Compensation paid by a maktab to his master for work performed or earnings if the kitabah contract fails.
ديّة (diya): Blood money or financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim in cases of homicide or injury.

Kafalah for the Kitabah Debt: Conditions and Proof
Explains the ruling and proof for guaranteeing the kitabah debt of a mukatab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah for the kitabah debt, identify the condition of the mukatab's wealth, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding badal al-sa'ayah.

Core Idea
Kafalah for kitabah debt is valid only if the mukatab has wealth, and is treated like a bequest valid only during illness.

The kitabah debt is unique: it is established despite the mukatab's limited capacity. This lesson explores how the Hanafi school applies kafalah (guarantee) to this exceptional debt. The passage draws an analogy to a bequest and a sick person's instruction, revealing both the conditions and a key internal dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is a kafalah (guarantee) by a free person or a slave for the kitabah debt (badal al-kitabah) of a mukatab. The ruling is sahih (valid) under specific conditions.
The guarantor can be free or slave, as long as the conditions are met.
2
Locate the condition of wealth
The text states the guarantee is only valid if the mukatab has wealth (مال). This condition prevents frivolous guarantees that would be void upon the mukatab's incapacity.
If the mukatab has no wealth, the debt is unstable and kafalah is invalid.
3
Understand the rationale: analogy to a bequest
The guarantee is said to be 'in reality a bequest.' This means it is only valid when made during the illness (marad al-mawt) of the person instructing the guarantee (the debtor or master). The analogy to a sick person instructing his heir clarifies that no acceptance from the creditor is needed.
The command is not a negotiation but immediate realization, like a bequest.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on badal al-sa'ayah
Abu Hanifa holds that kafalah for the mukatab's earnings (badal al-sa'ayah) is invalid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold it valid because the mukatab is considered a free debtor.
This dispute arises from differing views on the nature of the mukatab's capacity.
5
Evaluate the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a master guarantees his mukatab's earnings, the guarantee is valid according to the Sahibayn but not according to Abu Hanifa. The mufta bihi position is not specified, so both views are transmitted.
In practice, a mufti would need to weigh the authority of each view.

The Book of Kafalah: Its definition, conditions, and rulings. And with the property of the kitabah, a free person guarantees it, or a slave. Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding kafalah (guarantee) for the kitabah debt of a mukatab (a slave under a contract of manumission). The text begins with the matn stating that a free person or a slave can guarantee the kitabah debt. The commentary explains that this is treated as a bequest, so the creditor need not be specified. The reason is that this debt is exceptional: it is established despite the slave's lack of capacity. The restriction to a free person or a slave is to remove the misconception that a slave's guarantee is invalid; actually, a slave can guarantee if he has wealth. The commentary likens it to a sick person's command to his heir during illness, where the sick person stands in for the creditor because the sick person needs to clear his estate. The command is not a negotiation but an immediate realization, so no new acceptance is needed. The text then discusses that the kitabah debt is a debt that persists despite being against the master's right, making it suitable for kafalah. It notes that the restriction to kitabah property does not mean other debts on a mukatab can be guaranteed; in fact, no other debts on a mukatab are valid for kafalah because they are void upon his incapacity. An intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding badal al-sa'ayah (earnings during kitabah): Abu Hanifa holds kafalah for it invalid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold it valid because the mukatab is considered a free debtor. Finally, the text states that kafalah for blood money (diyah) is generally invalid, with a citation from al-Khaniyah, but al-Kafi presents a scenario where a person guarantees the diyah of a victim, and if the killing occurs, the guarantor is liable. This indicates a difference of opinion or a specific application.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A guarantee or surety where a person (kafil) becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another (asil) to a third party (talib).
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master, agreeing to pay a specified sum in exchange for freedom.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The payment agreed upon in the kitabah contract that the mukatab must pay to his master to earn freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Valid only if the guarantor is a free person
D) Valid only if the creditor is specified by name

Key Takeaway
Kafalah for the kitabah debt is valid only if the mukatab has wealth and the guarantee is made during illness, reflecting its nature as akin to a bequest.

Module 10 · Lesson 61

Kafalah — Surety
