6 min
Principal's Payment to Guarantor: No Recourse Without Creditor Payment
Ruling with proof: principal cannot reclaim paid to guarantor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a principal debtor (asil) pays the guarantor (kafil) before the creditor (talib) is paid, and distinguish between payment as discharge (qada) and payment as delivery (risalah).

Core Idea
Payment as discharge transfers ownership to the guarantor; payment as delivery remains a trust with possible reclaim.

In Hanafi kafalah, the principal debtor may sometimes pay the guarantor before the guarantor settles with the creditor. Does the principal have a right to reclaim that money? The answer depends on the nature of the payment—whether it was intended as a discharge of the principal's own liability or merely as a delivery for onward transmission. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the underlying reasoning, and the intra-Hanafi difference of opinion on the delivery scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the principal debtor's payment to the guarantor. The ruling is that the principal cannot reclaim the money if the payment was made as a discharge (qada). Determine the hukm level: the payment itself is valid (sahih), but the right to reclaim is negated.
The matn explicitly states the principal does not reclaim, even if the guarantor hasn't paid the creditor.
2
Understand the proof (dalil)
The proof is based on the structure of kafalah: it creates two debts—creditor's immediate debt against guarantor, and guarantor's deferred debt against principal. When the principal pays as discharge, the guarantor owns the money by virtue of collection (al-iqtida). Thus ownership transfers, preventing reclaim.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) from the nature of the contract.
3
Distinguish between qada and risalah
In qada (discharge), the principal says: 'I pay you to clear my debt.' The money becomes the guarantor's property. In risalah (delivery), the principal says: 'Take this and give it to the creditor.' The money remains a trust (amanah) with the guarantor.
The distinction impacts whether the principal can reclaim. In risalah, ownership does not transfer.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
For the risalah scenario, some Hanafi sources (al-Minh, al-Ramz, al-Tabyin) hold that the principal cannot reclaim because the creditor's right has attached to the money. Others (al-Hidayah, Ghayat al-Bayan) allow reclaim because the money is still a trust. The text leans towards allowing reclaim.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the operative view (mufta bihi) is the permissive one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يرجع أصيل بألف أدى إلى كفيله، وإن لم يعطها طالبه (ولا يرجع أصيل بألفر أدى إلى كفيله، وإن لم يعطها طالبه ) : أي إذا" عجل الأصيل، فأدى المال إلى الكفيل الذي كَفِلَ بأمرِهِ ليس له أن يستردها مع أن الكفيل لم يعطها للطالب
[ ] قوله : أي إذا ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه إذا دفع الأصيل، وهو المديون إلى الكفيل المال المكفول به ليس للأصيل أن يسترده من الكفيل، وإن لم يعطه الكفيل إلى الطالب ؛ لأن الكفيل ملكه بالاقتضاء.
فظهر أن الكفالة توجب ديناً للطالب على الكفيل وديناً للكفيل على الأصيل، لكن دين الطالب حال ودين الكفيل مؤجّل إلى وقت الأداء ؛ ولذا لو أخذ الكفيل من الأصيل رهناً أو أبرأه أو وهب منه الدين صح فلا يرجع بأدائه. [٢] قوله : فأدى المال ؛ أي على وجه القضاء بأن قال له : إني لا آمن أن يأخذ منك الطالب حقه فأنا أقضيك المال قبل أن تؤديه، بخلاف ما إذا دفع المال على وجه الرسالة، قال الأصيل للكفيل : خذ هذا المال وادفعه إلى الطالب. حيث لا يصير المؤدّى ملكاً للكفيل بل هو أمانة في يده، ولكن لا يكون للأصيل أن يسترده من يد الكفيل ؛ لأنه تعلَّق بالمؤدَّى حقٌّ الطالب، وهو بالاسترداد يريد إبطاله فلا يُمكنُ منه ما لم يقبض دينه كالمسألة الأولى، صرّح به في «المنح»، و«الرمز ) ، [٣] و«التبيين»، وغيرها. لكن صرح الشارح لله بخلافه حيث قال : وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أداه على وجه الرسالة ؛ لأنه حينئذ تمحض أمانةً في يده. انتهى. فظهر أنه يمكن الاسترداد للأصيل، [٤] وهكذا في «الهداية ، وقال في غاية «البيان»: له ولاية الاسترداد. انتهى. [٥]وقال في «الكفاية» بعد ذكر المسألة : بخلاف ما إذا كان الدفع على وجه الرسالة
And the principal does not reclaim a thousand (dirhams) that he paid to his guarantor, even if the guarantor has not given it to the claimant. (And the principal does not reclaim a thousand he paid to his guarantor, even if the guarantor has not given it to the claimant): i.e., when the principal makes an advance payment and gives the money to the guarantor who guaranteed by his order, he has no right to take it back, even if the guarantor has not given it to the claimant. [ ] His saying: i.e. when ... etc.; its summary: when the principal, who is the debtor, pays the guaranteed money to the guarantor, the principal cannot reclaim it from the guarantor, even if the guarantor has not given it to the claimant, because the guarantor owns it by virtue of collection. It becomes clear that kafalah creates a debt for the claimant against the guarantor and a debt for the guarantor against the principal, but the claimant's debt is due immediately while the guarantor's debt is deferred until the time of payment. Therefore, if the guarantor takes a pledge from the principal, or releases him, or gifts the debt to him, it is valid, and the principal does not reclaim his payment. [2] His saying: 'he paid the money' means by way of discharge, as he said to him: 'I fear that the claimant may take his right from you, so I pay you the money before you pay it.' This is contrary to if he pays the money by way of delivery, where the principal says to the guarantor: 'Take this money and give it to the claimant.' In that case, the money does not become the property of the guarantor but is a trust in his hand. However, the principal cannot reclaim it from the guarantor's hand because the claimant's right has attached to the money; by reclaiming, he intends to nullify that right, so he is not permitted until the claimant receives his debt, as in the first case. This is explicitly stated in al-Minh, al-Ramz, al-Tabyin, and others. But the commentator (al-Sharih) stated something contrary, saying: 'This is contrary to if he pays it by way of delivery; because then it is purely a trust in his hand.' End. So it appears that the principal can reclaim. This is also in al-Hidayah, and in Ghayat al-Bayan it says: 'He has the authority to reclaim.' End. [5] And in al-Kifayah after mentioning the issue: 'Contrary to if the payment is by way of delivery.'
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the situation where the principal debtor (asil) pays the guarantor (kafil) before the guarantor pays the creditor (talib). The matn states that the principal cannot reclaim the money even if the guarantor has not given it to the creditor. The commentary explains that this is because the guarantor owns the money by way of collection (bi al-iqtida). The structure of kafalah creates two debts: the creditor's immediate debt on the guarantor, and the guarantor's debt on the principal, which is deferred until the guarantor actually pays. Therefore, when the principal pays, the guarantor becomes the owner of that money. The text then distinguishes between two scenarios of payment: payment as a discharge (qada) and payment as a delivery (risalah). In the discharge scenario, the principal pays voluntarily to clear his liability, and the guarantor owns it. In the delivery scenario, the principal gives the money as a trust (amanah) to the guarantor to pass on to the creditor. The commentary initially states that even in the delivery case, the principal cannot reclaim because the creditor's right has attached. However, it then notes a difference: some commentators, including the author of al-Hidayah and Ghayat al-Bayan, hold that in the delivery case, the principal can reclaim because the money remains a trust. The text cites various sources and presents this as the relied-upon view. The overall lesson is that the default ruling is no recourse for the principal, but with the nuance that if the payment was explicitly as a delivery, the principal may have a right to reclaim according to some authorities.

Key Terms
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor whose debt is guaranteed by the kafil.
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor who assumes liability for the debt of the asil to the talib.
طالب (talib): The creditor or claimant to whom the debt is owed.
قضاء (qada): Discharge; payment made by the asil to the kafil with the intention of settling the debt.
رسالة (risalah): Delivery; payment made by the asil to the kafil as a trust to be passed on to the talib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the principal debtor (asil) pays the guarantor (kafil) the amount of the debt as a discharge (qada) before the guarantor pays the creditor, what is the ruling?
A) The principal can reclaim the money
B) The principal cannot reclaim the money because the guarantor owns it
C) The principal can reclaim only if the guarantor fails to pay within a month
D) The principal must pay again if the guarantor does not pay

Key Takeaway
The principal debtor cannot reclaim money paid to the guarantor as a discharge; but if paid as a delivery, the principal can reclaim according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Surety's Receipt of Payment and Profit: Discharge vs Agency
Commentary on rulings regarding surety's receipt of payment and profit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between payment as discharge (qāḍā') and payment as agency (risāla) and state the Hanafi ruling on the principal's right to reclaim and the surety's right to profit.

Core Idea
Payment to the surety as discharge transfers ownership; as agency, it remains a trust — only in trust can the principal reclaim.

This passage examines two important consequences when a principal debtor pays money to his surety before the surety pays the creditor. The first concerns whether the principal can reclaim that money. The second concerns any profit the surety makes by trading with it. The rulings turn on a crucial distinction: did the principal pay as a discharge of his debt to the surety, or merely as an agent to forward the money? Let's work through the legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish payment as discharge vs agency
The principal's intention when paying the surety matters. In discharge (qāḍā'), he intends to settle his debt to the surety. In agency (risāla), he entrusts the money for delivery to the creditor.
These are the two scenarios: payment as qāḍā' and payment as risāla.
2
Identify the ruling on reclamation in each case
In discharge, the surety acquires ownership (by iqtiḍā'), so the principal cannot reclaim. In agency, the surety is a trustee; there is a dispute among Hanafi authorities whether the principal can reclaim.
The majority view (al-Kifāyah, Ya'qūb Pasha) allows reclamation in the agency scenario.
3
Identify the ruling on profit
If the surety trades with the money, the profit belongs to the surety if he owned the money (discharge). If it was a trust (agency), profit is unlawful and must be given in charity.
The passage states: 'Whatever profit the surety makes, it is his, and he should not give it in charity.'
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on the agency scenario
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyād, Abu al-Layth, Najm al-A'imma al-Makki, and the author of al-Kifāyah allow reclamation. The commentator of al-Hidāyah and some others disallow it. The text invites reflection (fatafakkar).
Both positions are legitimate; the relied-upon view appears to be the permissive one.

Source Text

ولا يرجع أصيل بألف أدى إلى كفيله، وإن لم يعطها طالبه : أي إذا عجل الأصيل، فأدى المال إلى الكفيل الذي كفل بأمره ليس له أن يستردها مع أن الكفيل لم يعطها للطالب. قوله: أي إذا ... الخ؛ حاصله: أنه إذا دفع الأصيل، وهو المديون إلى الكفيل المال المكفول به ليس للأصيل أن يسترده من الكفيل، وإن لم يعطه الكفيل إلى الطالب؛ لأن الكفيل ملكه بالاقتضاء. فظهر أن الكفالة توجب ديناً للطالب على الكفيل وديناً للكفيل على الأصيل، لكن دين الطالب حال ودين الكفيل مؤجل إلى وقت الأداء؛ ولذا لو أخذ الكفيل من الأصيل رهناً أو أبرأه أو وهب منه الدين صح فلا يرجع بأدائه. قوله: فأدى المال؛ أي على وجه القضاء بأن قال له: إني لا آمن أن يأخذ منك الطالب حقه فأنا أقضيك المال قبل أن تؤديه، بخلاف ما إذا دفع المال على وجه الرسالة، قال الأصيل للكفيل: خذ هذا المال وادفعه إلى الطالب. حيث لا يصير المؤدى ملكاً للكفيل بل هو أمانة في يده، ولكن لا يكون للأصيل أن يسترده من يد الكفيل؛ لأنه تعلق بالمؤدى حق الطالب، وهو بالاسترداد يريد إبطاله فلا يمكن منه ما لم يقبض دينه كالمسألة الأولى، صرح به في «المنح»، و«الرمز»، و«التبيين»، وغيرها. لكن صرح الشارح بخلافه حيث قال: وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أداه على وجه الرسالة؛ لأنه حينئذ تمحض أمانةً في يده. انتهى. فظهر أنه يمكن الاسترداد للأصيل، وهكذا في «الهداية»، وقال في غاية «البيان»: له ولاية الاسترداد. انتهى. وقال في «الكفاية» بعد ذكر المسألة: بخلاف ما إذا كان الدفع على وجه الرسالة. ثم قال: كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها. وما ريح فيها الكفيل، فهو له، ولا يتصدق به : أي إذا عامل الكفيل في الألف التي أدى الأصيل إليه وريح فيها، فالربح له حلالاً طيباً، لا يجب تصدقه لما أنه ملكه بأن قال المطلوب للكفيل: خذ هذا المال وادفعه إلى الطالب حيث لا يصير المؤدى ملكاً للكفيل، بل هو أمانة في يده، لكن لا يكون للمطلوب أن يسترده من الكفيل؛ لأنه تعلق بالمؤدى حق الطالب، والمطلوب بالاسترداد يريد إبطال ذلك فلا يقدر عليه، كذا في «الكافي». لكن ذكر في كتاب «الكفالة» من «الكبرى»: قال الحسن بن زياد وقال أبو الليث: هذا إذا دفعه إلى الكفيل على وجه القضاء، أما إذا دفعه على وجه الرسالة فله الاسترداد، وقال نجم الأئمة المكي: واليه وقعت الإشارة في «باب الكفالة بالمال» من «الأصل» فإنه على مال الكفيل يكون أمينا. انتهى. وقال يعقوب باشا في «حاشية» هذا الكتاب: والظاهر ما نقله صاحب «الكفاية»؛ لأنه أمانة محضة ويد الرسول كيد المرسل، وكأنه لم يقتضه فلا يعتبر حق الطالب. انتهى. فتفكر. قوله: كما إذا عجل الزكاة؛ ودفعها إلى الساعي، وكما إذا اشترى شيئاً بشرط الخيار ونقد الثمن قبل مضي مدة الخيار، وهو أن يصير زكاة وثمناً عند مضي الحول ومضي مدة الخيار. قوله: إنه ملكه؛ أي بالقبض فكان الربح بدل ملكه، وظاهره أنه لا يجب عليه التصدق به، ولا فرق بين أن يكون قضى الدين هو أو قضاء الأصيل، كما في «البحر».
And the principal does not revert with a thousand that he paid to his surety, even if he (the surety) did not give it to the claimant. Meaning: When the principal hurries and pays the money to the surety who guaranteed by his order, he cannot reclaim it even though the surety did not give it to the claimant. [Commentary]: The gist is that when the principal, who is the debtor, pays the guaranteed money to the surety, the principal cannot reclaim it from the surety even if the surety did not give it to the claimant, because the surety owned it by acquisition (iqtiḍā). Thus it appears that suretyship creates a debt for the claimant upon the surety and a debt for the surety upon the principal, but the claimant's debt is due immediately and the surety's debt is deferred to the time of payment. Hence if the surety takes a pledge from the principal or releases him or gifts the debt to him, it is valid and he does not revert by paying. [Commentary on 'paid the money']: i.e., by way of discharge, saying: 'I fear the claimant will take his right from you, so I pay you the money before you pay it.' Contrary to if he paid the money as a gift/agency, where the principal tells the surety: 'Take this money and pay it to the claimant.' In that case the paid amount does not become the property of the surety but is a trust in his hand, yet the principal cannot reclaim it from the surety's hand because the claimant's right is attached to the paid amount, and by reclaiming he intends to nullify it, so he is not empowered as long as he has not received his debt, like the first case. This is stated explicitly in al-Munyah, al-Ramz, al-Tabyin, and others. But the commentator explicitly states contrary, saying: 'This is different if he paid it by way of agency, because then it is purely a trust in his hand.' End. Thus it appears that the principal can reclaim, and similarly in al-Hidayah, and Ghāyat al-Bayān says: 'He has the authority to reclaim.' End. And in al-Kifāyah after mentioning the case: 'Contrary to if the payment was by way of agency.' Then: 'Book of Kafālah, its definition, conditions, and rulings. And whatever profit the surety makes therein is his, and he should not give it in charity.' Meaning: When the surety trades with the thousand that the principal paid him and makes profit, the profit is his, lawful and good; he is not obligated to give it in charity because it is his property. That the debtor tells the surety: 'Take this money and pay it to the claimant' where the paid amount does not become the property of the surety but is a trust in his hand, but the debtor cannot reclaim it from the surety because the claimant's right is attached to the paid amount, and the debtor by reclaiming intends to nullify that, so he is not able to do so, as in al-Kāfī. However, it is mentioned in the book 'al-Kafālah' from 'al-Kubrā': al-Hasan ibn Ziyād said and Abu al-Layth said: 'This is if he paid it to the surety by way of discharge; but if he paid it by way of agency, he has the right of reclamation.' And Najm al-A'imma al-Makki said: 'To this reference was made in the chapter on suretyship for wealth from al-Aṣl, as he is a trustee over the surety's money.' End. And Ya'qūb Pasha said in his gloss on this book: 'The apparent view is what the author of al-Kifāyah transmitted, because it is a pure trust and the hand of the agent is like the hand of the principal, and it is as if he did not acquire it, so the claimant's right is not considered.' End. So reflect. [Footnote]: 'As when he hastens to pay zakat and gives it to the collector, and as when he buys something with an option and pays the price before the option period expires, such that it becomes zakat and price at the end of the year and the option period.' [Another footnote]: 'His saying: It is his property, i.e., by taking possession, so the profit is in place of his property, and its apparent meaning is that he is not obligated to give it in charity, and there is no difference between whether he paid the debt or the principal paid it, as in al-Bahr.'
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two related rulings in the book of suretyship (kafālah). The first ruling states that if the principal debtor (aṣīl) pays the guaranteed amount to the surety (kafīl) before the surety pays the creditor (ṭālib), the principal cannot reclaim that money from the surety, even if the surety has not yet handed it over to the creditor. The reasoning is that the suretyship contract creates two debts: one from the surety to the creditor (due immediately) and one from the principal to the surety (deferred until the surety pays). When the principal pays the surety, the surety acquires ownership of that money through 'iqtiḍā' (taking possession as a claim). Therefore, the principal's payment is valid and cannot be revoked. The commentary further distinguishes between two scenarios: payment as a discharge (qāḍā') and payment as an agency (risāla). In the first, the principal explicitly intends to settle his debt to the surety, so the surety owns the money. In the second, the principal merely entrusts the money to the surety to pass it on to the creditor, so the surety is a trustee. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the principal can reclaim in the agency scenario: some authorities (like the commentator of al-Hidāyah) say no, while others (like al-Kifāyah, al-Ḥasan ibn Ziyād, and Ya'qūb Pasha) say yes, because it remains a trust and the creditor's right has not yet attached. The second ruling states that if the surety trades with the money and makes a profit, that profit is his lawful property, and he is not required to give it in charity. This is because he owned the money upon receipt (in the discharge scenario), and the profit follows ownership. The passage also draws analogies with hastening zakat payment and buying with an option, where early payment does not change the legal consequences.

Key Terms
أصيل (aṣīl): The principal debtor who is guaranteed by a surety.
كفيل (kafīl): The surety who guarantees the debt of the principal.
طالب (ṭālib): The creditor who is owed the debt.
قضاء (qāḍā'): Payment made by the principal to the surety with the intention of discharging the debt owed to the surety.
رسالة (risāla): Payment made by the principal to the surety as an agent to deliver to the creditor.
ربح (ribḥ): Profit earned by the surety from trading with the money received from the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the principal pays the surety by way of discharge (qāḍā'), can the principal later reclaim the money from the surety before the surety pays the creditor?
A) Yes, because the creditor has not received payment.
B) No, because the surety owns the money by acquisition.
C) Yes, if the surety has not used it.
D) No, because the money is a trust.

Key Takeaway
The principal's payment to the surety as discharge transfers ownership and profit to the surety irrevocably; as agency, it remains the principal's property, and he can reclaim it before it reaches the creditor.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Profit of the Surety: Ownership vs. Trust
Ruling on surety's profit from received payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qada (settlement) and risala (agency) in surety transactions and state the ruling on profit ownership.

Core Idea
If the principal pays the surety as settlement, the surety owns the profit; if as agent, he does not.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school regarding profit earned by a surety (kafil) from money given to him by the principal debtor (asil) to pay a creditor (talib). The key is how the principal gives the money: as a settlement (qada) or as an agency (risala). Understanding this distinction is vital for mu'amalat transactions where a surety may temporarily hold funds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text begins by stating the general rule: 'Whatever profit the surety earns in it, it is his, and he does not give charity from it.' Identify the legal nature: the profit is halal and owned by the surety.
2
Understand the legal rationale
The commentary explains that when the principal pays the surety as qada, the surety becomes the owner of that money because the suretyship creates a debt chain: creditor vs. surety, and surety vs. principal. By paying early, the surety acquires ownership.
3
Distinguish between qada and risala
Contrast the case of risala (agency), where the surety is merely a trustee (amin). In that case, the principal can reclaim the money, and the surety does not own the profit. This distinction is supported by authority from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, Abu al-Layth, and Najm al-A'imma.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some later scholars (e.g., Ya'qub Pasha) preferred the view that the surety remains a trustee even when receiving as qada, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the main ruling that profit is owned.
The commentary invites reflection: 'fa tafakkar'.
5
Apply the rule to examples
The text analogizes to early payment of zakat and purchase with option of stipulation: in both, early payment with ownership transfer yields ownership of the benefit.

Source Text

كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها
وما ريح فيها الكفيل، فهو له، ولا يتصدَّقُ بـ
دين
كما إذا عجل" أداء الزَّكَاةِ للسَّاعي ؛ لأنَّ الكفالة بأمر المكفول عنه انعقدت سبباً للدينين : الطالب على الكفيل، ودين الكفيل على المكفولِ عنه مؤجلاً إلى وقتِ أَدائِهِ ، فَإذا وُجِدَ السَّببُ وعَجَلَ صح الأداء، وملكه الكفيل، فلا يسترده المكفولُ عنه، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أداه على وجه الرِّسالة ؛ لأنه حينئذ تمحض أمانة
في يده.
(وما ريح فيها الكفيل ، فهو له ، ولا يتصدَّقُ به) : أي إذا عامل الكفيل في الألف التي أدى الأصيل إليه ورَيحَ فيها ، فالرّبِّحُ له حلالا طيباً ، لا يَحِبُ تَصَدُّقه لِمَا أَنَّه ملكه
بأن قال المطلوب للكفيل : خذ هذا المال وادفعه إلى الطالب حيث لا يصير المؤدَّى ملكاً للكفيل، بل هو أمانة في يده، لكن لا يكون للمطلوب أن يسترده من الكفيل ؛ لأنه تعلق بالمؤدَّى حق الطالب، والمطلوب بالاسترداد يريد إبطال ذلك فلا يُقَدَّر عليه، كذا
في «الكافي».
لكن ذكر في كتاب «الكفالة» من «الكبرى»: قال الحسن بن زياد وقال أبو الليث الله : هذا إذا دفعه إلى الكفيل على وجه القضاء، أما إذا دفعه على وجه الرسالة فله الاسترداد، وقال نجم الأئمة المكي: واليه وقعت الإشارة في «باب الكفالة بالمال» من «الأصل» فإنّه [على] مال الكفيل يكون أمينا. انتهى.
وقال يعقوب باشا في «حاشية» (۱) هذا الكتاب : والظاهر ما نقله صاحب «الكفاية» ؛ لأنه أمانة محضة ويد الرسول كيد المرسل، وكأنه لم يقتضه فلا يعتبر حق الطالب. انتهى. فتفكر. [ ١ ] قوله : كما إذا عجل الزكاة ؛ ودفعها إلى الساعي، وكما إذا اشترى شيئاً بشرط الخيار ونقد الثمن قبل مضي مُدَّة الخيار، وهو أن يصير زكاة وثمناً عند مضي الحول
ومضي مدة الخيار. (٢) قوله : إنه ملكه ؛ أي بالقبض فكان الربح بدل ملكه، وظاهره أنه لا يجب عليه التصدق به، ولا فرق بين أن يكون قضى الدين هو أو قضاء الأصيل، كما في
«البحر»).
Book of Kafala (Suretyship): Its definition, conditions, and rulings. And whatever profit the surety earns in it, it is his, and he does not give charity from it. ... As when he hastens the payment of zakat to the collector; because the suretyship at the command of the principal becomes a cause for two debts: the creditor's claim against the surety, and the surety's claim against the principal deferred until the time of his payment. So when the cause is found and he hastens, the payment is valid, and the surety owns it, so the principal cannot reclaim it. This is unlike when he pays it as an agent, for then it is a pure trust in his hand. (And whatever profit the surety earns in it, it is his, and he does not give charity with it): That is, if the surety trades with the thousand which the principal paid to him and earns profit therein, the profit is his, lawful and good; he is not obliged to give it in charity because he owns it. ... But it is mentioned in the book of Kafala from al-Kubra: al-Hasan ibn Ziyad said, and Abu al-Layth said: This is if he paid it to the surety as a settlement (qada). But if he paid it as an agency (risala), then he can reclaim it. And Najm al-A'imma al-Makki said: And to this the reference was made in the chapter of Kafala bil-Mal from al-Asl, that he is a trustee over the surety's wealth. End. And Ya'qub Pasha said in his Hashiya on this book: The apparent is what the author of al-Kifaya transmitted, because it is a pure trust and the hand of the messenger is like the hand of the sender, and as if he did not take possession, so the right of the creditor is not considered. End. So reflect. [1] His statement: as when he hastens the zakat and pays it to the collector, and as when he buys something with the option of stipulation and pays the price before the expiry of the option period, so that it becomes zakat and price at the end of the year and the expiry of the option period. (2) His statement: He owns it, i.e., by possession, so the profit is in exchange for his ownership. And its apparent meaning is that he is not obliged to give charity with it, and there is no difference whether he paid the debt himself or the principal paid, as in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling (hukm) from the commentary on al-Wiqaya regarding the profit earned by a surety (kafil) from property he received from the principal (asil) to pay a debt. The core ruling: if the surety trades with that money and earns profit, the profit is his, and he is not required to give it in charity. The reasoning: when the principal gives the money to the surety as a settlement (qada), the surety becomes the owner of that money because the suretyship creates a debt from the surety to the creditor and a debt from the principal to the surety. By paying early, the surety acquires ownership. This is contrasted with giving as an agency (risala), where the surety is merely a trustee and does not own the money. The text cites several authorities: al-Kafi, al-Kubra (with al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and Abu al-Layth), and al-Asl (via Najm al-A'imma). There is also a note from Ya'qub Pasha preferring the agency view, but the dominant position is that the profit is halal and charity not required. The commentary then adds analogies with early payment of zakat and purchase with option to illustrate ownership by possession. The lesson teaches that a surety who receives money as payment (not as agent) can legitimately keep any profit from trading it.

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety or guarantor who undertakes to pay a debt on behalf of the principal.
الأصيل (al-asil): The principal debtor on whose behalf the surety acts.
الطالب (al-talib): The creditor who is owed the debt.
قضاء (qada'): Payment of debt as a settlement, whereby the surety takes ownership of the money.
رسالة (risala): Agency or message; when money is given to the surety only to deliver, not to own.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about profit earned by a surety from money received to pay a debt?
A) The profit must always be given in charity.
B) The profit belongs to the creditor because the money was meant for him.
C) If the money was given as settlement (qada), the surety owns the profit.
D) The profit belongs to the principal because he provided the money.

Key Takeaway
A surety who receives money as settlement (qada) becomes owner of that money, so any profit from trading it is his and charity is not required; this differs from agency (risala) where he is a trustee.

Profit from Specific Fungible in Kafalah: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on returning profit from specific fungible in kafalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding profit from a specific fungible in suretyship, identify the views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and apply the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

Core Idea
Profit from a specific fungible in suretyship is permissible but returning it to the debtor is the most correct (al-asah).

In Hanafi fiqh, when a surety receives a specific commodity (like wheat) from the principal and sells it at a profit, what should he do with that profit? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah reveals an intra-Hanafi dispute with three narrations from Abu Hanifa and differing views from the Sahibayn. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to distinguish between recommended and obligatory actions in suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The surety receives a specific fungible (e.g., a kur of wheat) from the principal as settlement (qada). He sells it and earns a profit. What is the ruling on that profit?
The passage distinguishes between possession as settlement (qada) vs. agency (risala).
2
Map the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
State the three narrations from Abu Hanifa: (1) return profit to principal (most correct), (2) keep it as lawful, (3) give it in charity. Then note the Sahibayn's view: profit is lawful if possession was as qada, but not if as risala (with a nuance from Abu Yusuf).
The dispute's origin is analogized to profit from usurped dirhams.
3
Identify the mufta bihi ruling
The text declares 'wa huwa al-asah' for the narration of returning it to the principal. This is the relied-upon position.
The term 'ahabb' indicates it is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib.
4
Distinguish the surety from the usurper
Contrast the surety's case with that of a usurper (ghasib) who must return profit. The surety has a right to the property, so the ruling differs.
Leasing usurped property is a separate sub-case where the surety owns the rent by contract.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a surety following the asah returns the profit; one following the Sahibayn keeps it if possession was as qada. In fatwa, returning is preferred.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; both positions have textual basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : وربح كر كفل به وقبضه له ، ورَدّه إلى قاضيه أحب (۳). قوله : وربح كر مبتدأ. وله : خبره أي إن كانت الكفالة لـه بكر حنطة ، فأداه الأصيل إلى الكفيل، فباعه الكفيل، وريح فيه ، فالربح له، لكن ردّه إلى قاضيه، وهو الأصيل أحب وهذا مقيَّد بما إذا قبضه على وجهِ القضاء كما تقدَّم، وأما إذا قبضه على وجهِ الرسالة فلا يطيب له الربح على قولهما ؛ لأنه لا ملك له، وعند أبي يوسف له يطيب له، وأصل الخلاف في الربح بالدراهم المغصوبة. (1) 11 ] قوله : وربح كرّ ؛ أراد به ما يتعيَّن بالتعيين : كالحنطة والشعير، احتراز عما لا يتعيَّن فإن ربحه يندب ردُّه على المطلوب، وهل يطيب للأصيل إذا ردَّه الكفيل عليه ، قال في «البحر» معزياً إلى «البناية) إن كان الأصيل فقيراً طاب له وإن كان غنياً، ففيه روايتان والأشبه كما قال فخرُ الإسلام في شرح الجامع الصغير»: إنه يطيب له ؛ لأنه إنما رده عليه؛ لأنه حقه. انتهى. [۲] قوله : كفل به ؛ قيَّد بالكفالة ؛ لأن الغاصب إذا ربح وجب ردُّه على المالكِ ويجبر على الدفع له ؛ لأنه لا حق للغاصب في الربح ، كذا في «البحر» (۳). وقال في «الفتح» ) : إن الغاصب إذا أخَّرَ المغصوب ثم ردَّه، فإن الأجر له (٤) يتصدق به أو يردّه إلى المغصوب منه .منه. انتهى. ولا مخالفة ، بين هذا وبين ما تقدم ؛ لأن ذلك في صورة ما اتجر في المغصوب المتعين وربح فيه، وهذا فيما أجر العين المغصوبة فإنّه يملك الأجر بالعقد. كما صرح به في الكتب المعتمدة . [۳] قوله : وردّه إلى قاضيه أحب ؛ وهذا هو أحد الروايات عن الإمام، وهو الأصح، وعنه أنه لا يردّه ، بل يطيب له، وهو قولهما ؛ لأنه نماء ملكه، وعنه : إنه يتصدق به كذا في «النهر» مُفصلاً.
His statement: And the profit of a kur for which he became surety and took possession, and returning it to his debtor (qadi) is preferable. His statement: 'Warih kur' is mubtada, 'lahu' is khabar. That is, if the suretyship was for a kur of wheat, and the principal paid it to the surety, then the surety sold it and profited, then the profit is his, but returning it to his debtor, i.e., the principal, is preferable. And this is restricted to when he took possession as settlement (qada) as previously. But if he took possession as agency (risala), then the profit is not lawful for him according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because he has no ownership. According to Abu Yusuf, it is lawful for him. And the origin of the dispute is in the profit from usurped dirhams. (1) 11 His statement: Warih kur; he meant what is specified by specification, like wheat and barley, as a precaution against what is not specified, for its profit is recommended to be returned to the debtor. And whether it is lawful for the principal if the surety returns it to him, it is said in al-Bahr quoting al-Binaya: If the principal is poor, it is lawful for him; if he is rich, there are two narrations, and the more apparent is as Fakhr al-Islam said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: it is lawful for him because he only returned it to him because it was his right. End. [2] His statement: kafala bihi; he restricted it to suretyship because if a usurper profits, he must return it to the owner and is compelled to pay it, because the usurper has no right to the profit, as in al-Bahr. And it is said in al-Fath: If the usurper leases the usurped property and then returns it, the rent is for him; he may give charity or return it to the owner. End. And there is no contradiction between this and what preceded, because that is in the case of trading in the specific usurped property and profiting in it, and this is in the case of leasing the usurped property, for he owns the rent by contract, as stated in authoritative books. [3] His statement: returning it to his debtor is preferable; and this is one of the narrations from the Imam (Abu Hanifa), and it is the most correct (asah). And from him (another narration) that he does not return it, rather it is lawful for him, and that is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because it is the increase of his property. And from him (another narration) that he gives charity with it, as stated in al-Nihr in detail.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) concerning the profit earned by a surety on a specific fungible commodity (like a kur of wheat) that he received from the principal. The base ruling: if the surety takes possession as settlement (qada), the profit is his, but it is preferable (ahabb) to return it to the principal (asil). This is restricted to qada; if he took as agent (risala), according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the profit is not lawful for him because he never owned it. However, Abu Yusuf has a separate view that it is lawful. The text then distinguishes between specific fungibles (like wheat) and non-specific: for non-specific, returning the profit is recommended (yundabu). It further discusses whether the principal may lawfully keep the profit if the surety returns it: if the principal is poor, yes; if rich, there are two narrations, but the preferred view (from Fakhr al-Islam) is that it is lawful because it is his right. The passage also contrasts the surety's case with that of a usurper (ghasib): a usurper must return profit to the owner, but a surety is different because he had a right to the property. The last part clarifies the three narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa: (1) return to principal (the most correct), (2) keep it as lawful, (3) give in charity. The relied-upon position (al-asah) is to return it.

Key Terms
كر (kurr): A measure of grain, here representing a specific fungible commodity like wheat or barley.
قاضيه (qadihi): His debtor, referring to the principal (asil) who owes the debt originally.
أحب (ahabb): More beloved/preferable, indicating a recommended action but not obligatory.
الربح بالدراهم المغصوبة (al-ribh bid-darahim al-maghsubah): The dispute over profit from usurped money; an analogous case that underlies the ikhtilaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct (asah) narration from Imam Abu Hanifa in the passage, what should the surety do with the profit from a kur of wheat?
A) Keep it as lawful.
B) Return it to the principal.
C) Give it in charity.
D) Divide it equally with the principal.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a surety earning profit from a specific fungible should return it to the principal as a recommended act, not an obligation.

Ruling on Profit of Guarantor (Kafil) from Selling a Specified Commodity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on profit of guarantor from selling specified commodity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the profit of a guarantor from selling a specified commodity and explain the reason for the taint (khabath) according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Profit on a specified commodity held by a guarantor is tainted due to the debtor's contingent right; returning it is recommended, not obligatory.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a common fiqh scenario: a guarantor (kafil) receives a specified commodity (like a kurr of wheat) from the debtor (asil) and sells it at a profit. The central question is whether the profit is lawful for the guarantor or tainted in some way. The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: Abu Hanifa holds the profit is tainted and recommends its return or charity, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it fully lawful. The commentary delves into the reasons for this difference, focusing on the distinction between specified and unspecified goods and the nature of the debtor's contingent right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal question
Read the passage to understand the case: a guarantor (kafil) receives a specified commodity (e.g., a kurr of wheat) from the debtor (asil) and then sells it for a profit. The question is who owns the profit and what the kafil should do with it.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note the three positions: Abu Hanifa's preferred view (return or charity as recommendation), the Sahibayn view (profit is lawful), and another narration from Abu Hanifa (charity). Identify which view is considered 'al-asahh' (most correct).
The text states: 'وهذا هو أحد الروايات عن الإمام، وهو الأصح'
3
Understand the key distinction: specified vs. unspecified commodities
The commentary stresses that the taint (khabath) only applies to commodities that become determined by specification (ما يتعين بالتعيين), like wheat or barley. For unspecified items (e.g., dirhams), the profit is lawful.
This distinction is crucial for applying the ruling.
4
Analyze the reasons for the taint (khabath)
Two reasons are given: (1) the asil retains a contingent right to reclaim the commodity if he pays the debt himself, and (2) the asil's consent was conditional on the kafil paying. Understand why these create a 'doubt' (shubha) that taints the profit.
The taint is not strong enough to make the profit haram, but it mandates a recommendation to purify.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical implication
The relied-upon view is that the kafil is recommended (mustahabb) to return the profit to the asil or give it in charity. It is not obligatory (not wajib). The kafil may not freely use the profit without discharge.
This is derived from the text: 'استحباب لا جبر'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وريح كُر كفل به وقبضه له ، ورَدّه إلى قاضيه أحب (۳).
قوله : وربح كُر مبتدأ. وله : خبره أي إن كانت الكفالة لـه بكر حنطة ، فأداه الأصيل إلى الكفيل، فباعه الكفيل، وريح فيه ، فالربح له، لكن ردّه إلى قاضيه، وهو الأصيل أحب وهذا مقيَّد بما إذا قبضه على وجهِ القضاء كما تقدَّم، وأما إذا قبضه على وجهِ الرسالة فلا يطيب له الربح على قولهما ؛ لأنه لا ملك له، وعند أبي يوسف له يطيب له، وأصل الخلاف في الربح بالدراهم المغصوبة.
(1) 11 ] قوله : وربح كُرّ ؛ أراد به ما يتعيَّن بالتعيين : كالحنطة والشعير، احتراز عما لا يتعيَّن فإن ربحه يندب ردُّه على المطلوب، وهل يطيب للأصيل إذا ردَّه الكفيل عليه ، قال في «البحر» معزياً إلى «البناية) إن كان الأصيل فقيراً طاب له وإن كان غنياً، ففيه روايتان والأشبه كما قال فخرُ الإسلام في شرح الجامع الصغير»: إنه يطيب له ؛ لأنه إنما رده عليه؛ لأنه حقه. انتهى.

لكنه تمكن فيه خبث بسبب أن للأصيل حق استرداده، على تقدير أن يقضي الأصيل الدين بنفسه، فيكون حق الأصيل متعلقاً به، فهذا الخبث يعمل فيما يتعين بالتعيين" : كالكُر بخلاف ما لا يتعيَّنُ بالتَّعيينِ :كالدراهم والدنانير، كما في المسألةِ السَّابقة، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وأما عندهما : لا يكون الرَّدُّ إلى قاضيه أحب إذ لا خبث فيه أصلاً. [٢]قوله : يعمل فيما يتعين بالتعيين ؛ فيكون سبيله التصدق في رواية، وردُّه عليه في رواية ؛ لأن الخبت بحقه، وهذا أصح لكنّه استحباب لا جبر؛ لأن الحق له، كذا في «الهداية»(۱).
ووجه ذلك : أنه لو كان الملك صحيحاً من كلِّ وجه لا يؤمر بالتصدق والرد لا إيجاباً ولا استحباباً، ولو انعدم الملك أصلاً كان الربح خبيثاً ، فإن كان صحيحاً من وجه وفاسداً من وجه أمرناه بالتصدق أو الرد على المالك استحباباً لا إيجاباً توفيراً على الشبهين حظهما ، كذا في «الكفاية»(۳).
[٣] قوله : وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ؛ وهذا إذا أعطاه على وجه القضاء لدينه ، وإن دفع إليه على وجه الرسالة لا يطيب له الربح بالاتفاق ؛ لأنه لا يملكه ويتعلق العقد بعينه لتعيينه، فتكون الحرمة فيه حقيقة كالمغصوب المتعيّن إذا ربح فيه بخلاف ما لا يتعين عند أبي يوسف ه ، كذا في «التبيين»، وغيره.
And the profit of a kurr for which he stood surety and took possession, and returning it to his debtor is preferable. His statement: 'And the profit of a kurr' is a subject. 'For him' is its predicate. That is, if the guarantee was for him for a kurr of wheat, and the principal paid it to the guarantor, then the guarantor sold it and made a profit, then the profit is his, but returning it to his debtor, i.e. the principal, is preferable. And this is restricted to when he took possession on the basis of repayment, as previously mentioned. But if he took possession on the basis of agency, then the profit is not lawful for him according to the two disciples, because he has no ownership. And according to Abu Yusuf, it is lawful for him. And the origin of the disagreement is about profit from usurped dirhams. (1) [11] His statement: 'And the profit of a kurr' — he intended by it what becomes determined by specification, like wheat and barley, as opposed to what does not become determined, for its profit, its return to the sought party is recommended. And does it become lawful for the principal when the guarantor returns it to him? He said in 'Al-Bahr' citing 'Al-Binaya': If the principal is poor, it is lawful for him; if rich, there are two narrations. And the more likely, as Fakhr al-Islam said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: it is lawful for him, because he only returned it to him because it is his right. End. [2] His statement: 'for which he stood surety' — he restricted by suretyship, because if a usurper makes a profit, it is obligatory to return it to the owner, and he is compelled to pay it to him, because the usurper has no right to the profit. Thus in 'Al-Bahr' (3). And he said in 'Al-Fath': If the usurper rents the usurped object then returns it, then the hire is for him; he gives it in charity or returns it to the one from whom it was usurped. ... End. And there is no contradiction between this and what preceded, because that is in the case of trading in the specific usurped object and profiting from it, while this is regarding hiring out the usurped object, for he owns the hire by contract, as clarified in reliable books. [3] His statement: 'and returning it to his debtor is preferable' — and this is one of the narrations from the Imam, and it is the most correct. And from him that he does not return it, rather it is lawful for him, and this is the view of the two disciples, because it is the growth of his property. And from him that he gives it in charity. Thus in 'Al-Nahr' in detail. But a taint of impermissibility (khabath) is present in it because the principal has the right to reclaim it, on the assumption that the principal himself pays off the debt, so the principal's right is attached to it. So this taint operates with what becomes determined by specification, like the kurr, unlike what does not become determined by specification, like dirhams and dinars, as in the previous issue. And this is according to Abu Hanifa. But according to the two disciples, returning it to his debtor is not preferable, since there is no taint at all. [ ] His statement: 'because ... etc.'; and when it is so, the profit is acquired in a ownership that oscillates between being established and not being established, and such is deficient, so a suspicion of impermissibility attaches. The second reason for the taint in the profit is that the principal consented to the paid amount being the property of the guarantor on the condition that the guarantor pays, so if the principal himself pays, he is not consenting to it. [2] His statement: 'it operates with what becomes determined by specification' — so its course is charity in one narration, and its return to him in another narration, because the taint is due to his right. And this is more correct, but it is recommendation, not compulsion, because the right is his. Thus in 'Al-Hidayah' (1). And the reason for that is that if ownership were sound from every aspect, he would not be ordered to give charity or return, neither obligatorily nor recommendatorily. And if ownership were non-existent altogether, the profit would be entirely impermissible. So if it is sound from one aspect and unsound from another, we order him to give charity or return to the owner as a recommendation, not as an obligation, to give each of the two aspects their due. Thus in 'Al-Kifayah' (3). [3] His statement: 'And this is according to Abu Hanifa' — and this is when he gave on the basis of repayment of his debt. But if he gave on the basis of agency, the profit is not lawful for him by consensus, because he does not own it, and the contract attaches to the specific object due to its specification, so the prohibition is real, like a specific usurped object if profit is made from it, unlike what is not determined according to Abu Yusuf. Thus in 'Al-Tabyin' and others.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary on the matn of Al-Wiqaya, expanding on the ruling of profit for a guarantor (kafil) when he receives a specified commodity (like wheat) from the debtor (asil). The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute, as it presents the views of Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on whether the profit must be returned to the debtor or is permissible for the guarantor. The key distinction is between what becomes determined by specification (ما يتعين بالتعيين) like wheat and barley, and what does not, like dirhams and dinars. According to Abu Hanifa, the profit on a specified commodity is tainted (خبث) because the debtor retains a contingent right to reclaim the commodity if he pays the debt himself. Therefore, the guarantor is recommended (استحباب) to either return the profit to the debtor or give it in charity, but this is not obligatory. The taint arises from two reasons: first, the debtor's right to reclaim, and second, the debtor's consent being conditional on the guarantor paying. The two disciples hold that there is no taint, so the profit is lawful and need not be returned. The passage also clarifies that this applies when the guarantor receives the commodity on the basis of repayment (وجه القضاء), not as an agent (رسالة). In the latter case, all agree the profit is unlawful. The commentary cites various sources like Al-Bahr, Al-Binaya, Fath al-Qadir, Al-Hidayah, and Al-Kifayah, showing the depth of Hanafi literature on this point. The ruling is an application of the general principle found in the case of a usurper (غاصب) who makes a profit, where the profit must be returned to the owner. However, here ownership is partially valid, leading to the recommendation rather than obligation.

Key Terms
ما يتعين بالتعيين (ma yata'ayyanu bil-ta'yin): A commodity that becomes specifically identified by designation, such as a particular bundle of wheat or barley, as opposed to fungible items. The ruling on taint applies to such items.
خبث (khabath): A legal taint or impermissibility that attaches to the profit because it is partially unlawful. It does not make the profit entirely haram but renders it recommended to purify.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct (al-asahh) view in the Hanafi school, what should a guarantor do with the profit from selling a specified commodity (e.g., wheat) received from the debtor?
A) Keep the profit because he owned the commodity.
B) Return the profit to the debtor as an obligation.
C) Give the profit in charity as an obligation.
D) Either return the profit to the debtor or give it in charity as a recommendation.

Key Takeaway
The profit of a guarantor on a specified commodity is tainted (khabath) and must be purified by returning it to the debtor or giving it in charity as a recommendation, not an obligation.

Profit Taint for a Surety: Specified vs Unspecified Property
Explaining taint in profit due to principal's right of recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on profit taint for a surety, distinguish between specified and unspecified property, and apply the recommended purification.

Core Idea
The surety's profit is tainted only if the principal later pays the debt, and only for specified property; charity or return is recommended, not obligatory.

Imagine you act as a surety (kafeel) for a friend's loan. You pay the debt from your own money, and then your friend repays you. Meanwhile, you used that money to generate profit. Is the profit yours to keep without concern? In the Hanafi school, there is an internal dispute about whether that profit is tainted (khabath) and what should be done. This lesson explores the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, clarifying the practical ruling for today.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Scenario
Identify the key parties: surety (kafeel) pays the debt on behalf of the principal (aseel). Later, the principal pays the surety back. During the period the surety held the money, he made profit. The question is whether that profit is clean or tainted.
2
Identify the Source of Taint
The text states that taint arises because the principal has the right to reclaim the property if he pays the debt himself. This right attaches to the property, creating uncertainty about ownership.
The taint is not about the profit itself but about the ownership basis.
3
Distinguish Specified vs Unspecified Property
According to Abu Hanifa, taint only operates in property that is specified by designation (mu'ayyan bil-ta'yin), like a specific bag of wheat. Fungible items like dirhams and dinars are not specified, so no taint occurs for them.
This distinction is crucial: the principal's right attaches to specific items, not to fungible ones.
4
Compare Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa holds that taint exists and recommends charity or return. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that there is no taint at all because once the surety pays, the property becomes his absolutely.
Understand the difference in reasoning: Abu Hanifa focuses on the principal's continuing right; the Sahibayn focus on the transfer of ownership.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi Ruling
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the recommendation (istihbab) to give the profit in charity or return it to the principal, but this is not obligatory. This is derived from al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah.
Profit is not haram, but it is not entirely clean either; the recommended action balances the competing considerations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنه تمكن فيه خبث بسبب أن للأصيل حق استرداده، على تقدير أن يقضي الأصيل الدين بنفسه، فيكون حق الأصيل متعلقاً به، فهذا الخبث يعمل فيما يتعين بالتعيين: كالكُر بخلاف ما لا يتعيَّنُ بالتَّعيينِ: كالدراهم والدنانير، كما في المسألةِ السَّابقة، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما: لا يكون الرَّدُّ إلى قاضيه أحب إذ لا خبث فيه أصلاً. [ ] قوله: بسبب أن ... الخ؛ وإذا كان كذلك كان الربح حاصلاً في ملك تردد بين أن يقر وبين أن لا يقرّ، ومثل ذلك قاصرٌ فتَمَكَّن فيه شبهة الخبث. السبب الثاني لتمكن الخبث في الربح أن الأصيل قد رضي بكون المدفوع ملكاً للكفيل على اعتبار قضاء الكفيل، فإذا قضاه الأصيل بنفسه لم يكن راضياً به. [٢]قوله: يعمل فيما يتعين بالتعيين؛ فيكون سبيله التصدق في رواية، وردُّه عليه في رواية؛ لأن الخبت بحقه، وهذا أصح لكنّه استحباب لا جبر؛ لأن الحق له، كذا في «الهداية». ووجه ذلك: أنه لو كان الملك صحيحاً من كلِّ وجه لا يؤمر بالتصدق والرد لا إيجاباً ولا استحباباً، ولو انعدم الملك أصلاً كان الربح خبيثاً، فإن كان صحيحاً من وجه وفاسداً من وجه أمرناه بالتصدق أو الرد على المالك استحباباً لا إيجاباً توفيراً على الشبهين حظهما، كذا في «الكفاية».
But there is taint in it because the principal has the right to reclaim it, on the assumption that the principal pays the debt himself, so the principal's right is attached to it. This taint operates in what is specified by specification: like a kurr of wheat, unlike what is not specified by specification: like dirhams and dinars, as in the previous issue. And this is according to Abu Hanifah. As for according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): there is no returning to the debtor (i.e., the principal) is preferable because there is no taint in it at all. His statement: 'because... etc.' And when that is the case, the profit occurs in a property that wavers between being established and not established, and such is deficient, so a suspicion of taint occurs in it. The second reason for the occurrence of taint in the profit is that the principal consented to the paid amount being the property of the surety on the basis of the surety's payment. But if the principal pays it himself, he did not consent to it. His statement: 'operates in what is specified by specification' so its way is to give in charity in one narration, and to return it to him in another narration; because the taint is due to his right, and this is more correct, but it is recommended not obligatory; because the right belongs to him, so in al-Hidayah. And the reason for that: if the ownership had been sound from every aspect, he would not be commanded to give in charity or return, neither obligatorily nor recommendatorily. And if ownership were completely absent, the profit would be entirely impure. But if it is sound from one aspect and corrupt from another, we command him to give in charity or return to the owner recommendatorily, not obligatorily, to give each of the two suspicions its share. So in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the taint (خبث) in profit earned by a surety (كفيل) on property he paid to discharge a debt. The text explains that if the principal (أصيل) later pays the debt himself, the surety's profit becomes questionable because the principal's right to reclaim the property creates a taint. According to Abu Hanifa, this taint affects only property that is specified by designation (يتعين بالتعيين), such as a specific measure of wheat, because the principal's right is attached to that specific item. For unspecified property like dirhams and dinars, no taint applies. However, the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that there is no taint at all because once the surety pays, the property becomes his absolutely. The text then discusses the consequences: if the profit is from property that is partly sound and partly corrupt, the surety is recommended (not obliged) to give it in charity or return it to the principal, as stated in al-Hidayah and al-Kifayah. This recommendation balances the conflicting presumptions.

Key Terms
خبث (khabath): A taint or impurity in the profit due to a legal right of another, making the profit not entirely pure (طيب).
يتعين بالتعيين (yata'ayyanu bi al-ta'yin): Property that becomes specifically designated by being pointed out or identified, such as a particular bag of wheat, as opposed to fungible items like coins.
أصيل (asīl): The principal debtor on whose behalf the surety (كفيل) stands guarantee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, in which type of property does the taint (خبث) in the surety's profit occur?
A) In dirhams and dinars only
B) In property that is specified by designation (like a specific kurr of wheat)
C) In all property equally
D) In property that is not specified

Key Takeaway
The taint in the surety's profit is only a recommendation to purify; it does not render the profit haram, and it only applies to specified property according to Abu Hanifa.

Definition and Ruling of Bay' al-'Inah (Credit Sale)
Definition of 'inah and its fiqh ruling in a suretyship context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define bay' al-'inah, state its ruling in the Hanafi school, and explain why the orderer is not liable in a suretyship arrangement.

Core Idea
Bay' al-'inah is a credit sale intended to avoid riba, but stipulations make it a prohibited legal stratagem.

Bay' al-'inah is a transaction where a person buys goods on credit and then sells them for cash to obtain liquidity, often as a workaround for interest. The Hanafi school closely scrutinizes such transactions, especially when a stipulation to repurchase exists. This passage defines 'inah, outlines the ruling under different conditions, and applies it to a suretyship scenario where a principal orders a surety to buy via 'inah.

Guided Path
1
Define bay' al-'inah
Understand the linguistic and technical meaning of 'inah: a credit sale where the buyer later resells for cash to avoid riba.
The term 'inah derives from 'ain (cash) because the buyer takes cash in exchange for the credit purchase.
2
Differentiate rulings based on stipulation
Identify the two scenarios: with a stipulation to repurchase (haram) and without (disputed among scholars).
The Shafi'i school allows it without stipulation; early Hanafis prohibited it absolutely.
3
Analyze the suretyship scenario
Examine the case: the principal orders the surety to buy via 'inah, guaranteeing losses. Determine why the orderer is not liable.
The guarantee of loss is invalid, and the proxy purchase fails due to ignorance of the item and price.
4
Apply the Hanafi mufta bihi
The surety bears the profit/loss; the orderer's instruction is not binding.
This is the operative ruling in later Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفيل أمرَهُ أصيله بأن يتعين عليه ثوبا ففعل، فهو له) : أي أمر الأصيل الكفيل بأن يشتري ثوباً بطريق العينة)
[١]قوله : بطريق العينة ؛ قال العلامة الفيومي في «المصباح المنير : العينة : بالكسر السلف، واعتان الرجلُ اشترى الشيء بنسيئة، وبعثه عيناً بعين: أي حاضراً، وعين التاجر تعيناً، والاسم العينة : بالكسر ، وفسرها الفقهاء : بأن يبيع الرجلُ متاعه إلى أجل ثم يشتريه بثمن حال ليسلم به من الربا. وقيل لهذا البيع عينة ؛ لأن مشتري السلعة إلى أجل يأخذ بدلها عيناً : أي نقداً؛ وذلك حرام إذا اشترط المشتري على البائع أن يشتريها منه بثمن معلوم، فإن لم يكن بينهما شرط فأجازها الشافعي الله له لوقوع العقد سالماً عن المفسدات، ومنعها بعض المتقدمين، وكان يقول : هي أخت الربا. فلو باعها المشتري من غير بائعها في المجلس، فهي منه أيضاً، لكنها جائزة بالاتفاق. انتهى. وقال الفقهاء : بيع العينة : هو أن يقول له اشتر من الناس حريراً أو غيره من الأنواع ثم بعه، فما ربح البائع منك وخسرته أنت فعلي. وصورته : أن يأتي هو إلى تاجر فيطلب منه القرض، ويطلب التاجر الربح، ويخاف من الربا، فيبيعه التاجر ثوباً يساوي عشرة مثلاً بخمسة عشر نسيئة ؛ ليبيعه هو بالسوق ليصل إلى العشرة، ويجب عليه للبائع خمسة عشر إلى أجل، أو يقرضه خمسة عشر درهماً ثم يبيعه المقرض ثوباً يساوي عشرة بخمسة عشر. فيأخذ الدراهم التي أقرضه على أنها ثمن الثوب، فيبقى عليه خمسة عشر درهماً، فإذا فعل ذلك نفذ عليه، والربح الذي ربحه التاجر يلزمه، ولا يلزم الأمر شيئاً من ذلك؛ لأنه إما ضامن لما يخسره نظرا إلى قوله : علي، والضمان بالخسران لا يجوز؛ لأن الضمان لا يكون إلا بمضمون وإمّا توكيله بالشراء نظراً إلى الآمر به، فلا يجوز أيضاً لجهالة الحرير وثمنه. وقيل صورته : أن يشتريه بأكثر من قيمته ليبيعه بأقل من ذلك الثمن بغير البائع.
(A surety whose principal ordered him to buy a garment via 'inah, and he did so, it belongs to him): i.e., the principal ordered the surety to buy a garment by way of 'inah. [1] His statement: 'by way of 'inah'. al-'Allama al-Fayyumi said in al-Misbah al-Munir: al-'inah (with kasra) means a loan. 'I'tana al-rajul means he bought something on credit. And his selling it 'ain bi 'ain means for cash. 'Aina al-tajir ta'yinan, and the noun is al-'inah (with kasra). And the jurists interpreted it as: a man sells his merchandise on credit and then buys it back for cash in order to escape riba. It is called 'inah because the buyer of the commodity on credit takes its equivalent in cash (i.e., spot). That is haram if the buyer stipulates to the seller that he will buy it back for a known price. If there is no stipulation, al-Shafi'i allows it because the contract is free of corrupting factors. Some early scholars prohibited it and used to say: it is the sister of riba. If the buyer sells it to someone other than the seller in the same session, it is also from him, but it is permissible by consensus. End quote. The jurists said: Bay' al-'inah is that someone says to another: buy silk or other items from the people, then sell it; whatever profit the seller makes from you and whatever loss you incur is on me. And its form: he goes to a merchant and asks for a loan, the merchant seeks profit, fears riba, so the merchant sells him a garment worth ten, for example, for fifteen on credit, so that he can sell it in the market to get the ten, but owes the seller fifteen on credit, or he lends him fifteen dirhams and then sells him a garment worth ten for fifteen. So he takes the dirhams he lent as the price of the garment, and thus remains owing fifteen dirhams. If he does that, it is effective, and the profit earned by the merchant is binding on him, and the orderer (the principal) is not liable for anything; because either he is a guarantor for the loss regarding his statement 'on me', and guarantee of loss is not permissible because guarantee can only be for a guaranteed thing; or it is a proxy to purchase regarding the orderer, which is also not permissible due to the ignorance of the silk and its price. And it was said its form is: that he buys it for more than its value in order to sell it for less than that price to a non-seller.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage expands on a matn statement: 'A surety whose principal ordered him to buy a garment via 'inah, and he did so, it belongs to him.' The commentator clarifies that the principal ordered the surety to purchase using a type of transaction called 'bay' al-'inah'. He then provides a detailed definition of 'inah from al-Misbah al-Munir, explaining that it is a credit sale where the buyer later sells the item for cash to avoid riba. The ruling: if there is a stipulation to repurchase, it is haram; if no stipulation, Shafi'i allows it, but early scholars prohibited it. Then the commentator gives the jurists' definition: a person instructs another to buy goods and then sell them, guaranteeing any loss. The transaction is not valid for the orderer because it involves either a guarantee of loss (which is invalid) or a proxy purchase with unknown subject matter. Therefore, the surety keeps the profit or bears the loss; the orderer is not liable.

Key Terms
عينة ('inah): A sale on credit with a subsequent repurchase for cash, intended to provide liquidity while avoiding an explicit interest transaction.
نسيئة (nasī'ah): Deferred payment; selling on credit with payment at a future date.
ضمان بالخسران (damān bi al-khusrān): Guarantee of loss; a promise to cover any loss incurred by another party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the orderer (principal) not liable for the loss in a bay' al-'inah transaction ordered to the surety?
A) Because the surety is absolutely liable
B) Because the orderer's instruction is a guarantee of loss, which is invalid, and the proxy purchase is void due to ignorance
C) Because the surety acted without authority
D) Because the profit is haram

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-'inah with a stipulation is haram; without stipulation it is disputed, but in a suretyship the orderer is not liable due to invalid guarantee and gharar.

Rulings on Profit in Kafalah and Validity of Suretyship
Explains matn on profit from defective sale and kafalah with uncertain amount.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on profit in a void agency and explain why a guarantee for an unknown amount is invalid.

Core Idea
A void agency places profit and loss on the agent; an uncertain guarantee is invalid due to ignorance.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling of profit in a defective agency (wakalah fasidah) and the invalidity of a suretyship (kafalah) for an unknown amount. The key issue is that ignorance of the commodity or price renders the contract void, so the surety bears any resulting profit or loss. Understanding this helps distinguish valid from invalid guarantees in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the ruling
The principal orders the surety to buy a cloth at an inflated price to cover a debt. The cloth is bought for 15, sold for 10, causing a loss. The ruling: the profit (the 5 difference) is upon the surety.
The profit is actually a loss to the surety, referred to as profit because the seller gained extra.
2
Determine the legal basis
The agency is void because the cloth type and the inflated price are unknown. Therefore, it is not a valid agency; the surety acts on his own account.
Ignorance of essential terms (jahalah) invalidates the contract.
3
Analyze the consequence
Since the agency is void, the surety bears the risk and profit/loss. The profit referred to is actually the loss from the inflated purchase.
4
Examine the second case: guarantee with unknown amount
If a person guarantees an unknown amount (e.g., 'what melted for him' or 'what was decreed'), the guarantee is invalid because the amount is uncertain.
The claimant's evidence is rejected when the principal is absent.
5
Apply the principle
Ignorance (jahl) of the subject matter or amount renders the contract void, and the claimant's case is rejected when the principal is absent.
This is a procedural consequence of an invalid guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
وما ريح بائعه فعليه ، ولو كَفِلَ بما ذابَ له ، أو بما قَضَى له عليه ، وغاب أصيله، فأقام مُدَّعيه بينةً على كفيله أن له على أصيله كذا ردت
[۱]
وبيع العينة : أن يستقرض رجل من تاجر شيئاً، ولا يقرضُهُ قرضاً حسناً، بل يعطيه عيناً، ويبعها من المستقرض بأكثر من القيمة ، فالعينة مشتقة من العين سمي بها ؛ لأنه إعراض عن الدين إلى العين، فالأصيل أمر كفيله بأن يشتري ثوباً بأكثر من القيمة ليقضي به دينَهُ ففعل، فالثَّوبُ للكفيل ؛ لأن هذه وكالة فاسدة" لعدم تعين النُّوبِ والثَّمن.
(وما ربح بائعُهُ فعليه) : أي إذا اشترى الثَّوبَ بخمسة عشر، وهو يساوي عشرة، فباعه بالعشرة، فالرِّبْحُ الذي حصل للبائع، وهو الخمسة التي صارت على الكفيل، فعلى الكفيل ؛ لأنَّ الوكالة لما لم تصح صارَ كأَنه قال له : إن اشتريت ثوبا بشيء، ثُمَّ بعتَهُ بأقل من ذلك فأنا ضامن لذلك الخسران، فهذا الضمان ليس بشيء.
ولو كفل بما ذاب له ، أو بما قَضَى له عليه، وغاب أصيله، فأقام مُدَّعيه بينةً على كفيله أن له على أصيله كذا ردَّت)
[١] قوله : أن يستقرض ...الخ ؛ قال في «فتح القدير ) (۱): وأما تفسيره بأن يستقرض من تاجر...الخ، فلا يصح هذا إذ ليس المراد من قوله : تعين علـي حـريـرا اذهب فاستقرض، فإن لم يرض المسؤول أن يقرضك فاشتر منه الحرير بأكثر من قيمته، بل المقصود اذهب فاشتر على هذا الوجه. انتهى. وهذا بحث دقيق فانظر فيه. [٢] قوله : وكالة فاسدة ؛ أما كونه وكالة ؛ فلأنه أمره بالشراء علـى وجـه مخصوص، وأما فسادها ؛ فلجهالة الثوب، فإن الثوب أجناس مختلفة ولم يعين نوعاً منه ؛ ولجهالة الثمن فإن بيع العينة لا يتحقق إلا بازدياد الثمن من قيمة السلعة، وتلك الزيادة مجهولة، فإن كان الدين معلوماً وقدر الدين مع تلك الزيادة ثمن السلعة فيكون الثمن مجهولا .
[٣] قوله : ولو كَفل بما ذاب.... الخ ؛ حاصله : أن رجلاً لو كفل عن رجل آخر بما وجب لفلان على ذلك الرجل أو كفل عن رجل بما قضى لفلان على ذلك الرجل،
The cream of the end for the support of the care. And whatever profit its seller makes is upon him, and if he guarantees with what melted for him, or with what was decreed for him against him, and his principal is absent, and his claimant brings evidence against his surety that he has such and such against his principal, it is rejected. [1] Bay' al-'Inah: that a man borrows something from a merchant, and he does not lend him a good loan, but gives him property and sells it to the borrower for more than its value. 'Inah is derived from 'ayn, so named because it is turning away from debt to property. So the principal ordered his surety to buy a cloth for more than its value to pay his debt, and he did, so the cloth is for the surety because this is a void agency due to the non-specification of the cloth and the price. (And whatever profit its seller makes is upon him): meaning if he bought the cloth for fifteen while it is worth ten, and sold it for ten, the profit that accrued to the seller, which is the five that became due on the surety, is upon the surety; because when the agency is invalid, it becomes as if he said to him: 'If you buy a cloth for something, then sell it for less than that, I am guarantor for that loss,' so this guarantee is nothing. And if he guaranteed with what melted for him, or with what was decreed for him against him, and his principal is absent, and his claimant brings evidence against his surety that he has such and such against his principal, it is rejected. [1] His saying: 'that he borrows...' etc.; he said in Fath al-Qadir: 'As for interpreting it as borrowing from a merchant...etc., this is not correct, for the meaning of his saying: 'obligate on me silk, go and borrow,' if the borrower is not willing to lend you, then buy from him silk for more than its value,' but the intent is: go and buy in this manner. End. And this is a precise discussion, so look into it. [2] His saying: 'a void agency'; as for it being an agency, it is because he ordered him to purchase in a specific manner; and as for its voidness, it is due to the ignorance of the cloth, for cloths are of different types and he did not specify a type; and due to the ignorance of the price, for Bay' al-'Inah is not realized except with an increase in price from the value of the commodity, and that increase is unknown; for if the debt is known and the amount of the debt with that increase is the price of the commodity, then the price becomes unknown. [3] His saying: 'and if he guaranteed with what melted...' etc.; its summary: that if a man guarantees for another man with what is obligatory for so-and-so on that man, or guarantees for a man with what was decreed for so-and-so on that man,
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage contains a matn and its commentary. The matn states: 'Whatever profit its seller makes is upon him' — meaning that if a person (kafil) is asked to buy a cloth at an inflated price to cover a debt, and the sale results in a loss (the cloth bought for 15 sold for 10), the profit (the difference) is upon the seller (kafil) because the agency was void. The commentary explains that the agency is void due to ignorance of the cloth type and price. Then the matn continues: 'if he guaranteed with what melted for him, or with what was decreed for him against him, and his principal is absent, and his claimant brings evidence against his surety that he has such and such against his principal, it is rejected.' This means that if the guarantee is for an unknown amount (like 'what is known' or 'what was decreed'), and the principal is absent, the claimant's case against the surety is rejected because the amount is uncertain. The commentary also clarifies the definition of Bay' al-'Inah and explains why the agency is void.

Key Terms
ما ربح بائعه فعليه (ma rabiha ba'i'uhu fa-'alayhi): Whatever profit the seller makes (in a defective agency) is upon him (the agent/surety) — meaning he bears the loss.
كفل بما ذاب له (kafala bima dhaba lahu): To guarantee with what is known (or melted) for him — an uncertain amount known only to the parties.
وكالة فاسدة (wakalah fasidah): A corrupt agency — one that lacks essential specifications (e.g., unknown item or price).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the agency (wakalah) in the Bay' al-'Inah scenario considered void?
A) Because the buyer did not intend to pay
B) Because the cloth type and price were unknown
C) Because the surety was not present
D) Because the debt was already paid

Key Takeaway
A contract with unknown essential terms is void, placing risk on the agent; guarantees for unknown amounts are invalid.

Kafalah with Condition of Post-Contract Judicial Decree
Ruling on rejecting evidence against kafil when principal is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on rejecting evidence against a kafil when the principal is absent, and explain the condition that the debt must be established by judicial decree after the kafalah.

Core Idea
A kafil is only liable for debts established by judge after the kafalah, not for pre-existing debts.

This lesson examines a specific kafalah scenario where the guarantee is conditional on a future judicial decree. When the principal is absent, the claimant's evidence against the kafil is rejected unless it shows the debt was adjudged after the kafalah contract. We will unpack the condition, the reasoning, and the intra-Hanafi difference of narration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The act is kafalah (suretyship) with a condition that the guaranteed debt is established by a judge. The ruling: if the principal is absent, evidence against the kafil is rejected.
2
Understand the condition of the kafalah
The kafalah is conditional: the debt must be established (through judicial decree) after the contract. The condition must be future and uncertain (shart mustaqbal 'ala khatar al-wujud).
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
The kafil only guarantees what arises after the contract. Evidence of a pre-existing debt does not fall under the guarantee. The phrase 'ma dhaba' (what has established) and 'ma qada' (what was adjudged) both imply a future judicial event.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi narration dispute
There is a weaker narration that rejects evidence absolutely and a stronger narration (mufta bihi) that allows acceptance if the debt was adjudged after kafalah. The stronger view says the Hanafi judge should accept evidence if the debt was established after the kafalah, but only if the judge permits it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٣] قوله : ولو كَفل بما ذاب.... الخ ؛ حاصله : أن رجلاً لو كفل عن رجل آخر بما وجب لفلان على ذلك الرجل أو كفل عن رجل بما قضى لفلان على ذلك الرجل، لأنَّه إذا أقام البينة أن له على أصيله ، ولم يتعرّض لقضاء القاضي به لا يجب على الكفيل ؛ لأنَّه كَفِلَ بما قضى القاضي به ، ولم يوجد هذا في الكفالة بما قضى له عليه ظاهر، وكذا بما ذاب له عليه ؛ لأن معناه تقرر ، وهو بالقضاء. فغاب المطلوب أعني المكفول عنه ، وأقام المدعي البينة على أن له على المطلوب ألفاً مثلاً رُدَّت البينة حتى يحضر الغائب فيقضى عليه.

(1) وفيه نظر؛ فإن المفتى به نفاد القضاء على الغائب من حاكم يراه كشافعي حتى لو رفع حكمه إلى الحنفي نفذه كما حرَّرَه صاحب «البحر»(۳) نفسه في (كتاب القضاء) وكلامهم هاهنا في الحاكم الحنفي، فإن حكمه ينفدُ لِما علمته من عدم الخصم، كذا في «رد المختار»(4).
And if he becomes surety for what has been established for him, or for what was adjudged for him upon him, and his principal is absent, and the claimant brings evidence against his surety that he has such-and-such upon his principal, it is rejected. [3] His saying: 'and if he becomes surety for what has been established... etc.'; its summary: if a man becomes surety for another regarding what is due to someone from that man, or becomes surety for a man regarding what was adjudged for someone against that man, because if he brings evidence that he has a right against his principal without mentioning that the judge adjudged it, it is not binding on the surety because he became surety for what the judge adjudged, and this is not present. Similarly for 'what has been established' because its meaning is established by adjudication. So if the defendant (the guaranteed) is absent, and the claimant brings evidence that the absent owes him a thousand, the evidence is rejected until the absent person comes and judgment is passed against him. [1] His saying: 'because... etc.'; its clarification: he is only a surety for a liability arising after the suretyship because he made the establishment a condition, and a condition must be future and uncertain. So if the establishment did not occur after the suretyship, he is not a surety. The evidence does not witness for a debt arising after the suretyship, so it is not established against the one who is described as surety for the absent, but rather against a third party. This is clear in the wording 'adjudged'. Similarly for 'established' because it means decided and obligatory, and that is by adjudication after the suretyship. So even if he claims that I brought the absent to such a judge and established evidence, and the judge adjudged for me after the suretyship, and he brings evidence of that, then he becomes a surety and the claim is valid, and judgment is passed against the surety for the money, making his heirs a litigant on behalf of the absent, whether the suretyship was by his order or not.
