(1) There is consideration; the mufta bihi is the validity of the judgment against the absent by a judge who considers it permissible like the Shafi'i, so if his ruling is brought to a Hanafi judge, he implements it, as the author of al-Bahr himself clarified in Kitab al-Qada'. And their discussion here is about the Hanafi judge, for his ruling is implemented due to the absence of the opponent, as in Radd al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific type of kafalah where the kafil guarantees a debt that has been established by judicial decree or has become due. The author explains that if the principal is absent and the claimant brings evidence against the kafil, the evidence is rejected unless it shows that the debt was adjudged after the kafalah. The reasoning is that the kafalah is conditional upon the debt being established by the judge after the contract, so evidence of a pre-existing debt does not fall within the scope of the guarantee. The commentary distinguishes between 'what has been established' and 'what has been adjudged', both requiring a judicial decree after the kafalah. It also discusses the scenario where the kafalah was with or without the principal's order, and the resulting effect on the judgment. The author notes a difference of narration: the stronger view (mufta bihi) allows judgment against an absent defendant, but the Hanafi judge's ruling is only binding if the opponent is present, so in this case the evidence is rejected until the absent principal is present. The commentary from Radd al-Mukhtar clarifies that this applies specifically to a Hanafi judge; a Shafi'i judge may rule against the absent, and a Hanafi judge will enforce it.

Key Terms
ما ذاب له (ma dhaba lahu): What has become established or due as a debt through judicial decree.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; guarantee of a debt or obligation.
قضاء على الغائب (qada' ala al-gha'ib): Judgment against an absent defendant.

Key Takeaway
A kafil is only liable for debts that are established by a judge after the kafalah contract; evidence of a pre-existing debt is rejected when the principal is absent.

Guarantor Liability for Debts Established After Guarantee
Ruling on suretyship for court-established debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for guarantor liability in Hanafi fiqh and differentiate between the weak and strong transmissions regarding proof against an absent principal.

Core Idea
A guarantor is only liable for a debt established by court ruling after the guarantee.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqāyah clarifies a key condition in suretyship (kafālah). The guarantor is not responsible for any pre-existing debt; rather, the debt must become due (yadhūb) after the guarantee is made, as evidenced by a court ruling. The text also addresses the dispute within the Hanafi school about accepting proof when the principal is absent, and explains the muftā bihī position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? The act is kafālah (suretyship). The ruling is that the guarantor is not liable unless the debt is established by a court ruling after the guarantee.
The ruling here is a condition for liability, not the validity of the contract itself.
2
Locate the condition
The condition is that the debt must become due (yadhūb) after the guarantee. The text uses the term 'dhawāb' to indicate the debt's establishment through a court ruling.
The condition is future and uncertain at the time of the guarantee.
3
Understand the proof requirement
The claimant must present evidence that a court ruling was issued after the guarantee. If the proof does not mention a ruling after the guarantee, it is insufficient.
The proof is rejected until the absent principal appears.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a weak transmission that rejects proof against an absent principal absolutely. The stronger transmission (muftā bihī) allows it if the judge permits ruling on absent persons.
This dispute affects whether the guarantor can be held liable when the principal is absent.
5
Determine the operative fatwa
The muftā bihī is the stronger transmission: if a judge deems it valid to rule against an absent person, the proof is accepted and the guarantor may be liable, provided the debt was established after the guarantee.
This is the position relied upon in later Hanafi fatwa literature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة تعريفها وشروطها وأحكامها
لأنَّه إذا أقام البينة أن له على أصيله ، ولم يتعرّض لقضاء القاضي به لا يجب على الكفيل ؛ لأنَّه كَفِلَ بما قضى القاضي به ، ولم يوجد هذا في الكفالة بما قضى له عليه ظاهر، وكذا بما ذاب له عليه ؛ لأن معناه تقرر ، وهو بالقضاء. فغاب المطلوب أعني المكفول عنه ، وأقام المدعي البينة على أن له على المطلوب ألفاً مثلاً رُدَّت البينة حتى يحضر الغائب فيقضى عليه.
[١]قوله : لأنه...الخ ؛ توضيحه : أنه إنما كفيل عنه بمال مقتضى بعد الكفالة ؛ لأنه جعل الذوب شرطاً، والشرط لا بد من كونه مستقبلاً على خطر الوجود، فما لم يوجد الذوب بعد الكفالة لا يكون كفيلاً، والبيّنة لم تشهد لقضاء دين وجب بعد الكفالة ، فلـن تقـم عـلـى مَن اتَّصف بكونه كفيلاً عن الغائب، بل على أجنبي، وهذا في لفظ القضاء ظاهر.
وكذا في ذاب ؛ لأن معناه تقرر ووجب، وهو بالقضاء بعد الكفالة حتى لو دعا أني قدمت الغائب إلى قاضي كذا وأقمت عليه بيّنة، هكذا بعد الكفالة وقضى لي عليه بذلك، وأقام البينة على ذلك صار كفيلاً وصحت الدعوى، وقضى على الكفيل بالمال ليصر ورثته خصماً عن الغائب سواء كانت الكفالة بأمره أو لا ، إلا أنه إذا كانت بغير أمره يكون القضاء على الكفيل خاصة ، كذا في «الفتح» (1) وقال صاحب «البحر» (2) : وجزمهم بهذا بعدم القبول ينبغي أن يكون على الرواية الضعيفة، أما على أظهر الروايتين المفتى به من نفاذ القضاء على الغائب ينبغي النفاذ، ولم أر من نبه عليه هاهنا. انتهى.
(1) وفيه نظر؛ فإن المفتى به نفاد القضاء على الغائب من حاكم يراه كشافعي حتى لو رفع حكمه إلى الحنفي نفذه كما حرَّرَه صاحب «البحر» (2) نفسه في (كتاب القضاء) وكلامهم هاهنا في الحاكم الحنفي، فإن حكمه ينفدُ لِما علمته من عدم الخصم، كذا في «رد المختار».
Book of Suretyship: Its Definition, Conditions, and Rulings
Because if he establishes proof that he has a claim against his principal, and does not mention a court ruling on it, the guarantor is not liable; because he guaranteed what the court ruled on, and this (court ruling after guarantee) does not exist in the case of a claim established before guarantee, and similarly for a debt that has become due; because its meaning is established, and that is through court ruling. So the defendant, i.e., the guaranteed person, is absent, and the claimant establishes proof that he has, for example, a thousand against the defendant — the proof is rejected until the absent person appears and judgment is passed against him.
[1] His statement: 'because... etc.'; explanation: He is only a guarantor for a debt that becomes due after the guarantee; because he made the accrual a condition, and a condition must be future and uncertain to exist. So as long as the accrual after the guarantee does not exist, he is not a guarantor, and the evidence does not testify to a debt that became obligatory after the guarantee, so it is not established against someone who is characterized as a guarantor for the absent person, but rather against a stranger. And this is clear in the wording of 'ruling'.
And similarly for 'became due'; because its meaning is established and obligatory, and that is through a court ruling after the guarantee. So if he claims that he brought the absent person to such-and-such judge and established proof against him after the guarantee, and the judge ruled in his favor, and he establishes proof of that, he becomes a guarantor and the lawsuit is valid, and judgment is passed against the guarantor for the money, making him a representative for the absent person, whether the guarantee was with the principal's permission or not, except that if it was without permission, the ruling is against the guarantor only. This is stated in al-Fatḥ. And the author of al-Baḥr said: Their definite statement about non-acceptance should be based on the weak transmission. As for the more apparent of the two transmissions that is the basis for fatwa — the validity of judgment against an absent person — acceptance should be valid. And I have not seen anyone draw attention to this here. End.
(1) And there is consideration; because the basis for fatwa is the validity of judgment against an absent person by a judge who deems it like a Shāfiʿī, so that if his ruling is presented to a Ḥanafī judge, he executes it, as al-Baḥr himself clarified in the Book of Judgments. And their discussion here concerns the Ḥanafī judge; his judgment is not valid due to the absence of the opponent, as stated in Radd al-Mukhtār.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage opens the book of Kafālah (suretyship) in the commentary on al-Wiqāyah. The author begins by defining the chapter. He then presents a ruling: if a claimant brings proof that he has a debt against the principal (aṣīl) but does not also prove that a court ruling was issued after the guarantee, the guarantor (kafīl) is not liable. The reason is that the guarantee is specifically for the debt that the court rules on after the guarantee, not for any pre-existing debt. The proof is rejected until the absent principal appears. The commentary explains the condition: the accrual of the debt (dhawāb) must occur after the guarantee; otherwise, the guarantor is not bound. The commentator also notes that if the guarantee was without the principal's permission, the ruling is only against the guarantor, who acts as representative. There is a discussion about conflicting transmissions: some say the proof is absolutely rejected, but the muftā bihī position (the operative fatwa) allows ruling against an absent person if the judge follows that view. The super-commentator clarifies that the discussion here is about a Ḥanafī judge, who does not rule against an absent party.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): A contract where one person (guarantor) joins another (principal) in liability for a debt or obligation.
الأصيل (al-aṣīl): The original debtor for whom the guarantee is made.
الغائب (al-ghāʾib): The absent principal who is not present in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the debt existed before the guarantee.
B) When the debt is established by court ruling after the guarantee.
C) Whenever the principal admits the debt.
D) Only if the guarantee was made with the principal's permission.

Key Takeaway
For guarantor liability, the debt must be established by a court ruling that occurs after the guarantee, based on the stronger transmission that allows proof against an absent principal.

Guarantor with Permission: Joint Liability and Conditions
Ruling statement with conditions and response to an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions under which a guarantor's permission leads to joint liability and explain why judgment against an absent principal is permitted in this case.

Core Idea
Principal's permission implies acknowledgement, making him liable despite absence.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in kafalah (suretyship): when a guarantor has the principal's permission to guarantee a debt. The commentator clarifies that this permission carries legal consequences, including allowing judgment against an absent principal. We will unpack the three required conditions and the rationale behind the exception to the general Hanafi rule against judging absent persons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
A claimant proves a debt against an absent principal and also proves that a present person guaranteed that debt with the principal's permission. What is the court's ruling?
The ruling is: judgment against both guarantor and principal.
2
List the Three Conditions
The commentator specifies three conditions that must be present: (1) the guarantee is restricted to this specific money, (2) the money is not restricted to a court ruling after the guarantee (it is absolute), (3) the guarantee is with the principal's permission. Understand why each condition matters.
Permission is the most critical—it implies acknowledgement of the debt.
3
Address the Objection about Absent Persons
A standard Hanafi rule is that judgment cannot be given against an absent person. Why is this an exception? The claimant cannot reach his right from the present guarantor without establishing the debt against the absent principal. Necessity allows the exception.
The analogy to a slave claim case supports this exception.
4
Note the Guarantor's Right of Recourse
After paying, the guarantor can seek reimbursement from the principal without re-proving the debt, because the principal is implicitly judged against.
This follows from the principal's implied acknowledgement.

And if he establishes proof that he has such-and-such against Zayd, and this is his guarantor with his permission, the judge shall rule against both of them. This is the beginning of a new issue not connected to what preceded, which was about guarantee for a debt that became due or was ruled upon. The scenario: a man establishes proof that he has a thousand against Zayd, and this person is his guarantor for this money with Zayd's permission — the judge rules against both. In this case, he guaranteed this money without reference to a court ruling, unlike the previous issue. So when judgment is given against both, the guarantor has the right to recourse against the principal.
[1] His statement: 'And if he establishes...' etc.; summary: a man brings a person to the judge and establishes proof that he has a thousand dirhams against the absent Zayd, and that this person is his guarantor with Zayd's permission. The proof is accepted, and judgment is given against both the principal and the guarantor. In this issue there are three notable conditions:
First: The guarantee is restricted to this specific money.
Second: This money is not restricted to being ruled upon after the guarantee; rather it is an absolute debt. This distinguishes this issue from the previous one, where the guaranteed money was restricted to a court ruling.
Third: This guarantee is restricted to being with the principal's permission, because permission entails acknowledgement of the debt, so he becomes judged against. But if it is without his permission, it does not entail acknowledgement, so judgment against the guarantor does not include judgment against the principal. [2] His statement: 'The judge shall rule against both.' If it is said: How can judgment be given against an absent person when the guarantee was with his permission, and judgment against an absent person is not permissible in our school? We say: If the claimant cannot reach his right against the present person except by proving it against the absent person, then it is permissible to give judgment against the absent person. Just as if a slave claims that the present person bought him from his absent master and then freed him, and the present person denies the purchase and manumission — the present person is a representative for his master; once the slave proves the purchase and manumission, it is effective against the absent master, and even if he later appears, he cannot contest it. This is stated in al-Ramz and al-Tabyīn.
[3] His statement: 'The right of recourse against the principal without re-establishing proof when he appears; because he has become implicitly judged against.'

Commentary
This passage introduces a distinct scenario in suretyship: a claimant brings a guarantor who has guaranteed a specific debt with the principal's permission. The commentator notes that this case differs from the previous one because here the debt is absolute (not tied to a court ruling after the guarantee), and the guarantee is with the principal's permission. Because permission implies the principal's acknowledgement of the debt, the court can rule against both the guarantor and the absent principal, treating the guarantor as a representative. The text then addresses a potential objection: Ḥanafī law generally does not permit judgment against an absent person. The response explains that when the claimant's only means to get his due from the present guarantor is to establish the debt against the absent principal, it becomes permissible, akin to the case of a slave claiming purchase from an absent master. Finally, the guarantor has the right to recourse against the principal when he appears, without needing to re-prove the debt, as the principal is impliedly judged against.

Key Terms
بأمره (bi-amrihi): The guarantee is made with the permission and instruction of the principal.
الرجوع (al-rujūʿ): The guarantor's right to demand reimbursement from the principal after paying.

Key Takeaway

Kafalah with Principal's Order: Judgment Against Both Surety and Absent Principal
Ruling on suretyship with principal's order and judgment against absent debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid judgment against an absent principal in Hanafi kafalah and explain why the principal's order suffices for ruling in absentia.

Core Idea
The principal's order implies acknowledgment, allowing judgment against the absent debtor.

This lesson explores a specific case of kafalah where the surety guarantees the debt with the principal's order. The key legal question is how a judge can rule against an absent principal when Hanafi law generally prohibits judgment in absentia. The passage explains that the principal's order serves as an implicit acknowledgment of the debt, and necessity permits the exception. Through qiyas with a slave purchase case, the Hanafi school justifies this ruling to protect the creditor's right without requiring the principal's presence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The passage begins: a claimant presents evidence that he is owed money by Zaid (absent) and that a present person is his surety with Zaid's order. The judge rules against both the surety and the absent principal.
This is the core ruling of the lesson.
2
Extract the three conditions
The commentary lists three conditions: (1) suretyship is specifically for that money, (2) the guaranteed money is not conditional on a prior judgment, and (3) the suretyship is made with the order of the principal. The third condition is critical.
Without the principal's order, only the surety is judged.
3
Analyze the apparent contradiction
The text raises a question: how can the judge rule against an absent person when the Hanafi school does not permit judgment in absentia? The answer uses a qiyas analogy.
This step addresses the central legal challenge.
4
Understand the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
The analogy: a slave claims the present person bought him from his absent master and freed him. The present person denies. The present person is considered a representative for his master, so if the slave proves the claims, the judgment is effective against the absent master. Similarly, the surety, through the principal's order, becomes a representative; proving the surety's obligation also proves the principal's acknowledgment.
The 'illa (effective cause) is the representation and necessity.
5
Note the practical consequence
The surety has the right of recourse against the principal without needing to re-establish evidence when the principal appears, because he is already ruled against implicitly.
This facilitates the surety's recovery.

[٢] قوله : قضى به عليهما ؛ فإن قيل : كيف يقضي على الغائب إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره والقضاء على الغائب لا يجوز عندنا. قلنا : إذا لم يتوصل إلى حقه على الحاضر إلا بإثباته على الغائب يجوز القضاء على الغائب. كما إذا ادعى عبد أن الحاضر اشتراه من مولاه الغائب، ثم أعتقه فأنكر الحاضر الشراء والإعتاق، كان الحاضر خصماً عن مولاه حتى إذا أثبت العبدُ الشراء والعتق نفذ على الغائب، حتى إذا حضر ليس له أن يدَّعيه ، كذا في «الرمز» (۱) ، و«التبيين» (۲) [٣] قوله : حق الرجوع على الأصيل بلا إعادة بينة عليه إذا حضر؛ لأنه صار مقضياً عليه ضمناً.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its explanation on kafalah (suretyship) where the surety guarantees with the order of the principal. The base text is from the matn, and the author (the commentator) expands on it. The ruling states that if a claimant presents evidence that he is owed money by Zaid (the principal debtor) and that a present person is his surety with Zaid's order, the judge rules against both the surety and the absent principal. The commentary explains three conditions: (1) the suretyship is specifically for that money, (2) the guaranteed money is not conditional on a prior judgment (unlike a previous case), and (3) the order implies the principal's acknowledgment of the debt, making him effectively a party to the judgment. A key question arises: How can the judge rule against an absent person when Hanafi law generally prohibits judgment against the absent? The answer is that when the right cannot be obtained from the present party without establishing it against the absent one, it becomes permissible. The example of a slave bought from an absent master is given as analogy. The right of recourse for the surety against the principal does not require renewing evidence when the principal appears, as he is already implicitly judged.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person (kafil) guarantees a debt or obligation of another (asil) to a creditor.
أصيل (asil): The principal debtor whose debt is guaranteed by the surety.
كفيل (kafil): The surety who guarantees the debt of the principal.

Kafalah Without Order: Judgment Only Against Surety
Ruling on suretyship without principal's order and the effect on the absent principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah without order and distinguish the legal effect on the surety versus the absent principal.

Core Idea
In kafalah without order, judgment is only against the surety; the principal is not implicated due to lack of acknowledgment.

This passage examines a key distinction in Hanafi suretyship law: when a person guarantees a debt without the principal's authorization, the judge rules solely against the surety. The principal, being absent from the transaction, is not affected by the judgment. This lesson unpacks the rationale behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine the type of kafalah: without the principal's order (bila amrih). The surety voluntarily guarantees a debt that the principal owes, but the principal did not request or acknowledge the guarantee.
2
State the ruling
The judge rules against the surety only (qada 'ala al-kafil faqat). The absent principal is not subject to the judgment.
3
Understand the basis
The distinction between kafalah with order and without order: with order, the principal implicitly acknowledges the debt (tabarru' ibtida'an wa mu'awada intiha'an); without order, it is purely a donation (tabarru' ibtida'an wa intiha'an), so the principal's side is not touched.
The key is the absence of acknowledgment from the principal.
4
Apply to the absent principal
Since the claim is for absolute money and the principal never acknowledged the guarantee, the judgment cannot extend to him. The surety’s liability stands alone.
5
Note the legal implication
If the surety pays, he may have recourse against the principal only if he had a valid agreement, but the court judgment itself does not create liability for the principal.
This is a corollary from the principle, not directly stated in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الكفالة بلا أمره قضى على الكفيل فقط (وفي الكفالة بلا أمرِه قضى على الكفيل فقط ) : أي أقامَ البيِّنةَ على أنَّه كفيله بلا أمره يقضي القاضي بالمال على الكفيل فقط. [٢] قوله : على الكفيل فقط ؛ ولا يقضي على الغائب ؛ لأن المدعى هاهنا مال مطلق فأمكن إثباته بخلاف ما تقدَّم، فإنّه مقيَّد بكون المال مقضيا به على الأصيل، وإنما يختلف الحكم بالأمر وعدمه ؛ لأنهما يتغايران ؛ لأن الكفالة بأمره تبرع ابتداءً ومعاوضة انتهاء، وبغير أمره تبرع ابتداءً وانتهاء. فالقضاء بأحديهما لا يكون قضاء بالآخر إذا قضى بها بالأمر ثبت، وهو يتضمَّنُ الإقرار بالمال فيصير مقضياً عليه، والكفالة بغير أمره لا يمس جانبه ؛ لأن صحتها تعتمد قيام الدين في زعم الكفيل، فلا يتعدى إليه. كذا قالوا.
And in suretyship without his order, he rules against the surety only. (And in suretyship without his order, he rules against the surety only): i.e., he establishes evidence that he is his surety without his order, the judge rules for the money against the surety only. [2] His statement: 'against the surety only'; and he does not rule against the absent one, because the claim here is for absolute money, so it is possible to establish it, contrary to what preceded, which was restricted to the money being ruled against the principal. The ruling differs with and without order because they differ; for suretyship with his order is a donation initially and a compensation finally, and without his order it is a donation initially and finally. So a ruling on one is not a ruling on the other. When he rules with his order, it is established, and it includes acknowledgment of the money, so he becomes ruled against. And suretyship without his order does not affect his side, because its validity depends on the existence of the debt in the surety's belief, so it does not extend to him. Thus they said.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This is a ruling on kafalah when the surety guarantees the debt without the order of the principal debtor. In this case, the judge only rules against the surety, not against the absent principal. The commentary explains that the claim here is for absolute money (not conditional on a prior judgment), so it is possible to establish it. The distinction between kafalah with order and without order is that with order, the principal acknowledges the debt, making him implicitly a party, while without order, the surety is acting purely as a donor (tabarru'), and the validity of the suretyship depends on the surety's belief that the debt exists. Therefore, the ruling does not extend to the principal. This is a clear distinction that guards against conflating the two types of suretyship.

Key Terms
تبرع (tabarru'): A gratuitous act, donation; here it describes the nature of the suretyship without order as purely voluntary with no expectation of compensation.
الكفالة بلا أمر (al-kafalah bila amr): Suretyship without the principal's order or authorization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Against both the surety and the principal.
B) Against the surety only.
C) Against the principal only.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
In kafalah without order, the judgment is confined to the surety because the principal never acknowledged the debt.

Guarantee Against Eviction (Daman al-Darak) and Its Effect on Claims
Ruling on the effect of daman al-darak on subsequent claims and the dispute with Imam Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on daman al-darak, describe its effect on subsequent claims, and analyse the intra-Hanafi dispute with Imam Zufar regarding recourse after denial.

Core Idea
A guarantee of eviction acts as an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, barring the guarantor from later claiming the property or denying the debt.

In Hanafi sales law, a third party may guarantee the buyer against eviction (daman al-darak). This seemingly simple promise carries significant legal consequences: it functions as an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, precluding the guarantor from later asserting any property right or denying the debt. This lesson unpacks the ruling and examines a subtle disagreement within the Hanafi school concerning the guarantor's right to recourse if he denies the underlying debt.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on the effect of darak guarantee
Identify the ruling: the guarantee nullifies any subsequent claim by the guarantor (e.g., ownership, pre-emption) because it is deemed an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.
This is the majority view, labelled 'this is according to us' in the text.
2
Identify Imam Zufar's opposing view
Zufar argues that if the guarantor denies the debt (i.e., claims the guarantee was invalid), he cannot have recourse against the seller because his denial means he believes the debt was never owed, so he should not wrong another by seeking repayment.
Zufar's focus is on the recourse after denial, not on the nullification of claims per se.
3
Follow the proof of each side
The majority relies on the idea that sharia belies the denial: the guarantee itself is an acknowledgment, so the later denial is contradictory and disregarded. Zufar insists on respecting the guarantor's stated belief.
The text provides an analogy: a buyer who acknowledges the seller's ownership then loses to a claimant still can seek recourse.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Zufar
The Hanafi response is that sharia belies his denial (the acknowledgment in the guarantee overrides the later claim), so his denial is nullified.
This is based on the principle that contradictory statements are resolved in favour of the earlier one if it constitutes a legal acknowledgment.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under the majority view, the guarantee bars all later claims by the guarantor; if the guarantor denies the debt, he forfeits recourse. Under Zufar's view, if the guarantor denies, he avoids liability for recourse but also loses any right to claim repayment from the seller.
The text adopts the majority view as the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he guarantees the darak, his claim afterwards is nullified, and this is according to us, and according to Zufar, may Allah have mercy on him, he does not have recourse against him, because when he denied, his claim was that this right is not established, but rather the claimant wronged him, so he should not have the right to wrong another. We said: The sharia belied him, so his denial is removed. (And if he guarantees the darak, his claim afterwards is nullified): because it is an encouragement for the buyer to purchase, so it is like an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, so his claim of ownership is not valid. [11] His statement: 'The sharia belied him'; and this is like one who buys something and acknowledges that the seller sold his own property, then a person comes and claims it with evidence, his right to recourse against the seller for the price is not nullified, because he has become disproven by sharia, thus in 'Al-Kifayah'.

Commentary
This passage deals with the concept of 'daman al-darak', which is a guarantee given by a third party to the buyer that if the purchased property is evicted (i.e., taken away by a rightful owner), the guarantor will pay back the price. The ruling states that if someone gives such a guarantee, then later claims ownership of the property (or asserts a right like pre-emption or lease), his claim is not heard because the guarantee itself acts as an acknowledgment that the seller owned the property. The passage includes a difference with Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar): Zufar argued that if the guarantor later denies the debt, he should not have recourse against the seller because he claims the debt was invalid. The Hanafi response is that the sharia belies him (i.e., his acknowledgment in the guarantee overrides his later denial). An example is given: if someone buys an item and acknowledges that the seller sold his own property, then a third party proves ownership, the buyer still has the right to recover the price from the seller because his earlier acknowledgment is overridden by sharia. This ruling emphasizes that the guarantee creates an estoppel against the guarantor.

Key Terms
ضمان الدرك (daman al-darak): A guarantee given to the buyer that if the purchased property is evicted (taken by a rightful claimant), the guarantor will return the price to the buyer.
درك (darak): The risk of eviction; the loss suffered by the buyer if the property is taken from him.

C) It nullifies his claim only if the buyer relies on the guarantee.
D) It nullifies his claim only if the guarantor denies the debt later.

Key Takeaway
Daman al-darak is not merely a promise; it is a binding acknowledgment that precludes the guarantor from asserting ownership or denying the debt.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Two Hanafi Rulings on Kafalah: Without Order and Daman al-Darak
Commentary on two rulings with inter-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for kafalah without order (judgment only against surety) and daman al-darak (barring later claims), and explain Imam Zufar's disagreement on recourse.

Core Idea
Kafalah without order: surety alone liable; daman al-darak: guarantee bars later claims.

In this lesson, we examine two important rulings from the chapter of kafalah. The first deals with suretyship undertaken without the debtor's order—here, the judge only enforces against the surety, not the absent debtor. The second deals with a guarantee against eviction (daman al-darak), which acts as an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership, preventing the surety from later claiming the property. We also explore Imam Zufar's differing view on whether the surety can later seek recourse from the debtor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct legal issues
Recognise that the passage covers two separate rulings: (a) kafalah without the debtor's order and (b) daman al-darak (guarantee against eviction). Each has its own legal consequence.
2
State the ruling and reasoning for kafalah without order
The judge gives judgment only against the surety, not the debtor. Reason: the claim is for absolute property, so it can be established directly against the surety; the debtor is not touched because the suretyship is a pure gift (no compensation at end).
The distinction between with and without order: with order starts as gift ends as compensation; without order remains gift.
3
State the ruling and reasoning for daman al-darak
If a person guarantees against eviction (daman al-darak), his later claim of ownership, pre-emption, or lease is void. Reason: the guarantee serves as an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.
The guarantee is a condition suitable to the contract; it strengthens the buyer's confidence.
4
Analyze Imam Zufar's disagreement on recourse
Imam Zufar holds that if the debtor denies the debt, the surety cannot have recourse against him. His proof: the debtor's denial implies the claim was unjust, so the surety should not wrong the debtor. Hanafi response: the Law (Shariah) rejects the debtor's denial when the claim is proven, so the surety can still claim reimbursement.
The Hanafi view is that the surety's payment is valid and he can recover from the debtor.
5
Apply the rulings to a hypothetical scenario
Given a scenario where a person stands surety without the debtor's order and later the debtor denies the debt, determine: (1) against whom can the creditor obtain judgment? (2) Can the surety later recover from the debtor? (3) If the surety also gave daman al-darak, can he later claim the property?
Practice ensures understanding of the practical outcomes.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two related rulings in the chapter of kafalah (suretyship). The first ruling: when a person stands surety without the debtor's order, the judge only gives judgment against the surety, not against the absent debtor. This is because the claim is for absolute property, and the surety's liability is independent. The second ruling: if the surety guarantees 'al-darak' (the risk of eviction, i.e., compensating the buyer if the sold property is claimed by a third party), this act is treated as an acknowledgment that the seller owned the property at the time of sale. Consequently, the surety cannot later claim ownership, pre-emption, or lease rights to that property. The text notes that Imam Zufar differs: he holds that if the debtor denies the debt, the surety cannot have recourse against him, because the debtor's denial implies the debt was unjust. The Hanafi response is that the Law (Shariah) rejects the debtor's denial when the claim is proven, so the denial is nullified. The commentary further explains that the distinction between kafalah with and without order lies in the nature of the contract: with order it starts as a gift and ends as compensation; without order it remains a gift throughout. The discussion on ضمان الدرك emphasizes that it is a condition suitable for the contract, and once given, it becomes an acknowledgment of the seller's ownership.

Key Terms
كفالة بلا أمره (kafālah bilā amrihī): Suretyship undertaken by a person without being ordered by the debtor to do so.
ضمان الدرك (ḍamān al-darak): A guarantee given to the buyer that if the purchased property is claimed by a third party, the guarantor will compensate the buyer, typically by returning the price.
الدرك (al-darak): The risk that a purchased item may be reclaimed from the buyer by a rightful owner; the guarantee against this risk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The guarantor cannot later claim ownership, pre-emption, or lease rights to the property.
C) The guarantee only activates if the buyer is evicted, and then the guarantor must pay.
D) The guarantee is void unless the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah without order binds only the surety; daman al-darak is an acknowledgment barring later claims by the surety.

The Guarantee of 'Uhda: Why Ambiguity Invalidates
Explanation that guarantee of al-uhda is invalid due to multiple possible meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guaranteeing 'uhda and explain why it is void due to ambiguity.

Core Idea
A guarantee is void if its subject is ambiguous — 'uhda has too many meanings to enforce.

In sales transactions, parties sometimes include a guarantee of the 'covenant' ('uhda). But what exactly is being guaranteed? The term 'uhda carries multiple possible meanings, from an old deed to compensation for a reclaimed item. This lesson explores why the Hanafi school rules such a guarantee void and how clarity is essential in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act in question? It is guaranteeing the 'uhda in a sale. The ruling is that it is batil (void).
2
Understand why 'uhda is problematic
The commentator explains that 'uhda is a homonym with multiple meanings: old deed, contract, rights of contract, option of stipulation, and compensation (darak).
3
Apply the principle of ambiguity
Because the meaning is uncertain, the obligation is unknown, and the guarantee cannot be enforced. The hadith 'uhda al-raqiq thalatha ayyam shows 'uhda can mean option of stipulation, further proving its ambiguity.
4
Contrast with darak guarantee
Unlike 'uhda, darak (compensation when the sold item is reclaimed) has a clear meaning and is known and deliverable, so its guarantee is valid.
5
Evaluate the hypothetical objection
Some might argue that 'uhda should be interpreted as darak to validate the guarantee. The commentator refutes this by noting that without specification, the ambiguity remains.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(أو الخلاص) قوله : العهدة ; بالضم: نبشته سوکند و همان و نبشته خرید و فروخت و تاوان. يقال في الأمر عهدة. كذا في منتهى الارب. قوله : لأن العهدة... الخ ; حاصله : أن العهدة اسم مشترك : قد يقع على الصك القديم ; لأنه وثيقة بمنزلة كتاب العهدة، وهو لملك البائع ولا يلزمه التسليم، فإذا ضمِن تسليمه إلى المشتري فقد ضمِن ما لا يقدر عليه، فلا يصح. وقد يقع على العقد ; لأنه مأخوذ من العهد، والعهد والعقد واحد. وقد يقع على حقوق العقد ; لأنها من ثمرات العقد. وقد يقع على خيار الشرط، كما جاء في الحديث : «عهدة الرقيق ثلاثة أيام» : أي خيار الشرط فيه. وقد يقع على الدرك. فتعذر العمل بها قبل البيان، فبطل الضمان بخلاف الدرك، فإن ضمانه صحيح ; لأنه عبارة عن ضمان الثمن عند استحقاق المبيع، وهو معلوم مقدور التسليم. فإن قيل : ينبغي أن يصرف إلى ما يجوز الضمان به، وهو الدرك تصحيحاً لتصرفه.

His statement: because 'uhda... summary: 'uhda is a homonym: it may refer to the old deed because it is a document like the 'uhda book, and it pertains to the seller's ownership and he is not obliged to deliver, so if he guarantees delivery to the buyer, he guarantees what he cannot do, so it is not valid. And it may refer to the contract because it is derived from 'ahd (covenant), and covenant and contract are the same. And it may refer to the rights of the contract because they are fruits of the contract. And it may refer to the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart), as comes in the hadith: 'The 'uhda of a slave is three days' meaning the option of stipulation in it. And it may refer to compensation (darak). Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage begins a chapter on guarantee (الضمان) in sales. The author states that if a person guarantees the 'uhda (عهدة) or al-khalas (الخلاص), the guarantee is void. The commentator explains that 'uhda is a homonym (اسم مشترك) with multiple possible meanings: the old deed (الصك القديم), the contract itself, rights of the contract, the option of stipulation (خيار الشرط), and compensation (الدرك). Because of this ambiguity, one cannot know which meaning is intended, so the guarantee is invalid (باطل). In contrast, the guarantee of darak (الدرك) — which is a guarantee to refund the price if the sold item is reclaimed — is valid because its meaning is clear, known, and capable of performance. The commentator also provides the Persian/Urdu definitions for 'uhda. The passage ends with a potential objection: why not interpret 'uhda as darak to validate the guarantee? The answer is implicit: 'uhda is not specific enough; only explicit darak guarantee is valid.

Key Terms
عهدة ('uhda): A term with multiple meanings: old deed, contract, rights of contract, option of stipulation, or compensation (darak). The commentator treats it as a homonym.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or surety; an obligation to compensate or fulfill a duty on behalf of another.
درك (darak): Compensation paid when a sold item is reclaimed by a rightful owner; essentially a guarantee of the price.

D) Because guaranteeing 'uhda is prohibited by the hadith.

Key Takeaway
A guarantee is void if the obligation it covers is ambiguous; 'uhda's multiple meanings render its guarantee invalid.

Invalidity of Guarantee of 'Uhdah Due to Ambiguity
Ruling with proof on invalidity of guarantee of 'uhdah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guarantee of 'uhdah and explain why it is invalid due to ambiguity.

Core Idea
A guarantee is invalid if the guaranteed term is ambiguous — 'uhdah is a homonym, so obligation cannot be established.

This passage examines the validity of a guarantee of 'uhdah (title guarantee) in a sale. The term 'uhdah has multiple meanings, including the old document, the contract itself, its rights, the option of stipulation, or recourse (darak). Because the guarantor does not specify which meaning is intended, the guarantee is void. The text contrasts this with a guarantee of darak, which is valid due to its clear meaning, and responds to an objection that the ambiguous term should be interpreted as darak to validate the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a guarantee (kafalah) of 'uhdah. The ruling is that it is invalid (batil). Note the hukm level: batil means void, not merely fasid.
2
Recognise the ambiguity of 'uhdah
List the possible meanings of 'uhdah as given in the text: the old document, the contract, the rights of the contract, the option of stipulation (from the hadith), and darak (recourse). This makes it a mushtarak (homonym).
The hadith 'uhdah of a slave is three days' is cited to show one meaning.
3
Apply the principle of ambiguity
Since the term is ambiguous, the exact obligation cannot be determined. In Hanafi law, obligation cannot be established by doubt (shakk). Therefore, the guarantee is invalid.
4
Contrast with valid guarantee of darak
A guarantee of darak (recourse) is valid because it has a single clear meaning: guaranteeing the price if the sold item is claimed by another. It is known and possible to deliver.
5
Evaluate the objection and response
Objection: Why not interpret the ambiguous 'uhdah as darak to validate the contract? Response: The default is freedom of liability (bara'at al-dhimmah). Obligation cannot be imposed by mere probability. Thus, the ambiguous term cannot be reinterpreted to make the guarantee valid.
This highlights the Hanafi principle that default is no liability until proven.

[١]قوله : العُهدة ؛ بالضم: نبشته سوکند و همان و نبشته خرید وفروخت و تاوان
يقال في الأمر عهدة. كذا في منتهى الارب».
[٢]قوله : لأن العُهدة...الخ ؛ حاصله : أن العهدة اسم مشترك :
١. قد يقع على الصك القديم ؛ لأنه وثيقة بمنزلة كتاب العهدة، وهو لملك البائع ولا يلزمه التسليم ، فإذا ضَمِنَ تسليمه إلى المشتري فقد ضَمِنَ ما لا يقدر عليه، فلا يصح .
٢. وقد يقع على العقد ؛ لأنه مأخوذ من العهد، والعهد والعقد واحد.
٣. وقد يقع على حقوق العقد ؛ لأنها من ثمرات العقد.
٤. وقد يقع على خيار الشرط ، كما جاء في الحديث : «عهدة الرقيق ثلاثة أيام») : أي خيار الشرط فيه. ه. وقد يقع على الدرك.
فتعدَّر العمل بها قبل البيان، فبطل الضمان بخلاف الدرك، فإن ضمانه صحيح ؛ لأنه عبارة عن ضمان الثمن عند استحقاق المبيع، وهو معلوم مقدور التسليم. فإن قيل : ينبغي أن يصرف إلى ما يجوز الضمان به، وهو الدرك تصحيحاً لتصرفه.
قلنا إنمـا لـم يصرف إليه ؛ لأن فراغ الذمة أصل فلا يثبت الشغل بالشك والاحتمال. هكذا قالوا.
A chapter on guarantee. And if he guarantees the 'uhdah (title/responsibility): meaning a man bought a garment, then another guaranteed the 'uhdah, so the guarantee is invalid, because 'uhdah has come for meanings: 1. The old document. 2. The contract and its rights. 3. The defect/recourse. So one of the meanings cannot be established by doubt. [1] The author's saying: al-'uhdah is with damm (u): meaning (Persian gloss). It is said in the matter 'uhdah. So in Muntaha al-Arab. [2] The author's saying: because al-'uhdah...etc.; its gist is that al-'uhdah is a homonym: 1. It may refer to the old document, because it is a document like a book of covenant, and it is the property of the seller and he is not obliged to deliver, so if he guarantees its delivery to the buyer, he has guaranteed what he cannot do, so it is invalid. 2. It may refer to the contract, because it is taken from 'ahd (covenant), and 'ahd and contract are the same. 3. It may refer to the rights of the contract, because they are fruits of the contract. 4. It may refer to the option of stipulation, as in the hadith: 'The 'uhdah of a slave is three days' meaning the option of stipulation in it. 5. It may refer to the defect/recourse (darak). So it is impossible to act upon it before clarification, so the guarantee is invalid, unlike the guarantee of darak, because its guarantee is valid, as it is a guarantee of the price upon entitlement of the sold item, and it is known and possible to deliver. If it is said: It should be directed to what is permissible to guarantee, which is darak, to correct his action. We say: It was not directed to it only because freedom of liability is the default, so obligation cannot be established by doubt and probability. That is what they said.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the invalidity of guaranteeing the 'uhdah (title/responsibility). The author explains that 'uhdah is a mushtarak (homonym) with multiple meanings: it can mean the old document of sale, the contract itself, the rights of the contract, the option of stipulation, or the recourse (darak) for defects. Because the term is ambiguous, when someone guarantees the 'uhdah without specification, it is impossible to know what exactly is being guaranteed. Therefore, the guarantee is invalid (batil). The author contrasts this with guaranteeing the darak (recourse), which is valid because it has a clear meaning: guaranteeing the price if the sold item is found to belong to another. An objection is raised: why not interpret the ambiguous guarantee as a valid darak guarantee to validate the person's action? The response is that freedom of liability (bara'at al-dhimmah) is the default, and obligation cannot be established by doubt and probability. Thus, the ambiguous guarantee cannot be reinterpreted. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that ambiguous terms in contracts require specification, and if not specified, the contract is invalid.

Key Terms
العهدة (al-'uhdah): A homonym that can refer to multiple things: the old document, the contract, rights of contract, option of stipulation, or darak (recourse).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a guarantee of 'uhdah (title guarantee) invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the guarantee is for an impossible thing.
B) Because 'uhdah is a homonym with multiple meanings, so the obligation is unenforceable.
C) Because the guarantee of darak is the only valid form.
D) Because the hadith prohibits it.

Key Takeaway
A guarantee whose subject matter is ambiguous due to the term being a homonym is invalid because obligation cannot be established by doubt; the default is freedom of liability.

Guarantee of Khulas (Release) – An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee of khulas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the guarantee of khulas, explain the interpretive basis of their disagreement, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
A guarantee to 'release' a sold item is invalid if taken literally, but valid as a guarantee of darak (recourse).

In Hanafi law, guarantees (kafalah) must be possible to fulfill. This lesson examines a dispute within the school over the guarantee of 'khulas' (release) – a promise to free a sold item from any claim. The dispute turns on how the term 'khulas' is interpreted, leading to different rulings on validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What exactly is being guaranteed? The term 'khulas' means release, but from what obligation? The guarantee promises to free the sold item from any third-party claim and deliver it to the buyer.
The context is a sale where the buyer fears a latent defect in title.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa holds that such a guarantee is invalid (batil) because it requires the guarantor to deliver the exact item, even if the rightful owner does not consent or if the item is a free person. This is impossible, and committing to an impossible act invalidates the guarantee.
He interprets 'khulas' strictly as physical release.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reinterpretation
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold the guarantee valid (sahih). They reinterpret 'khulas' as a guarantee of darak (recourse): if the guarantor can deliver the item, he must; otherwise, he must return the price. This is within his ability.
They see it as effectively a guarantee of the price, which is well-known and enforceable.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa, such a clause in a contract is void and unenforceable. Under the Sahibayn, the clause is valid, and the guarantor is liable to either deliver the item or compensate the buyer with the price.
The mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn's view.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the Sahibayn, as it is practical and avoids the problem of impossibility by reinterpreting the obligation.
This is endorsed by later Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Marghinani).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الخلاص) : أي إذا ضَمِنَ الخلاص فلا يصح عند أبي حنيفة ، وهو أن يشترط أن المبيع إن استحق يخلصه ويسلّم عينه إليه بأي طريق كان، وهذا باطل إذا لا قدرة له على هذا. وعندهما يصح، وهو محمول على ضمان الدَّرَك.
[١] قوله : عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ والخلاف مبني على تفسيره ، فهمـا فـراه : بتخليص المبيع إن قدر عليه ورد الثمن إن لم يقدر عليه ، وهذا ضمانُ الدَّركِ في المعنى، وفسره الامام : بتخليص المبيع فقط ولا قدرة له. كذا في «النهر». ولو ضمن تخليص المبيع أو ردَّ الثمن جاز ؛ لأنه ضَمِنَ ما يملك الوفاء به، وهو تسليم المبيع إن أجاز المستحق المبيع ورد الثمن إن لم يجز، كذا في «الرمز».
[٢]قوله : إذ لا قدرة له على هذا ؛ لأنه إن ظهر مستحقاً فـ نا فربما لا يساعده المستحق، أو حُرَّاً فلا يقدر مطلقاً، والتزام ما لا يقدر على الوفاء به باطل، كذا في «الكفاية».
Or (the guarantee of) khulas (release): meaning if he guarantees the release, it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. He stipulates that if the sold item is found to be owned by another, he will release it and deliver the exact item by any means. This is invalid because he has no ability for this. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is valid, and it is taken as a guarantee of darak (recourse). [1] The author's saying: According to Abu Hanifa, the disagreement is based on interpretation. They (the Sahibayn) interpret it as: to release the sold item if he can, and to return the price if he cannot, and this is effectively a guarantee of darak. The Imam (Abu Hanifa) interpreted it as releasing the item only, and he has no ability. So in 'al-Nahr'. If he guarantees to release the item or return the price, it is valid because he guaranteed what he can fulfill, which is delivering the item if the rightful owner approves, or returning the price if he does not. As in 'al-Ramz'. [2] The author's saying: He has no ability for this, because if the item is found to be owned by another, perhaps the rightful owner will not assist him, or if it is a free person, he has no ability at all. Committing to what one cannot fulfill is invalid. As in 'al-Kifayah'.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the guarantee of 'khulas' (release). Abu Hanifa holds that such a guarantee is invalid because it requires the guarantor to deliver the exact item free from any claim, which is beyond his ability. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) interpret the guarantee of khulas differently: they see it as a guarantee of darak (recourse), meaning the guarantor is liable to either deliver the item if he can, or return the price if he cannot. Under this interpretation, the guarantee is valid because it is essentially a guarantee of the price against defects. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn, as it practically resolves the issue. The text also notes that if one explicitly guarantees to either deliver the item or return the price, that is clearly valid.

Key Terms
الخلاص (al-khulas): Literally 'release', referring to freeing the sold item from any claim by delivering it to the buyer.
الدرك (al-darak): Recourse or defect liability; the guarantee to refund the price if the sold item is found to be owned by another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) As a guarantee to compensate the buyer with the price only.
B) As a guarantee of darak (recourse) that can be fulfilled by either delivering the item or returning the price.
C) As a guarantee to fight the rightful owner in court.
D) As a guarantee that the seller is free from defects.

Key Takeaway
The guarantee of khulas is valid in Hanafi law when interpreted as a guarantee of darak (recourse), as per the relied-upon position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Invalidity of Mudarib and Agent Guaranteeing the Sale Price
Ruling with rational proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two reasons why a mudarib or agent cannot guarantee the sale price to their principal and apply the principle that a trustee cannot be made a guarantor.

Core Idea
A mudarib or agent is a trustee; a trustee cannot guarantee the trust property.

In this passage, the author examines two cases where a mudarib or an agent attempts to guarantee the sale price to their respective principals. The ruling is clear: such guarantees are invalid. The text provides two distinct rational proofs: one based on the trust status of the price, and the other based on the right to claim. Understanding these proofs is essential for grasping the Hanafi stance on the limits of trusteeship and suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the act
Recognize that the mudarib guarantees the price to the capital owner, and the agent guarantees to the principal. Both involve a sale.
2
State the ruling
The guarantee is not permissible (batil).
3
Extract the first proof
The price is a trust (amanah); the mudarib/agent is a trustee (amin). A trustee cannot be a guarantor because it changes the ruling of Shari'ah.
Analogy to depositary and borrower.
4
Extract the second proof
Suretyship is an undertaking to claim. The right to claim the price belongs to the mudarib/agent themselves, so they would be guaranteeing to pay themselves, which is invalid.
5
Observe the broader principle
Freedom from liability (faraagh al-dhimmah) is the default; obligation cannot be established by doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو المضاربُ الثَّمن لرب المال : أي باعَ المضاربُ، وضَمِنَ الثَّمن لرب المال.
(أو الوكيل بالبيع لموكله ) : أي باغ الوكيل وضَمِنَ للموكل الثَّمن، وإنما لا يجوز قلنا إنمـا لـم يصرف إليه ؛ لأن فراغ الذمة أصل فلا يثبت الشغل بالشك والاحتمال. هكذا قالوا.
[٣] قوله : وإنما لا يجوز...الخ ؛ حاصله إن لعدم جواز الكفالة في هاتين الصورتين وجهين :
أحدهما : إن الثمن أمانة في يد المضارب والوكيل فيكونان أمينين، والأمينُ لا يكون ضامناً ، فالضمان تغيير حكم الشرع، والعبد لا يقدر عليه، فيرد الضمان على كل واحد منهما كاشتراطه على المودع والمستعير.
والثاني : إن الكفالة التزام المطالبة ، وحق المطالبة للمضارب والوكيل، فإن حقَّ القبض إلى الوكيل والمضارب بجهة الأصالة في البيع ؛ ولهذا يبطل الوكالة بموت الموكل وبموت رب المال وبعزله ، ولو وُكُل الموكَّلُ أو ربُّ المال بقبض الدين ثم عزله صح
Or the mudarib (guarantees) the price for the capital owner: meaning the mudarib sold and guaranteed the price to the capital owner. Or the agent for sale (guarantees the price) for his principal: meaning the agent sold and guaranteed the price to the principal. It is not permissible. We say it was not directed to it only because freedom of liability is the default, so obligation cannot be established by doubt and probability. That is what they said. [3] The author's saying: 'and it is not permissible...etc.' Its gist is that the impermissibility of suretyship in these two cases is for two reasons: One: The price is a trust (amanah) in the hand of the mudarib and agent, so they are trustees, and a trustee cannot be a guarantor, because the guarantee changes the ruling of Shari'ah, and one cannot change it, so the guarantee is invalid for each of them, like stipulating it upon the depositary or borrower. The second: Suretyship is an undertaking of claim (litigation), and the right of claim belongs to the mudarib and agent themselves, because the right of receipt belongs to the agent and mudarib by way of original authority in the sale; for this reason the agency becomes void upon the death of the principal and the death of the capital owner and upon dismissal, and if the principal or capital owner were authorized to receive the debt and then dismissed, it would be valid.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage deals with two specific cases: a mudarib (silent partnership manager) guaranteeing the sale price to the capital owner, and an agent (wakil) for sale guaranteeing the sale price to his principal. Both are ruled invalid. The first reason given is that the price in the hands of the mudarib and agent is a trust (amanah); they are trustees (amin), and a trustee cannot be a guarantor for that trust property, as that would change the legal status of the property from trust to liability, which is not permissible. This is analogous to a depositary (mudi') or borrower (musta'ir) who cannot be made a guarantor for the deposited or borrowed item. The second reason is that the right to claim the price belongs to the mudarib and agent themselves, not to the principal, because they have the right to receive the price by virtue of their original authority in the sale. Therefore, if they guarantee the price to the principal, it would mean they are undertaking to claim from themselves, which is nonsensical. The overall principle is that guaranteeing one's own liability is invalid.

Key Terms
المضارب (al-mudarib): The manager in a mudarabah contract who handles the investment and trade with the capital owner's money.
الوكيل (al-wakil): The agent authorized to sell on behalf of the principal.
الأمين (al-amin): A trustee who holds property with permission, not liable for loss unless negligence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is one reason given for the invalidity of a mudarib guaranteeing the sale price to the capital owner?
A) The mudarib does not have the right to sell.
B) The sale price is a trust in the mudarib's hand, and a trustee cannot be a guarantor.
C) The capital owner is not trusted.
D) The mudarib is not competent to guarantee.

Key Takeaway
A mudarib or agent cannot guarantee the sale price because they are trustees and the right to claim belongs to them.

Guarantee by Co-Sellers: Joint vs. Separate Sales
Explains invalidity of guarantee in joint sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a co-seller's guarantee of his partner's share of the price and distinguish when it is valid based on the nature of the sale transaction.

Core Idea
A co-seller's guarantee of a joint debt is invalid because it leads to self-guarantee and circular recourse.

When two people jointly own an item and sell it, one might want to guarantee his partner's share of the payment. But is that allowed? The Hanafi school says it depends on how they sold it. In this lesson, we will explore the reasoning behind the invalidity of such guarantee in a single transaction and the valid scenario when they sell separately, along with a nuanced debate within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
We have two co-sellers (say A and B) who jointly own a slave and sell it to a buyer. One of them (A) guarantees his partner B's share of the price.
2
Determine the type of sale
Was the sale in one transaction (safqah wahidah) or two separate transactions (safqatayn)? This distinction is crucial.
3
Apply the principle: price is a trust
Before the buyer pays, the price is considered a trust (amanah) held by the seller who is acting as agent (wakil) or mudarib. Guaranteeing it would change the legal ruling.
4
Note the exception: voluntary payment
If A pays B's share without a prior guarantee, it is valid but A cannot seek reimbursement from B. With a guarantee, A can seek recourse but the guarantee is void.
5
Recognize the intra-madhhab dispute
Abu Hanifa requires repeating the word 'bitt' (I sold) to create separate transactions; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider detailing the price sufficient. The relied-upon view is that of the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو أحد البائعين حصة صاحبه من ثمن عبد باعاه بصفقة بطل .. لأنَّ الثَّمن أمانةٌ عند المضارب والوكيل، فالضمان تغيير حكم الشرع. .. ولأنَّ حق المطالبة للمضارب والوكيل، فيصيران ضامنين لنفسهما . [١] قوله : بصفقتين صح ؛ صورته : أن رجلين باعَ كل واحدٍ منهما نصفه بعقد على حدة ، ثم ضمن أحدهما لصاحبه حصته من الثمن صح الضمان. [٢] قوله : يصير ضامناً ؛ إذ ما من جزء يؤديه المشتري أو الكفيل من الثمن الأول لشريكه فيه نصيب. [٣] قوله : وذا لا يجوز؛ لأن القسمة عبارة عن الإفراز والحيازة، وهو أن يصير حق كل واحد منهما مفرزاً في حيز على حدة، فهو لا يتصور في غير العين ؛ لأن الفعل الحسي يستدعي محلاً حسياً، والدين حكمي، فلا يرد عليه الفصل الحسي. فإن لم تصح قسمته يكون كل شيءٍ يؤديه إلى شريكه مشتركاً بينهما، فيرجعُ المؤدي بنصف ما أدى لكونه مشتركاً بينهما، ثم يرجع أيضاً بنصف الباقي إلى أن لا يبقى في يده شيء، فيؤدي تجويزه ابتداءً إبطاله انتهاء، كذا في «التبيين» (٢). Or one of the two sellers (guarantees) his partner's share of the price of a slave they sold in one transaction: it is invalid... because the price is a trust with the mudarib and the agent, so the guarantee is a change of the ruling of the Shariah. .. and because the right of claim belongs to the mudarib and the agent, so they become guarantors for themselves. (Or one of the two sellers his partner's share from the price of a slave they sold in one transaction: it is invalid because the price became due to the agent or the mudarib from the buyer, since the rights of the contract revert to the contractor. And likewise, the mudarib, if the owner of capital deputized him to receive the price, then he (the owner) has removed him because he (the mudarib) is the contractor, so rights revert to him; and the contractor for another in matters of rights is like the contractor for himself; and hence the claim is specific to him. And if the buyer swore that the principal does not have anything, he would be truthful in his oath; and if he swore that the agent does not have anything, he would be false. So when it is established that the agent is original in receiving, then if he guarantees, he becomes guarantor for himself, so it is not permissible. [1] His statement: 'they become guarantors'; unlike the messenger and the agent selling spoils from the imam and the marriage agent, where their guarantee is valid, because each of them is an intermediary and a conduit, so much so that if the commander forbids them from receiving the substitute, his prohibition is valid. [2] And his statement: 'or one of the two sellers...etc.'; its example: two men sold a jointly owned slave to a man in one transaction, and one of them guaranteed his partner's share of the price: it is not permissible. Yes, if he paid his partner's share without a prior guarantee, it is permissible, and he does not have recourse for what he paid, unlike the case of guarantee, for then he has recourse for what he paid because he paid it based on a void (guarantee). Thus it is mentioned in Radd al-Mukhtar, quoting from Jami' al-Fusulayn. And likewise, the kafala of each partner for a jointly owned debt is absolutely invalid, even if by inheritance. For example: two men have a debt against a man, and one becomes a surety for his partner for his share of the debt: his suretyship is not valid. And if one gratuitously pays his partner's share of the debt, it is valid. Also, if a man dies and has a debt against a man, and leaves two sons, and one becomes a surety for his brother against the debtor for his brother's share: the suretyship is not valid. And if one of them gratuitously pays his brother's share of the debt, it is valid. Thus in al-Khaniyya and others. [1] His statement: 'and with two transactions, it is valid'; its example: two men each sold half of it by a separate contract, then one guaranteed his partner's share of the price: the guarantee is valid. [2] His statement: 'he becomes guarantor'; because there is no part that the buyer or the surety pays from the first price that does not have a share for his partner. [3] His statement: 'and that is not permissible'; because division (qisma) means segregation and possession, i.e., that the right of each becomes segregated in a separate space, so it is not conceivable in other than a tangible thing, because a physical act requires a physical locus, and debt is a legal construct, so physical separation does not apply to it. And if its division is not valid, then everything he pays to his partner is jointly owned between them, so the payer takes recourse for half of what he paid because it is jointly owned, then he also takes recourse for half of the remaining, until nothing remains in his hand, so allowing it initially leads to its nullification in the end. Thus in al-Tabyeen. [4] His statement: 'with two transactions'; they have considered here for the multiplicity of the transaction the detailing of the price, and they mentioned in the chapter of sales that this is the view of the Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). As for his view (Abu Hanifa), the word 'I sold' must be repeated. Thus in al-Bahr and others. [5] His statement: 'because there is no joint ownership'; because when the transaction is multiple, what becomes due to each by his contract belongs specifically to him without joint ownership. [6] His statement: 'kharaj'; it is said what is meant by it is the imposed kharaj, which is obligatory in the dhimmah by ...
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a guarantee (daman) by one of two co-sellers who jointly own a slave and sell it. The matn states: if they sell in a single transaction and one guarantees his partner's share of the price, the guarantee is invalid. The commentary explains that the price, before delivery, is an amanah (trust) with the agent (wakil) or mudarib, so guaranteeing it would change the legal ruling. Moreover, the right to claim payment belongs to the agent/mudarib, so they are effectively guaranteeing for themselves, which is invalid. The commentary distinguishes this from a mere messenger (rasul) or an agent selling spoils or a marriage agent, where guarantee is valid because they are mere intermediaries. The example is given: two men sell a jointly owned slave in one transaction; one guarantees his partner's share – invalid. However, if he pays without prior guarantee, it is valid but he has no recourse. The same applies to kafala (suretyship) between co-creditors of a debt or heirs of a debt: kafala is invalid, but voluntary payment is valid. Then the passage explains the second part: if they sell in two separate transactions, the guarantee is valid because there is no joint ownership in the price – each seller's share is separate. The reasoning is given: in the single transaction, every part of the price is owned jointly, so the guarantor would be paying himself and then having endless recourse, leading to circularity. The text also notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), separation of price suffices for multiplicity of transaction, but according to Abu Hanifa, the word 'bitt' must be repeated. The relied-upon view is that of the Sahibayn for practical convenience. Finally, a comparison is made with guarantee of kharaj (land tax), which is valid because it is a debt on the peasant.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): A contract of guarantee where a person becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another.
صفقة (safqah): A transaction or bargain, particularly the striking of hands to conclude a sale. Here it refers to a single contract of sale.
عاقد (ʿāqid): The person who contracts; here, the agent or mudarib who is the direct party in the sale.
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where a person becomes responsible for the debt or obligation of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men jointly own a slave and sell it to Zaid in a single transaction. One of them guarantees his partner's share of the price. What is the ruling?
A) Valid, because it is a separate contract of guarantee.
B) Invalid, because the price is jointly owned.
C) Valid only if the partner agrees.
D) Invalid only if the buyer objects.

Key Takeaway
A co-seller's guarantee is invalid when the sale is in a single transaction due to joint ownership of the debt; it is valid only when the sale is in separate transactions, and the relied-upon view allows detailing the price to achieve separation.

Guarantee of Fixed vs. Share-Cropping Kharaj
Ruling on the validity of guaranteeing land tax and similar obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fixed kharaj and share-cropping kharaj and state which type can be guaranteed according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Only the fixed kharaj (a debt) can be guaranteed; the share-cropping kharaj (a physical share) cannot.

In this passage, we examine a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh regarding the validity of guaranteeing land tax (kharaj). While the general rule allows guarantee for debts, not all forms of kharaj are considered debts. The commentator clarifies the relied-upon position and provides the reasoning, including an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute in the text
The matn states that the guarantee of kharaj is valid absolutely, but later scholars restrict it. Recognize the tension between the absolute wording and the restricted position.
The author of al-Kanz gave it absolutely; al-Bahr included both types.
2
Examine the proof for fixed kharaj
The fixed kharaj is a debt in the dhimma, demanded and enforced by imprisonment. Therefore, guaranteeing it is like guaranteeing any other debt, which is permissible by consensus.
The reasoning is qiyas to other debts.
3
Examine the proof against share-cropping kharaj
Share-cropping kharaj is a physical portion of the crop and is not guaranteed (if destroyed, no compensation). Guaranteeing a non-guaranteed physical asset is invalid, analogous to zakat on apparent wealth.
This analogy strengthens the restriction.
4
Identify the tarjih
The authors of al-Fath and al-Kifayah restrict the guarantee to fixed kharaj, and this is the mufta bihi position. The commentator sides with this view, noting that the absolute wording was too general.
Tarjih is based on the strength of the dalil.

Source Text

أمَّا الخراج فقد مرَّ .
وظاهر المتن أن المعتمد الإطلاق، ومن ثم أطلقه صاحب «الكنز»، وقال في «البحر» : أطلقه فشمل الخراج الموظف وخراج المقاسمة ؛ لأنه لم يكن ديناً في الذمة، والرهن كالكفالة بجامع التوثيق، فيجوز في كل موضع تجوز الكفالة فيه. كذا ذكره منقوض بالدَّرَك ، فإن الكفالة به جائزة دون الرهن. انتهى.
واستدلوا على صحة ضمان الخراج بأنه دين مضمون حقاً للعبد يطالب بـه ويحبس، فصار ضمانه كسائر الديون، وهذا التعليل اعتمدوه جميعاً، فيدل على اختصاص الخراج المضمون بالموظف.
أما خراج المقاسمة فجزء من الخراج، وهو عين غير مضمون حتى لو هلك لا يوخذ شيء، والكفالة بأعيان غير مضمونة لا تجوز كالزكاة في الأموال الظاهرة ؛ ولهذا قيد الخراج المضمون بالموظف صاحب «الفتح» ، وقال صاحب «الكفاية» : المراد به الخراج الموظف.
[١] قوله : النوائب ؛ هو جمع نائبة وهي النازلة كذا في «المصباح»، وقال في «الأرب» نائبة مصيبت و كار وشوار نوائب .جمع. انتهى. وفي الاصطلاح ما أشار إليه الشارح الله بقوله : فهي إما بالحقِّ ... الخ.
And the nawaa'ib (incidental taxes) and qismah (division): i.e., the guarantee of these things is valid.
As for kharaj, it has passed.
The apparent meaning of the text is that the absolute is relied upon, hence the author of al-Kanz gave it absolutely, and the author of al-Bahr said: he gave it absolutely so it includes the fixed kharaj and the share-cropping kharaj, because it is not a debt in the dhimmah, and rahn (pledge) is like kafalah due to the common element of tawthiq (securing), so it is permissible in every case where kafalah is permissible. This was mentioned as being contradicted by al-darak (seller's guarantee of title), for kafalah for it is permissible but not rahn. End.
They adduced as proof for the validity of guaranteeing kharaj that it is a guaranteed debt, a right of the Treasury, demanded and imprisoned for, so its guarantee is like other debts. This reasoning they all relied upon, indicating that the guaranteed kharaj is specific to the fixed one.
As for share-cropping kharaj, it is a part of the produce, a physical item not guaranteed, so if it is destroyed nothing is taken, and kafalah for non-guaranteed physical items is not permissible, like zakat on apparent wealth. Hence the author of al-Fath restricted the guaranteed kharaj to the fixed one, and the author of al-Kifayah said: what is meant is the fixed kharaj.
[1] The commentator's statement: 'nawaa'ib' is the plural of naa'ibah, which means a calamity, as in al-Misbah. In terminology, it refers to what the commentator indicated by his saying: 'they are either by right... etc.'
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of guaranteeing (kafalah) for kharaj (land tax), nawaa'ib (incidental taxes), and qismah (division of assets). The main ruling is that such guarantees are valid, but with a crucial distinction for kharaj. The commentator explains that the relied-upon view is that the guarantee applies to both types of kharaj: the fixed kharaj (kharaj muwazzaf) which is a debt in the dhimma, and the share-cropping kharaj (kharaj al-muqasamah) which is a share of the produce. However, the majority of scholars restrict the validity to the fixed kharaj only. The reasoning is that the fixed kharaj is a debt owed by the landowner, so it is like any other debt and can be guaranteed. The share-cropping kharaj is a physical portion of the crop and is not a debt; it is not guaranteed (if destroyed, no compensation), and guaranteeing a non-guaranteed physical item is invalid, similar to the case of zakat on visible wealth. Therefore, the correct view is that only the fixed kharaj can be guaranteed. The passage also defines 'nawaa'ib' as calamities or incidents that occur.

Key Terms
خراج موظف (kharaj muwazzaf): Fixed land tax imposed annually on land, which becomes a debt in the dhimma of the landowner.
خراج مقاسمة (kharaj muqasamah): Share-cropping tax where a portion of the crop is taken; it is a physical share and not a debt.
نائبة (naa'ibah): Calamity or incidental event that may impose a financial obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of kharaj can be guaranteed according to the relied-upon opinion?
A) Both fixed and share-cropping kharaj.
B) Only the fixed kharaj (al-muwazzaf).
C) Only the share-cropping kharaj (al-muqasamah).
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The guarantee of kharaj is valid only when it is a fixed debt; share-cropping kharaj is a physical share and cannot be guaranteed.
