Suretyship for Kharaj: Fixed vs Proportional Land Tax
Explains validity of kafalah for kharaj types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between kharaj muwazzaf and kharaj muqasamah and state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah for each.

Core Idea
Kafalah is valid only for guaranteed debts; kharaj muwazzaf qualifies, kharaj muqasamah does not.

This passage refines the general ruling on suretyship for land tax, distinguishing between two types: a fixed annual tax (muwazzaf) and a proportional share of the harvest (muqasamah). The Hanafi school holds that only the fixed tax is a guaranteed debt, making kafalah valid; the proportional tax is unguaranteed property, rendering kafalah invalid. We will analyze the proof and the resulting operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Kafalah (suretyship) for kharaj (land tax). The ruling is that it is valid for muwazzaf and invalid for muqasamah.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an analogy (qiyas): kharaj muwazzaf is a guaranteed debt, similar to other debts for which kafalah is valid by consensus.
Dalil text: 'واستدلوا على صحة ضمان الخراج بأنه دين مضمون حقاً للعبد يطالب به ويحبس'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The fixed kharaj is a debt in the dhimma, demanded and enforced by imprisonment, thus it shares the effective cause (illa) with other guaranteed debts.
This reasoning applies only to muwazzaf, not muqasamah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text initially appears absolute, allowing kafalah for both types. Later authorities (e.g., Ṣāḥib al-Fatḥ, Ṣāḥib al-Kifāyah) restrict to muwazzaf.
Cross-madhhab: other schools may differ on the nature of kharaj.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the restricted view: kafalah valid only for muwazzaf, because muqasamah is an unguaranteed property.
Tarjih authority: Ṣāḥib al-Fatḥ and Ṣāḥib al-Kifāyah.

As for the kharaj, it has passed.
The imam imposes every year on property according to his opinion to extract al-muqasamah, which is what the imam divides from the yield of the land because it is not an obligation in the dhimma. Thus in al-Ramz and others.
The apparent of the text is that the relied-upon is the absolute, and thus its author of al-Kanz made it absolute, and he said in al-Bahr: He made it absolute, so it includes al-kharaj al-muwazzaf and kharaj al-muqasamah because it was not a debt in the dhimma, and the pledge is like suretyship due to the commonality of documentation, so it is permissible in every place where suretyship is permissible. Thus it was mentioned as refuted by al-darak, for suretyship for it is permissible without the pledge (the commentator means al-Zayla'i). End.
And they adduced proof for the validity of suretyship for kharaj that it is a debt guaranteed as a right of the subject that is demanded for and imprisoned for, so its suretyship becomes like other debts, and this reasoning they all relied upon, so it indicates the specificity of the guaranteed kharaj to the fixed (land tax).
As for kharaj al-muqasamah, it is a part of the kharaj, and it is a non-guaranteed real property such that if it perishes, nothing is taken, and suretyship for non-guaranteed real properties is not permissible, like zakat on apparent properties. Therefore the author of al-Fath restricted the guaranteed kharaj to the fixed, and the author of al-Kifayah said: what is intended by it is al-kharaj al-muwazzaf.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding suretyship (kafalah) for kharaj (land tax). It first clarifies that kafalah for kharaj is valid. The discussion distinguishes two types: kharaj muwazzaf (fixed annual tax) and kharaj muqasamah (proportional tax based on yield). Initially, the text appears absolute, allowing kafalah for both. However, a detailed analysis shows that the fixed kharaj is a guaranteed debt (dayn madhmūn) in the dhimma, demanded and enforced by imprisonment, thus kafalah is valid by analogy to other debts. In contrast, the proportional kharaj is an unguaranteed property (ʿayn ghayr madhmūn); if the crop perishes, nothing is taken. Kafalah for such properties is invalid, analogous to zakat on apparent wealth (al-amwāl al-ẓāhirah), which is also not guaranteed. Therefore, later authorities like Ṣāḥib al-Fatḥ and Ṣāḥib al-Kifāyah restrict the validity of kafalah for kharaj to the fixed type (al-muwazzaf). The proof (dalīl) is that the fixed kharaj meets the criteria of a guaranteed debt, while the proportional does not.

Key Terms
الخراج الموظف (al-kharaj al-muwazzaf): A fixed annual land tax imposed by the imam on land, considered a guaranteed debt in the dhimma.
خراج المقاسمة (kharaj al-muqasamah): A proportional land tax based on the crop yield, which is not a debt but a share of the produce.
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; joining one's liability to another in a demand or debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is not a debt in the dhimma but an unguaranteed property.
B) Because the imam has not authorized it.
C) Because it is less than a fixed amount.
D) Because it is forbidden by the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafalah for land tax depends on whether the tax is a fixed debt (muwazzaf) or a proportional share (muqasamah); only the former is a guaranteed debt, making kafalah valid.

Suretyship for Lawful and Unlawful Levies (Nawa'ib)
This passage defines nawa'ib and presents two Hanafi views on suretyship for unlawful levies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on suretyship for unlawful levies and identify the reliance on tangible demand as the basis for validity.

Core Idea
Suretyship for unlawful nawa'ib is valid based on tangible demand, not the lawfulness of the underlying obligation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a debated issue: can one be a surety for someone who owes unlawful exactions (nawa'ib) imposed by oppressors? While lawful public levies are uncontroversial, the status of unlawful levies splits the Hanafi scholars. We will explore the reasoning of Sadr al-Islam and Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, and understand why the tangible demand is considered sufficient for suretyship.

Guided Path
1
Define nawa'ib
Explain the linguistic and technical meaning: nawa'ib are levies imposed on the public, either lawful or unlawful.
The passage uses the term for exactions, not just calamities.
2
Distinguish lawful and unlawful
Identify examples from the text: lawful (clearing a shared river, hiring guards, equipping armies) vs unlawful (tyrannical exactions by oppressors).
3
State the consensus on lawful
Note that kafalah for lawful nawa'ib is unanimously valid because it is a guaranteed obligation (wajib madhmūn).
4
Analyze the dispute on unlawful
Present Sadr al-Islam's view (invalid: no lawful demand or debt) and Fakhr al-Islam's view (valid: tangible demand suffices).
The text favors the latter, using the principle that the tangible demand is like the lawful demand.
5
Derive the practical consequence
If valid, the surety can be held liable and the payer can recourse if not coerced. If invalid, no liability.
The passage also notes that if the payer was coerced, recourse is not allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱] قوله : النوائب ؛ هو جمع نائبة وهي النازلة كذا في «المصباح»، وقال في «الأرب» : نائبة مصيبت و کار وشوار نوائب. انتهى. وفي الاصطلاح ما أشار إليه الشارح بقوله : فهي إما بالحقِّ ... الخ.

وأمَّا النَّوائبُ : فهي إما بحق ككري النَّهْرِ، وأجر الحارس، وما يُوظف لتجهيز الجيش، وغير ذلك.
(1) قوله : أما النوائب... الخ ؛ حاصله أن النوائب :
١. قيل : أراد بها ما يكون بحق كأجرة لحارس، وكري النهر المشترك، أو المال الموظف : كتجهيز الجيش وفداء الأسرى.
٢. وقيل : المراد بها ما ليس بحق كالجبايات التي في زماننا يأخذها الظلمة بغير حق. فإن كان المراد به هو الأوّل جازت الكفالة بها اتفاقاً ؛ لأنه واجب مضمون. وإن كان المراد به الثاني، ففيه اختلاف المشايخ :
١. فقال بعضُهم : لا تجوز الكفالة، ومنهم صدر الإسلام البَزْدَوِي له ؛ لأنها ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة أو الدين، وهاهنا لا مطالبة ولا دين شرعيان على الأصيل فلم يتحقق معناها.
٢. وقال بعضهم : تجوز ؛ منهم فخرُ الإسلام البزدويه أخو صدر الإسلام المتقدم ؛ لأنها في المطالبة مثل سائر الديون، بل فوقها، والعبرة للمطالبة ؛ لأنها شرعت لالتزامهما، والمطالبة الحسيّة كالمطالبة الشرعيّة؛ ولذا قلنا من قام بتوزيع هذه النوائب على المسلمين بالقسط : أي العدل يؤجر وإن كان الآخذ بالأخذ ظالم. وقلنا : من قضى نائبة غيره بأمره رجع عليه، وإن لم يشترط الرجوع وهو الصحيح، كما في «الخانية» كمن قضى دين غيره بأمره. وفي «العنايه» : قال شمس الأئمة : هذا إذا أمره به لا عن إكراه؛ أما إذا كان مكرهاً في الأمر فلا يعتبر أمره بالرجوع.
[۲] قوله : ككري النهر..الخ ؛ نحو أن يقضي القاضي بكري نهر مشترك بينه وبين غيره شركة خاصةً، فأبى واحد منهم من الكري، وأنفق شريكه بأمر القاضي، يصير حصته الآبي ديناً في ذمّته، فتصح الكفالة بها ؛ لأنه كفل بما هو مضمون على الأصيل.
[1] His statement: 'al-nawa'ib'; it is the plural of na'ibah, which is the calamity, as in al-Misbah, and he said in al-Arba: na'ibah is a calamity and work and troubles. End. In terminology, what the commentator indicated by his statement: 'they are either by right...'

And the nawa'ib are either by right, like clearing the river, and the hire of the guard, and what is imposed to equip the army, and other than that.
(1) His statement: 'As for the nawa'ib...'; summary of that: the nawa'ib are:
1. It was said: what is intended by them is what is by right, like the hire of a guard, and clearing the shared river, or the money imposed: like equipping the army and ransoming captives.
2. And it was said: what is intended by them is what is not by right, like the exactions that in our time the oppressors take without right. If what is intended is the first, suretyship for them is permissible by agreement because it is a guaranteed obligation. And if what is intended is the second, then there is a difference among the mashayikh:
   a. Some said: suretyship is not permissible, among them Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, because it is joining a dhimma to a dhimma in demand or debt, and here there is no lawful demand or debt on the principal, so its meaning is not realized.
   b. Some said: it is permissible, among them Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, the brother of the aforementioned Sadr al-Islam, because it is in demand like other debts, even above them, and the consideration is the demand, because it (i.e., suretyship) was legislated to undertake them, and the tangible demand is like the lawful demand. Hence we said: whoever undertakes to distribute these nawa'ib among the Muslims with equity (i.e., justice) is rewarded, even if the taker by taking is an oppressor. And we said: whoever pays the nawa'ib of another by his order can recourse upon him, even if he did not stipulate recourse, and this is the correct, as in al-Khaniyah, like one who pays the debt of another by his order. And in al-Inayah: Shams al-A'imma said: this is if he ordered him without duress. But if he was coerced in the order, then his order for recourse is not considered.
[2] His statement: 'like clearing the river...'; for example, that the judge rules to clear a river that is common property between him and another as a special partnership, and one of them refuses from the clearing, and his partner spends by order of the judge, then the share of the refuser becomes a debt in his dhimma, so suretyship for it is valid, because he guaranteed what is guaranteed upon the principal.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage defines nawa'ib (sing. na'ibah), which linguistically means calamities. In legal terminology, they refer to levies that can be either lawful (bi al-haqq) or unlawful (ghayr bi al-haqq). Lawful nawa'ib include contributions for public works like cleaning a shared river, hiring guards, equipping armies, and ransoming captives. According to the text, suretyship (kafalah) for these is unanimously valid because they are guaranteed obligations (wajib madhmūn). For unlawful nawa'ib—tyrannical exactions by oppressors—there is a dispute among the mashāyikh (Hanafi scholars). Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi holds that kafalah is invalid because there is no lawful demand or debt (mutālabah or dayn sharʿī) on the principal (aṣīl). His brother, Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, counters that kafalah is valid based on the tangible demand (al-muṭālabah al-ḥissiyyah). Since kafalah was legislated for the purpose of assuming liability, the actual demand suffices. Thus, one who pays such an impost by order can recourse, similar to paying a debt by order. However, if the payer was coerced, recourse is not allowed. The example of clearing a river illustrates a lawful nawa'ib: if a judge orders the clearing of a shared river and one partner refuses, the expenditure of the other partner becomes a debt on the refuser, and kafalah for that debt is valid.

Key Terms
النوائب (al-nawa'ib): Plural of na'ibah; in legal terminology, levies imposed on the public, either lawful (like public works) or unlawful (tyrannical exactions).
المطالبة الحسيّة (al-muṭālabah al-ḥissiyyah): Tangible or actual demand, as opposed to a lawful demand (sharʿiyyah). Used by Fakhr al-Islam to justify kafalah for unlawful exactions.
صدر الإسلام (Ṣadr al-Islām): Title for the early Hanafi scholar al-Bazdawi (the elder), who held kafalah for unlawful nawa'ib invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, why is suretyship for unlawful nawa'ib valid?
A) Because the underlying obligation is lawful.
B) Because the tangible demand (muṭālabah ḥissiyyah) is sufficient, as kafalah is based on demand.
C) Because the oppressor has the right to collect.
D) Because it is a form of charity.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for unlawful levies is valid in the Hanafi school because the tangible demand creates a liability, regardless of the lawfulness of the underlying exaction.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Surety for Unlawful Levies: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on surety for unlawful levies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on surety for unlawful extraordinary levies, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Surety for unlawful levies is valid by fatwa because demand is real, but the cursed miks remains absolutely forbidden.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: is surety (kafalah) valid for extraordinary levies that are themselves unlawful? While lawful levies are uncontroversial, the imposition of unjust taxes by rulers forces the question of whether a guarantor can be bound. Two great Hanafi scholars, Sadr al-Islam and Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, disagreed, and later authorities settled the fatwa on validity.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish lawful and unlawful nawa'ib
Read the opening distinction: lawful levies (bi haqq) like river dredging, guard wages, army equipment; unlawful levies (bi ghayr haqq) like oppressive exactions.
The passage then splits the ruling accordingly.
2
State the consensus on lawful levies
For lawful nawa'ib, surety is valid by agreement (ittifaq) because they are guaranteed obligations (wajib madmun).
3
Describe the ikhtilaf on unlawful levies
Some shuyukh (e.g., Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi) disallowed surety; others (e.g., his brother Fakhr al-Islam) allowed it. Identify each scholar's core argument.
Sadr al-Islam: no lawful debt/demand on principal. Fakhr al-Islam: demand exists physically; surety is for demand.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
What practical difference does each view produce? If disallowed, the guarantee is void; if allowed, the surety can recover from the principal if he paid by order.
The fatwa follows validity.
5
Note the absolute prohibition of miks
The passage strongly warns that the miks (market toll) is haram and cannot be guaranteed at all.
This is an exception even within the permissive view.

Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses the ruling of surety (kafalah) for extraordinary levies known as nawa'ib. The author first distinguishes two types of nawa'ib: those that are lawful (bi haqq) such as the costs of dredging a shared river, wages of a guard, or funds for equipping an army and ransoming captives; and those that are unlawful (bi ghayr haqq) such as the extortionate taxes taken by oppressors in the author's time. For lawful nawa'ib, there is consensus (ittifaq) that surety is valid because they are guaranteed obligations (wajib madmun). For unlawful nawa'ib, there is ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars. Sadr al-Islam al-Bazdawi argues that surety is not valid because surety entails adding one liability to another for a demand or debt, and here there is no lawful demand or debt on the principal. His brother Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi argues that surety is valid because the focus is on the demand (mutalabah) itself, which exists physically even if not lawfully; surety is legislated for undertaking such demands. The commentary then provides examples and cites authorities. The author reports that the fatwa (operative legal opinion) is on validity (sihhah) for unlawful nawa'ib, as they have become like valid debts in practice. However, there is a strong warning against the miks (illegal market tax), which is absolutely forbidden and cannot be guaranteed. The passage ends with a note that the correct view is validity, and the surety can recover from the principal if he paid by the principal's order.

Key Terms
نائبة (nā'iba): An extraordinary levy or impost, either lawful (like communal expenses) or unlawful (like extortionate taxes).
كفالة (kafāla): Surety; joining one's liability to another for a demand or debt.
مكس (maks): An illegal tax or toll collected by oppressors, considered haram and cursed by the Prophet.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fatwa position regarding surety (kafalah) for unlawful extraordinary levies (nawa'ib bi ghayr haqq)?
A) It is invalid by consensus.
B) It is valid, and the surety can recover from the principal if ordered.
C) It is valid only if the levy is for the army.
D) It is invalid because the debt is unlawful.

Key Takeaway
Surety for unlawful extraordinary levies (except miks) is valid by Hanafi fatwa, with recourse to the principal if paid by his order.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Validity of Kafalah for Unjust Levies and the Meaning of Qismah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on suretyship for unjust levies and interpretation of qismah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on suretyship for unjust taxes and analyze the differing interpretations of 'qismah' in this context.

Core Idea
Suretyship for unjust levies is valid in fatwa because they become like debts, but the levy itself remains haram.

This passage explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: whether one can validly act as surety for an unjust tax (nawa'ib) imposed on someone. While the tax itself is sinful, the later Hanafi fatwa considers suretyship valid, treating the levy like a debt. The passage also clarifies the contested meaning of 'qismah' in this context, affecting the scope of the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of nawa'ib
Identify the first type (by right, unanimous validity) and the second type (without right, disputed). Understand the examples given: jibayat in Persia are unjust, while army equipment levies are valid.
The first type is obligatory on wealthy Muslims for public benefit.
2
Analyze the reasoning for validity of the disputed type
Though the tax is unjust, custom has made it like a valid debt. The surety can be compelled to pay and has recourse. The Lawgiver's curse on maks (bribe) is distinguished from these levies.
This is a later fatwa, not from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Examine the opposing view and its proof
Some scholars (e.g., author of al-Khaniyyah) hold it invalid based on the general principle that suretyship for non-obligatory things is invalid, analogizing to a captive who pays for release and cannot recover.
This view is attributed to the majority of early scholars.
4
Understand the resolution (tarjih)
The fatwa is on validity, as stated in al-Bahr and Idah al-Islah. The captive analogy is distinguished because the payor in that case acted voluntarily, while the surety acts under compulsion of custom.
The mufta bihi is validity, though some later authorities prefer the opposite.
5
Explore the interpretations of 'qismah'
The scholars differ: qismah may mean the same as nawa'ib, a portion, a fixed regular tax, or division among partners. Regardless, suretyship for it is valid.
The conjunction 'aw' vs 'waw' affects meaning.

وأما بغير حق : كالجبايات في زماننا، والكفالة بالأولى" صحيحة اتفاقاً ، وفي الثانية خلافٌ، والفتوى على الصحةِ ، فإنَّها صارت كالديون الصحيحة حتى لو أخذت من الأكار فله الرجوع على مالك الأرض. وكذا أجرة الحارس بين قوم مضمونة يصح الضمان بها، وكذا ما وظف الإمام على الناس عند الحاجة إلى تجهيز الجيش لقتال المشركين، وقد خلا بيت المال، أو يحتاج إلى حذاء أسارى المسلمين فوظف على الناس لأجل ذلك، فهو واجب مضمون تصح الكفالة به ، كذا في «العناية)، وغيرها. ١ ] قوله : كالجبايات ; الموظفة على الناس في زماننا ببلاد فارس على الخياط والصبّاغ وغيرهم للسلطان في كل يوم أو شهر أو ثلاثة أشهر، فإنّها ظلم. كذا في «الفتح») وقال في «البناية»(۳) : وأما النوائب الكبرى والداهية الدهياء التي هي المكس فهي حرام قطعاً فلا يجوز الكفالة بها ولا التصرُّف فيها بوجه من الوجوه أصلاً، وقد لعن الشارع صاحب المكس. انتهى. [٢] قوله : والكفالة بالأولى ; أي بالنوائب إذا أريد بها ما يكون بحق صحيحة اتفاقاً؛ لأنها واجبة على كل مسلم موسر بإيجاب طاعة ولي الأمر فيما فيه مصلحة المسلمين ولم يلزم بيت المال، أو لزمه ولا شيء فيه، كذا في «الفتح». [٣] قوله : والفتوى على الصحة ; قال في «البحر ) : ظاهر كلامهم ترجيح الصحة في كفالة النوائب بغير حق ; ولذا قال في ايضاح الاصلاح : والفتوى على الصحة. وفي «الخانية»: الصحيح الصحة ويرجع على المكفول عنه إن كان بأمره. انتهى. وأما القسمة فقد قيل : هي النَّوائبُ بعينها، أو الحصة منها، وقيل: هي النائبة الموظفة الراتبةُ ، والتَّوائبُ هي غيرُ الموظفة، وأيا ما كان، فالكفالة بها صحيحة. (وإن قال : ضمنته إلى شهر صُدَّقَ هو وإن ادعى الطالبُ أنَّه حال) وعليه مشى في الاختيار» (١) ، و«المختار (۲) و «الملتقى (٣) ، نعم صحح صاحب و«الملتقى»(۳) «الخانية» في شرحه على «الجامع الصغير) عدم الصحة، وكذلك أفتى في «الخيرية» بعدم الصحة مستنداً لما في «البزازية (4) ، و«الخلاصة» من أنه قول عامة المشايخ، ولما في «العمادية» من أن الأسير لو قال لغيره خلصني فدفع المأمور مالاً وخلصه قال السَّرَخْسِي يرجعُ، وقال صاحب «المحيط»: لا ; وهو الأصح وعليه الفتوى. انتهى مختصراً. والله أعلم وعلمه أتقن وأحكم. 11) قوله فقد قيل ... الخ ; حاصله إن المشايخ اختلفوا في معناها : ١. فقال بعضُهم : هي النوائب بعينها، فالعطف للبيان والتفسير، وتكون الرواية بالواو ، أو حصة منها بأن قسَّم ما ينوب العامة ، نحو: مؤنة كري الأنهار المشتركة، فأصاب واحداً شيء من ذلك، فيجب أداؤه فكفل به رجلٌ. ٢. وقال بعضُهم :القسمة : أي النائبةُ الموظفة المقرّرة من عند الإمام، وهي المقاطعات الديوانية في كلّ شهر أو ثلاثة أشهر، والمراد بالنوائب ما هو منها غير راتب فتغايرا ، فالرواية في هاتين : بأو. . وقال بعضُهم معناها : إذا اقتسما ثم منع أحد الشريكين قسم صاحبه. ٤. وقال أبو جعفر الهندواني الله معناها : إن أحد الشريكين إذا طلب القسمة من صاحبه وامتنع الآخر عن ذلك فضمن إنسان ليقوم مقامه في القسمه جاز؛ لأن القسمة واجبة عليه.
As for without right: like the taxes in our time, and the suretyship by the first is valid unanimously, and regarding the second there is disagreement, and the fatwa is on its validity, for they have become like valid debts, so if it is taken from the cultivator, he has recourse against the landowner. Likewise, the wages of a guard among a group are guaranteed, and suretyship for it is valid. Likewise, what the ruler imposes on people when there is a need to equip the army to fight the polytheists, and the treasury is empty, or there is a need for sandals of Muslim captives, so he imposes on people for that, it is obligatory and guaranteed, and suretyship for it is valid. So in al-'Inayah and others.
1. His saying: 'like the taxes': the ones imposed on people in our time in the land of Persia on the tailor, the dyer, and others for the sultan every day or month or three months, for they are oppression. So in al-Fath. And he said in al-Binayah: As for the great calamities and the terrible disaster which is the bribe (maks), it is absolutely forbidden, so suretyship for it is not permissible, nor dealing with it in any way whatsoever, and the Lawgiver has cursed the collector of bribes. End.
2. His saying: 'and the suretyship by the first': meaning by the calamities if what is meant is that which is by right, it is valid unanimously; because it is obligatory upon every wealthy Muslim by obedience to the ruler in matters of benefit to the Muslims, and the treasury is not liable, or it is liable but has nothing. So in al-Fath.
3. His saying: 'and the fatwa is on its validity': He said in al-Bahr: The apparent meaning of their statements is the preference of validity in suretyship for the calamities that are without right; hence he said in Idah al-Islah: The fatwa is on its validity. And in al-Khaniyyah: The correct is its validity, and he has recourse against the principal if it was with his order. End.
And as for the division (qismah), it has been said: it is the calamities themselves, or the portion thereof; and it is said: it is the fixed regular calamity, while the calamities (nawa'ib) are those that are not fixed. Whichever it is, suretyship for them is valid.
(And if he says: I have guaranteed him until a month, he is believed, and if the claimant claims that it is due) And upon this is the way in al-Ikhtiyar, al-Mukhtar, and Multaqa al-Abhar. However, the author of al-Khaniyyah in his commentary on al-Jami' al-Saghir affirmed the lack of validity, and likewise he gave fatwa in al-Khayriyyah for lack of validity, relying on what is in al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Khulasah that it is the saying of the general body of scholars, and because of what is in al-'Imadiyyah: if a captive said to someone else, 'Release me', and the commanded one paid money and released him, al-Sarakhsi said: he can sue for it; and the author of al-Muhit said: no; and it is the most correct and upon it is the fatwa. End, summarized. And Allah knows best, and His knowledge is more precise and wise.
11) His saying: 'it has been said...' etc.; its summary: The scholars differed on its meaning:
1. Some said: it is the calamities themselves, so the conjunction is for explanation and interpretation, and the narration is with 'waw', or a portion thereof, such that what falls upon the general public is divided, like the expense of digging shared canals, and something of that befalls a person, so it is obligatory to pay it, and a man guarantees it.
2. Some said: 'division' (qismah) means the fixed calamity determined by the ruler, which are the diwan taxes every month or three months, and what is meant by nawa'ib is that which is not regular from them, so they are different; so the narration in these two is with 'aw'.
3. Some said its meaning: if two partners divide, then one of the partners prevents the other's share.
4. Abu Ja'far al-Hindawani (may Allah have mercy on him) said its meaning: if one of the partners demands division from his partner and the other refuses, and a person guarantees to take his place in the division, it is permissible because division is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rulings of kafalah (suretyship) for certain types of impositions (nawa'ib and jibayat). It distinguishes between lawful impositions that are obligatory for the public good (like army equipment when the treasury is empty) and unlawful ones (like the unjust taxes in Persia). The author notes that for the first type (the 'first' imposition, i.e., the rightful one), suretyship is unanimously valid. For the second type (the unjust one), there is disagreement, but the fatwa is on its validity because over time these unjust taxes have become treated like valid debts, so if the tax is collected from a farmer, he can recover from the landowner. The passage then discusses the meaning of 'qismah' (division) in this context: it may refer to the calamities themselves or to a portion, or to a fixed regular tax versus non-regular ones. Regardless of interpretation, suretyship for them is valid. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some authorities like the author of al-Khaniyyah reject validity, relying on the view of the majority of scholars and an analogy with a captive who pays for release. The preferred position per the text is the lack of validity, as it is the most correct and the fatwa is upon it.

Key Terms
النوائب (al-nawa'ib): Impositions or calamities; here, taxes or levies imposed on the public, sometimes unjustly.
الجبايات (al-jibayat): Taxes collected; here specifically the unjust taxes imposed on artisans in Persia.
الكفالة بالأولى (al-kafalah bi al-ula): Suretyship for the first type of imposition (the one by right), which is unanimously valid.
القسمة (al-qismah): Division; in this context, it is debated whether it refers to the nawa'ib themselves, a portion, a fixed tax, or partitioning among partners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on kafalah for the 'second' type of nawa'ib (unjust levies) in our time?
A) It is valid by consensus.
B) There is disagreement, but the fatwa is on its validity.
C) It is unanimously invalid.
D) It is invalid because it is like a maks.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for unjust levies is valid in the later Hanafi fatwa, even though the levy itself is sinful, and the surety has recourse against the principal.

Dispute between Guarantor and Claimant on Payment Term
Ruling on whose word is taken when guarantor and claimant disagree on payment term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on disputed payment terms in guarantee and distinguish it from the rule in debt acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Guarantor's word is taken; in debt acknowledgment, creditor's word is taken — deferment is a type of undertaking, not an attribute.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling on disputes between a guarantor (kafil) and a claimant (talib) regarding the payment term. The Hanafi school distinguishes sharply between guarantee and debt acknowledgment, leading to different outcomes. You will analyze the reasoning behind this distinction and compare it with the views of Imam al-Shafi'i and Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two scenarios: guarantee vs debt acknowledgment. State the ruling for each.
2
Identify the opponents
Who disagrees? Imam al-Shafi'i holds that in both cases the acknowledger's word is taken. Abu Yusuf holds the claimant's word in both.
3
Follow the opponents' proof
Understand Shafi'i's analogy: deferment is an attribute in both. Abu Yusuf's reasoning: the acknowledger claims a right against the other.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Read the Hanafi rebuttal: In debt, deferment is a right of the debtor, not an attribute. In guarantee, there is no debt, only a specific undertaking.
5
Determine the practical difference
What changes in application? A guarantor can defend by claiming the term; under Shafi'i, he would be forced to pay immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي قال الكفيل : كفلتُ بهذا المال لكن المطالبة بعد شهر، وقال الطالب: لا بل على صفة الحلول، فالقولُ قولُ الكفيل مع الحلف، وهذا بخلاف ما إذا أقر بدين مؤجل ، وقال المقر له : لا بل هو حال، فالقول للمقر له.
والفرق : أَنَّه إذا أقر بالدين
ه. وقال أبو بكـر بـن سـعـيـد : إن هذه الكلمة غلط ؛ لأن القسمة مصدر، والمصدر فعل، وهذا الفعل غير مضمون. ورُدَّ بأن القسمة تجيء بمعنى النصيب قال الله جلالة : وَنَيتُهُمْ أَنَّ الْمَاءَ قِسْمَةٌ بَيْنَهُم ، والمراد به النصيب.
(1)
(۱) قوله : والفرق ...الخ ؛ توضيحه : إن المقرَّ بالدين أقر بما هو سبب المطالبة في الحال، إذ الظاهر أن الدين كذلك ؛ لأنه إنما يثبت بدلاً عن قرض أو إتلاف أو بيع ونحوه ، والظاهر أن العاقل لا يرضى بخروج مستحقه في الحال إلا ببدل في الحال، فكان الحلول الأصل، والأجل عارض، فكان الدين المؤجَّلُ معروضاً بعارض لا نوعاً، ثم ادعى لنفسه حقاً، وهو تأخير المطالبة والآخر ينكره. وفي الكفالة ما أقرّ بالدين على ما هو الأصح ، بل بحق المطالبة بعد شهر ، والمكفول له يدعيها في الحال، والكفيل ينكر ذلك ، فالقولُ له ؛ وهذا لأنّ التزام المطالبة يتنوع إلى التزامها في الحال، أو في المستقبل كالكفالة بما ذاب أو بالدَّرَك ، فإنّما أقر بنوع منها ، فلا يلزم بالنوع الآخر، كذا في «الفتح».
وقال الشافعي : القول للمقر فيهما ؛ لأن الأجل وصف فيهما ، يقال : دين مؤجل وحال، وفي الأوصاف القولُ للمقر ، وقال أبو يوسف : القولُ للمقر له في الفصلين رواه عنه إبراهيم بن يوسف الله ؛ لأن المقر قد أقر له بحق ، ثم ادعى تأخيره فلا يُصَدَّقُ إلا بحجة ؛ لأنه ادَّعى أن له على صاحبه حقا وهو التأخير، ألا ترى أنه لو أقرّ بالكفالة على أنه بالخيار جازَ إقراره بالكفالة وبطل الخيار لما قلنا.
ونحن بينا الفرق بين الفصلين وليس هذا كالخيار ؛ لأن الخيار معنى يُبْطِلُ الكفالة فلا يُصَدَّقُ بإبطالها بعد الإقرار بخلاف الأجل ؛ لأنه ليس بإبطال، وإنما هو نوع في الكفالة على ما بينا، وما قاله الشافعي الله له أن الأجل وصف للدين لا يستقيم ؛ لا ليس بصفة للدين في الحقيقة، وإن كان وصفاً له لفظاً. ألا ترى أن الدين حق الطالب والأجـل حـق المطلوب ، ولو كان حقاً له لَمَا اختلف مستحقهما كالجودة والرداءة فيه، كذا في «التبيين»، وغيره.
ثم ادعى حقاً له ا، وهو تأخيرُ المطالبة ، والمقر له منكر، فالقول قوله بخلاف الكفالة
Namely, the guarantor said: 'I guaranteed this money but the claim is after a month,' and the claimant said: 'No, it is on the basis of immediate demand.' So the statement is the statement of the guarantor with oath. And this is contrary to the case where he acknowledged a deferred debt, and the acknowledgee said: 'No, it is immediate,' then the statement is the acknowledgee's.
And the difference: that when he acknowledged the debt... And Abu Bakr ibn Sa'id said: 'This word is an error because division (qisma) is a verbal noun, and the verbal noun is an action, and this action is not guaranteed.' And it was refuted that division is used in the sense of share; Allah the Exalted said: 'And We divided them that the water is a division between them,' and the intended meaning is share.
(1) His statement: 'And the difference...' etc.; its clarification: The acknowledger of a debt acknowledged what is a cause of immediate claim, because the apparent assumption about a debt is that it is that way; because it is established as a substitute for a loan, destruction, sale, etc., and the apparent assumption is that a rational person does not consent to the exit of his entitlement immediately except for a substitute immediately. So immediacy (hulul) is the original, and deferment is incidental. So the deferred debt is presented with an incidental attribute rather than as a type. Then he claimed a right for himself, which is deferment of the claim, and the other denies it. In guarantee, he did not acknowledge the debt according to the soundest view, but rather the right of claim after a month, and the guarantee-holder claims it immediately, and the guarantor denies that. So the statement is his. And this is because undertaking the claim varies into undertaking it immediately or in the future, like guarantee for what spoils or for liability (darak). So he only acknowledged one type, and he is not bound by the other type. Thus in al-Fath.
And al-Shafi'i said: 'The statement is the acknowledger's in both, because deferment is an attribute in both; it is said: a deferred debt and an immediate debt, and regarding attributes, the statement is the acknowledger's.' And Abu Yusuf said: 'The statement is the acknowledgee's in both cases,' as related by Ibrahim ibn Yusuf from him, because the acknowledger has acknowledged a right for him, then claimed its deferment; so he is not believed except with evidence, because he claimed that he has a right against his companion, which is deferment. Do you not see that if he acknowledged the guarantee on condition of option, his acknowledgment of guarantee is valid and the option is invalidated, due to what we said.
And we have shown the difference between the two cases, and this is not like the option case, because the option is a meaning that invalidates the guarantee, so he is not believed in invalidating it after acknowledgment, unlike the deferment, because it is not an invalidation, but rather a type in guarantee according to what we have explained. And what al-Shafi'i said that deferment is an attribute of debt is not sound, because in reality it is not an attribute of the debt, even if it is an attribute verbally. Do you not see that the debt is the right of the claimant and deferment is the right of the debtor? And if it were his right, their entitlement would not differ like quality and defect in it. Thus in al-Tabyin and others.
Then he claimed a right for himself, namely deferment of the claim, and the acknowledgee denies, so the statement is his, contrary to guarantee.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute discussing the legal ruling when a guarantor (kafil) and a claimant (talib) disagree on the term of payment. The Hanafi position is that the guarantor's word is taken (with oath) because he has only acknowledged a specific type of obligation (deferred claim) and is not bound by another type. This is contrasted with the case of acknowledging a debt with a term: if a debtor acknowledges a deferred debt and the creditor claims it is immediate, the creditor's word is taken. The reasoning is that in debt, immediacy is the origin and deferment is incidental, so the burden of proof lies on the claimant of the incidental. In guarantee, there is no underlying debt; the guarantor only undertook a specific type of claim. Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes both cases, arguing that deferment is an attribute in both, so the acknowledger's word should be taken. Abu Yusuf holds the creditor's word in both. The Hanafi response refutes Shafi'i by showing that deferment is not an attribute of the debt itself but a right of the debtor, thus a different category. A practical example: if a guarantor says 'I guarantee but do not pay for a month' and the beneficiary objects, the guarantor's statement prevails.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): A person who becomes a guarantor (surety) for another's obligation, specifically undertaking a claim for payment.
حلول (hulul): Immediacy of payment; a debt is due at the present time.
أجل (ajal): A deferred term; postponement of payment to a future date.
مقر له (muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor an acknowledgment (iqrar) is made; the creditor in the context of debt acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the rule different in guarantee vs debt acknowledgment?
A) Because deferment is an attribute in debt but a type in guarantee.
B) Because guarantee is a weaker contract.
C) Because the claimant is more trustworthy in guarantee.
D) Because the guarantor is a third party.

Key Takeaway
In guarantee, the guarantor's statement on the term is accepted because he only undertook a specific type of claim; in debt acknowledgment, the creditor's statement prevails because immediacy is the origin.

Ruling on the Guarantor of Recourse and the Nature of Term
Ruling with proof on guarantor of recourse and ajal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the guarantor of recourse becomes liable, distinguish the mufta bihi position from the rare narration, and explain the Hanafi view that the term in a debt is a right of the debtor, not a description.

Core Idea
The guarantor of recourse is not liable until the seller is adjudged to refund the price; the term in a debt is the debtor's right, not a description.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi sale and suretyship law: when can the buyer of a reclaimed item sue the guarantor of recourse? The answer hinges on whether mere reclamation (istihqaq) invalidates the sale. The text also delves into a related dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i about the legal nature of a deferred term (ajal) in a debt. By understanding the ruling and the underlying reasoning, you will grasp how Hanafi jurists protect the guarantor from premature claims and how they distinguish between the rights of creditor and debtor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
Read the first sentence: 'ولا يؤخذ ضامن الدَّرك إن استحق المبيعَ ما لم يقض بثمنه على بائعه'. Identify the act (suing the guarantor), the ruling (not allowed), and the condition (until the price is adjudged against the seller).
The word 'ما لم' indicates the condition is a court judgment.
2
Trace the reasoning from zahir al-riwayah
The text states that mere istihqaq does not invalidate the sale in the apparent narration. This is the basis for the ruling: since the sale remains valid, no debt is established against the seller, so the guarantor is not liable.
Zahir al-riwayah refers to the core books of Imam Muhammad.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Find the riwayat al-Amali from Abu Yusuf: the sale is invalidated immediately upon istihqaq, so the guarantor can be sued before judgment. Note that this is a rare narration and not the relied-upon position.
The mufta bihi is the zahir al-riwayah.
4
Examine the cross-madhhab dispute on ajal
Read the rebuttal of al-Shafi'i: 'ما قاله الشافعي: أن الأجل وصف للدين لا يستقيم'. Understand the Hanafi argument that ajal is a right of the debtor, not a description, using the analogy of quality and defect.
This dispute supports the main ruling by showing that the term is not inherent to the debt.
5
Apply the Hanafi proof
The proof uses qiyas: 'ألا ترى أن الدين حق الطالب والأجل حق المطلوب'. If ajal were a description, it could not be waived by the debtor, but it can be. Thus, it is a right, not a description.
This rational proof has zanni thubut and dalala.

ولا يؤخذ ضامنُ الدَّرَك إن استحق المبيعَ ما لم يقض بثمنه على بائعه؛ إذ بمجرَّدِ الاستحقاق لا ينتقض البيع في ظاهر الرواية؛ لأنه الكفالة على ما بينا، وما قاله الشافعي: أن الأجل وصف للدين لا يستقيم؛ ليس بصفة للدين في الحقيقة، وإن كان وصفاً له لفظاً. ألا ترى أن الدين حق الطالب والأجل حق المطلوب، ولو كان حقاً له لَمَا اختلف مستحقهما كالجودة والرداءة فيه. وفي رواية «الأمالي» عن أبي يوسف: إنه يبطل البيع بالاستحقاق قبل القضاء بالثمن على البائع فيأخذ الكفيل قبل أن يقضي على البائع بالثمن؛ لأن الضمان توجه على البائع ووجب للمشتري مطالبته، فكذلك على الكفيل.
And the guarantor of recourse shall not be taken (sued) if the sold item is reclaimed (by a third party) as long as the price has not been adjudged against his seller; because by mere reclamation the sale is not invalidated in the apparent narration. Because it is suretyship as we have explained. And what al-Shafi'i said that the term (ajal) is a description of the debt is not correct; it is not a description of the debt in reality, even if it is a description in wording. Do you not see that the debt is the right of the claimant (creditor) and the term is the right of the debtor? If it were his right, their entitlements would not differ like quality and defect in it. And in the narration of al-Amali from Abu Yusuf: Indeed the sale is invalidated by the reclamation before the adjudication of the price against the seller, so the guarantor is taken before adjudication against the seller; because the guarantee is directed against the seller and it becomes obligatory for the buyer to demand it, so likewise upon the guarantor.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the guarantor of recourse (ضامن الدرك) in a sale where the sold item is later claimed by a rightful owner (istihqaq). The ruling states that the buyer cannot sue the guarantor until a court has adjudged the seller to return the price. The reasoning is that mere reclamation does not invalidate the sale in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah); the sale remains valid until a judge decides. Therefore, no debt is established for the seller to pay until that judgment, so the guarantor is not yet liable. The text then addresses a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i who considered the term (ajal) in a debt as a description of the debt itself. The Hanafi response is that the term is actually a right of the debtor, not a description of the debt; it can be waived by the debtor, unlike inherent qualities like quality or defect. The text also mentions a different narration from Abu Yusuf (in al-Amali) that the sale is invalidated immediately upon istihqaq, so the guarantor can be sued before judgment. However, the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is the first one (zahir al-riwayah).

Key Terms
ضامن الدرك (dāmin al-darak): A guarantor who undertakes to indemnify the buyer if the sold item is reclaimed by a rightful owner (istihqaq).
الاستحقاق (al-istihqāq): The reclamation of property from a buyer by a third party who proves rightful ownership.
الأجل (al-ajal): The term or deferment period in a debt, considered a right of the debtor in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when can the buyer sue the guarantor of recourse (ضامن الدرك) after the sold item is reclaimed by a third party?
A) Immediately upon the reclamation (istihqaq).
B) Only after the court has adjudged the seller to pay the price.
C) After one year from the sale.
D) The guarantor is never liable.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor of recourse becomes liable only after a court judgment against the seller, because the sale is not invalidated by mere reclamation; the term in a debt is a right, not a description.

Kafala of Two Persons: Only the Excess Over Half is Recoverable
Ruling on mutual suretyship between two co-debtors with commentary expansions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule of recourse in mutual suretyship and identify the conditions that affect the right of recovery.

Core Idea
In mutual suretyship, a guarantor can only recover the amount he paid that exceeds his own share.

This lesson examines a complex area of kafala: when two persons are jointly indebted and each guarantees the other. The core ruling is that if one pays the entire debt, he can only recover from his partner the amount that exceeds his own half. The commentary then explains important conditions regarding the equality of the debts and the request of the partner.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the contract
The passage deals with two debtors who each guarantee the other. The contract of suretyship is valid (jāza al-‘aqd).
The commentary explains why this chapter follows the one on single suretyship: the two follow the one in sequence.
2
State the default rule of recourse
If one pays the entire debt, he can only recover the excess over his own share (i.e., more than half the total debt).
The matn says: 'لم يرجع على شريكه إلا بما أدى زائداً على النصف'.
3
Analyse the condition of equality of debts
The default rule applies only if the two debts are equal in attribute (both immediate or both deferred) and cause (e.g., both from the same transaction).
The phrase 'دين على اثنين' implies this equality.
4
Apply special cases when debts differ
If debts differ in attribute or cause, different rules apply for recourse and specification of payment.
For example, if one debt is deferred and the other immediate, paying the deferred debt early prevents recourse before maturity.
5
Note the requirement of request for recourse
The guarantor can only recover from the partner if he guaranteed at the partner's request (kafāla bi-amrih).
Commentary: 'فيرجع على شريكه إن كفل بأمره'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كفالة الرجلين. دين على اثنينِ كَفِلَ كلّ عن الآخرِ، لم يرجع على شريكه إلا بما أدى زائداً على النصف. قوله : دين على اثنين.... الخ؛ شرع في كفالة الاثنين بعد الفراغ عن ذكر كفالة الواحد؛ لأن الاثنين بعد الواحد تبعاً فأُخر وضعاً؛ ليوافق الوضع الطبع، فقال: دين على اثنين ... الخ. وحاصل المسألة : أنه إذا كان الدين على اثنين وكَفِل كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عن الآخر جاز العقد، وما أدّاه أحدهما من الدين لا يرجعُ على الآخرِ إلا إذا زاد على النصف، فينصرف إلى ما عليه كفالة، فيرجع على شريكه إن كفل بأمره. وفي قوله : دين على اثنين؛ إشارة في استواء الدينين صفة وسبباً، فلو اختلفا صفة كان ما على أحدهما مؤجّلاً وما على صاحبه حالاً، فإن أدى صح تعينه على شريكه، ورجع به عليه، وعلى عكسه لا يرجع؛ لأن الكفيل إذا عجل ديناً مؤجلاً ليس له الرجوع على الأصيل قبل الحلول. ولو اختلفا سبباً كإن كان على أحدهما فرضاً وما على الآخر ثمن مبيع، فإنّه يصح تعيين المؤدى؛ لأن النية في الجنسين المختلفين معتبرة، وفي الجنس الواحد لغو. ذكره في «الفتح»، وغيره. قوله : كل منهما عن صاحبه؛ ولو كَفِلَ أحدهما عن صاحبه دون الآخر، وأدى الكفيل فجعله عن صاحبه، فإنه يصدَّقُ. كذا في «البحر». قوله : زائداً على النصف، والمراد أن يكون زائداً على ما عليه، ولو كان دون النصف أو أكثر منه.
Chapter on the suretyship of two persons. A debt upon two, each of whom guaranteed the other, he does not have recourse against his partner except for what he paid in excess of half. His statement: 'A debt upon two, etc.' He began discussing the suretyship of two after finishing the mention of the suretyship of one, because the two are after the one in sequence so it was postponed in arrangement to match nature. So he said: 'A debt upon two, etc.' The summary of the issue: When there is a debt upon two and each one of them guarantees the other, the contract is valid. What one of them pays of the debt, he does not have recourse against the other except if it exceeds half; then it is applied to what he is surety for, so he has recourse against his partner if he was surety at his request. In his statement 'a debt upon two' is an indication of the equality of the two debts in attribute and cause. If they differ in attribute, one being deferred and the other immediate, then if he pays, his specification of it for his partner is valid and he has recourse for it; but in the opposite case he does not have recourse because if the guarantor pays a deferred debt early, he does not have recourse against the principal before maturity. If they differ in cause, for example one is a loan and the other the price of a sale, then the specification of what is paid is valid because intention is considered in different categories, but in the same category it is disregarded. This was mentioned in Al-Fath and others. His statement: 'each of them from the other' - if one of them guaranteed for the other and the other did not, and the guarantor paid and applied it to his partner, then he is believed. Thus in Al-Bahr. His statement: 'excess over half' - the meaning is that it exceeds what is upon him, even if it is less than half or more than it.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharh) on a matn concerning the chapter of suretyship of two persons. The matn states the core ruling: if two persons are jointly indebted and each guarantees the other, then if one pays the debt, he can only recover from the other the amount that exceeds his own half share. The commentary begins by explaining why this chapter comes after the chapter on suretyship of one person: the two follow the one in order, so it is placed later to match natural arrangement. The summary of the case is that if each guarantees the other with the request of the seller (or creditor), the contract is valid. If one pays more than half, that excess is considered payment of the guarantee, so he can recover it from the other partner (the principal debtor) if the guarantee was at his request. The commentary then draws attention to the wording 'a debt upon two', which implies equality of the two debts in both attribute (e.g., both immediate, both deferred) and cause (e.g., both from a sale). If the attributes differ – one deferred and one immediate – and the guarantor pays the immediate debt, he can specify it as payment for his partner and recover it. But if he pays the deferred debt early, he cannot recover it before maturity because the rule is that a guarantor who pays a deferred debt early has no recourse against the principal before the term. If the causes differ – for example, one debt is a loan (qard) and the other is the price of a sale (thaman mabi') – then the payer can specify which of the two debts he intends to pay, because intention is effective when the debts are of different types. However, if the debts are of the same type, intention is disregarded. The commentary also mentions a variant: if only one of the two guarantees the other (not mutual), and the guarantor pays and claims he applied it to his partner's share, his word is accepted (he is believed). Finally, the phrase 'excess over half' means any amount beyond what he himself owed, even if it is less than half of the total debt or more.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafāla): Suretyship; a contract whereby one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another, becoming jointly liable.
كفيل (kafīl): The surety or guarantor who undertakes to pay the debt if the principal debtor fails.
أصيل (aṣīl): The principal debtor whose debt is guaranteed.
رجوع (rujū‘): Recourse; the right of the guarantor to recover from the principal debtor what he paid on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two persons jointly purchase a slave for 1000 dirhams, with each guaranteeing the other to the seller. One of them pays the entire 1000. How much can he recover from the other partner?
A) 1000 dirhams
B) 500 dirhams
C) 0 dirhams
D) 500 dirhams, but only if he guaranteed at the partner's request

Key Takeaway
In mutual suretyship, a guarantor can only recover the amount he paid that exceeds his own share, subject to conditions of debt equality and the partner's request.

Recourse Between Mutual Guarantors (Kafālah Mutaqābilah)
Commentary on the ruling of mutual guarantee and recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse between two mutual guarantors and explain the reasoning based on the equality of liability.

Core Idea
Two mutual guarantors of the same debt share liability equally, so the paying guarantor recovers half from the co-guarantor.

This lesson examines a scenario in Hanafi guarantee law where two persons become guarantors for the same debt, and then each guarantees the other's guarantee. The ruling is that whichever one pays has recourse for half from the other, even if the amount paid is small. We will dissect the ruling, the proof, and the reasoning from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah, including variations and the underlying principle of equality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The scenario: two guarantors for a debt, each also guarantees the other for that same debt. The ruling: the paying guarantor recovers half from the co-guarantor, regardless of amount paid.
This is the default case where each guaranteed the full debt.
2
Understand the proof (talīl)
The proof is rational: the original debt is stronger (it is both a debt and a claim), while the guarantee is only a claim. However, when both are guarantees, they are equal in strength. The text analogizes to a purchase case where a stronger obligation preempts a weaker one.
The analogy: buying a garment and ten dirhams for twenty, where the cash paid in session is allocated to the exchange (ṣarf) because it is stronger.
3
Analyze the variations
If each guaranteed only half of the debt initially, then the mutual guarantee is only for that half, so recourse is limited to the excess above half. If they guaranteed simultaneously or successively, the same principle applies.
The key is whether the debt is split from the start.
4
Connect to the principle of equality (istiwā')
The ruling is based on the equality of the two guarantors in liability. Since both are equally liable, any payment is deemed common between them, leading to half recourse. This avoids circularity (dawr).
If one were the original debtor, that would tip the balance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] (ولو كفلا بشيء عن رجلٍ، وكفل كلّ به عن صاحبهِ رَجَعَ عليه بنصف ما أدى وإن قل)، على رجل ألف فكفل كل واحدٍ من شخصين آخرين عن الأصيل بهذا الألف ، ثم كَفل كلُّ واحدٍ من الكفيلين عن صاحبه بأمره بهـذا الألف، فكل ما أداه أحدهما وإن قل رجع على الآخر بنصفه بخلاف الصورة الأُولى، فإن الأصالة تُرجح على الكفالة . 11 ] قوله : أولى؛ لأن الأوَّل دين ومطالبة ، والثاني مطالبة فقط ، ولا يعارض الأول، وكذا سبب الأول وهو الشراء أقوى من سبب الثاني، وهو الكفالة ؛ ولهذا ينفذ الأوّل من المريض من جميع ماله، وإن كان عليه دين ، والثاني لا ينفذ إلا من الثلث بشرط أن لا يكون عليه دين. وإن كان عليه دين مستغرق لا ينفذ أصلاً ، فكان آكد، فلا يعارضه الضعيف ، وهذا نظير ما إذا اشترى ثوباً وعشرة دراهم بعشرين درهماً، فنقد في المجلس عشرة ، جعل النقود ثمن الصرف ؛ لأن الواجب أقوى لحاجته إلى القبض في المجلس. كما صرحوا ألفُ [٢]قوله : قوله : على رجل ...الخ ؛ توضيحه : أنه إذا كان على رجل دين أم درهم مثلاً ، فكفل عنه رجلان كل واحدٍ منهما بجميعهِ على الانفراد، ثم كفل كل واحد من الرجلين عن صاحبه بما لزمه إذ الكفالة عن الكفيل جائزة، كما تجوز عن الأصيل. فكلُّ ما أدى أحدهما رجع بنصفه على صاحبه، ثم يرجعان على الأصيل إن شاء، وإن شـاء رجـع هـو بالكلِّ على الأصيل ؛ لأن ما عليهما مستويان، فلا ترجيح للبعض على البعض إذ الكل كفالة، فيكون المؤدّى شائعاً عنهما، ويرجع بنصفه على شريكه إذ لا يؤدي إلى الدور ؛ لأن قضية عقد الكفالة الاستواء لاستوائهما في العلة، وهي ضمان الكفالة. فلَمَّا كان كذلك كان لِمَن أدَّى ولاية الرجوع على صاحبه بنصف ما أدى ؛ لأنه كتاب الكفالة الضمان وكفالة الرجلين وكفالة العبد أما هاهنا فالكل كفالة فلا رُجحان". ٣٨٥ وقال في «الهداية»: الصَّحيحُ أنَّ صورة المسألة على هذا الوجه احتراز عما إذا كفلا بألف حتّى كان الألفُ منقسماً عليهما نصفين، ثُمَّ كَفِلَ كُلُّ واحدٍ منهما عن صاحبه بأمره، ففي هذه الصورة لا يرجعُ على شريكه إلا بما زاد على مساو لصاحبه في الالتزام بجهة واحدة، فوجب أن يستويا في الغرم بخلاف المسألة النصف. الأولى ; Cream of the Finale for the Support of the Care

And if two persons become guarantors for something on behalf of a man, and each becomes guarantor for his companion for it, then he who pays has recourse against the other for half of what he paid, even if it is small. [1] Because the occurrence of what is paid upon what is due originally is more preponderant than its occurrence upon what is due by guarantee. [2] (And if two persons become guarantors for something on behalf of a man, and each becomes guarantor for his companion for it, then he who pays has recourse against the other for half of what he paid, even if it is small): For example, a man owes one thousand, so two other persons each become guarantors for the principal debtor for this one thousand, then each of the two guarantors becomes guarantor for his companion with his order for this one thousand. Then whatever one of them pays, even if it is small, he has recourse against the other for half of it, contrary to the first scenario, because the original debt is preponderant over the guarantee. [11] His saying: 'more preponderant' because the first is a debt and a claim, and the second is only a claim, and it does not oppose the first. Similarly, the cause of the first, which is purchase, is stronger than the cause of the second, which is guarantee. For this reason, the first is executed from the sick person from all his wealth, even if he has a debt, and the second is not executed except from one-third, on condition that he has no debt. And if he has an all-encompassing debt, it is not executed at all. So it is stronger, and the weak does not oppose it. This is similar to the case when someone buys a garment and ten dirhams for twenty dirhams, and he pays ten dirhams in the session, then the cash is made the price of the exchange because the obligatory is stronger due to its need for receipt in the session, as they have stated. [2] His saying: 'His saying: 'on a man...' etc.'; its clarification: that if a man owes a debt, for example one thousand dirhams, and two men become guarantors for him, each individually for the full debt, then each of the two men becomes guarantor for his companion for what he has incurred, because guarantee by a guarantor is permissible, just as it is permissible for the principal debtor. Then whatever one of them pays, he has recourse against his companion for half of it, then they both have recourse against the principal debtor if they wish, and if he wishes, he has recourse against the principal debtor for the full amount because what is upon them is equal, so there is no preponderance of some over others since all is guarantee. Thus, what is paid is common between them, and he has recourse against his partner for half of it because it does not lead to circularity; because the essence of the contract of guarantee is equality due to their equality in the cause, which is the liability of guarantee. So when it is like that, whoever pays has the authority of recourse against his companion for half of what he paid, because... the book of guarantee, liability, and the guarantee of two men and the guarantee of a slave; but here, all is guarantee, so there is no preponderance. 385 And he said in al-Hidāyah: The correct view is that the scenario of the issue in this manner is a precaution against the case where they guarantee one thousand such that the one thousand is divided between them in halves, then each of them becomes guarantor for his companion with his order. In this scenario, he does not have recourse against his partner except for what exceeds half, because he is equal to his partner in the obligation by one means, so they must be equal in the payment, unlike the first scenario, because preponderance exists from the beginning, so recourse does not harm it by leading to circularity. Then they both have recourse against the principal debtor because they paid his debt on his behalf. And if the one who paid wishes, he has recourse against the principal debtor for the full amount because he is a guarantor for the full amount with his order. This is when each of them becomes guarantor for the principal debtor for the entire debt successively, then each of them becomes guarantor for his companion for the full amount. And as for when each of them becomes guarantor for half, then each of them becomes guarantor for his companion for half, then it is like the first scenario in the correct view such that he does not have recourse against his partner from what he paid unless it exceeds half. Similarly, if they both become guarantors for the principal debtor for the entire debt simultaneously, then each of them becomes guarantor for his companion, because the debt is divided between them in halves, so he is not a guarantor for the principal debtor for the full amount. And similarly, if each of them becomes guarantor for the principal debtor for the full amount successively, then each of them becomes guarantor for his companion for half. So it is in al-Tibyān and al-Ramz and other comprehensive works. [1] His saying: 'so there is no preponderance' means that preponderance of some over others is inconceivable because they are equal. If everything paid by one were placed as for this or that, it would necessitate the preponderance of one of two equals, and the necessary consequence is false. (2) His saying: 'He said in al-Hidāyah... etc.'; He said in al-Hidāyah: The meaning of the issue in the correct view is that the guarantee is for the full amount from the principal debtor and for the full amount from the partner, and the claim is multiple, so they combine. [2] (1) Tabyīn al-Ḥaqā'iq (4:168). (2) Ramz al-Ḥaqā'iq (2:78). (3) Al-Hidāyah (3:97).
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah on the ruling of mutual suretyship (kafālah) and the right of recourse (rujū') between guarantors. The ruling states: If two persons become guarantors for a debt of a principal debtor (aṣīl), and then each becomes guarantor for the other (i.e., each guarantees the other's guarantee), then whichever one pays the debt has the right to claim half of what he paid from the other guarantor, even if the amount paid is small. This is because both are equally guarantors; there is no preponderance of one over the other. The commentary explains that if one of them were the original debtor and the other a guarantor, the original debtor would have no recourse against the guarantor because his obligation is primary. But here both are guarantors, so they share liability equally. The key reason is that the cause of obligation (sabab) is the same for both: the contract of guarantee. Therefore, when one pays, the payment is deemed to be from both equally, unless one of them had an original debt. The passage then discusses variations: if each guarantees only half of the debt initially, then their mutual guarantee is only for that half, so recourse is limited to excess over half. It also covers simultaneous vs. consecutive guarantees. Finally, it cites al-Hidāyah and other works to confirm the correct interpretation: the scenario described is a precaution against the case where the debt is divided equally from the start, in which case they share the burden without further recourse. The commentary emphasizes that this ruling avoids circularity (dawr) because the mutual guarantee equalizes the liability.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): A contract of suretyship/guarantee, where one person (kafil) becomes liable for a debt or obligation of another (aṣīl).
أصيل (aṣīl): The principal debtor on whose behalf a guarantee is given.
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor who assumes liability for the debt of the aṣīl.
رجوع (rujūʿ): The right of recourse; the claim of the guarantor who paid against the principal debtor or co-guarantor to recover what was paid.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee; often synonymous with kafālah in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where two persons guarantee a debt for a principal debtor and each guarantees the other's guarantee, what is the amount the paying guarantor can claim from the co-guarantor?
A) The full amount he paid
B) Half of what he paid
C) Nothing, because the principal debtor is primarily liable
D) Only if he paid more than half

Key Takeaway
When two mutual guarantors exist, their liability is equal, so any payment is shared equally; the paying guarantor can recover half from the co-guarantor.

Mutual Suretyship and Recourse Between Equal Guarantors
Refining the ruling on recourse between equal sureties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse between equal sureties in mutual kafalah and explain the logical argument (dalil aqli) of avoiding circularity (dawr).

Core Idea
Equal sureties share liability equally; recourse is only for excess over half to avoid circularity.

This lesson refines the Hanafi ruling on recourse between two guarantors who have guaranteed each other. The central issue is how to distribute the loss when both are equally liable. The text from the commentary tradition introduces a logical argument based on equality and the prevention of circularity (dawr), which leads to a nuanced position: neither guarantor has preference, so recourse is limited to what exceeds half of the total paid. We will walk through the scenario, the logical proof, and the intra-Hanafi disagreement over whether the passage is an exception or a mere clarification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two persons each guarantee the entire debt of the principal debtor (asil) successively, then each guarantees the other for the entire amount. This creates equal liability.
The key is that the surety for the whole is repeated on both sides.
2
State the ruling
When one surety pays, he can only recover from his partner any amount exceeding half of what he paid. He cannot recover the first half because that is his own share.
The ruling prevents preference of one equal over the other.
3
Understand the logical proof (dalil aqli)
If one surety could recover the full amount from the other, the other would have an equal right to recover back, leading to infinite circularity. Equality requires equal sharing, so recourse is limited to the excess over half.
The proof is based on the impossibility of circularity (dawr) and the principle that equals must be treated equally.
4
Apply to variations
The text applies the same ruling to other scenarios: if each guarantees half initially, then each guarantees the other for half; or if they guarantee jointly. In all cases, the debt is divided equally.
The key factor is that the suretyships are mutual and equal.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
The commentator (sharih) thought Al-Hidayah's wording was an exception (ihtiraz) from a different case, but the hashiya author clarifies it is merely a correction of the branch (tafri'). The practical ruling remains the same.
This shows the importance of precise reading of classical texts.

And the apparent is that the permissibility of having recourse for the whole against the guaranteed person is not affected by the estimation of the issue on the second manner, even if the ruling is so. Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hasiya) on the commentary of Al-Hidayah regarding mutual suretyship between two guarantors. The text begins by quoting Al-Hidayah's statement that the correct interpretation of the scenario is an exception (ihtiraz) from the case where two sureties each guarantee half of the debt and then each guarantees the other. In the exception scenario, each surety guarantees the entire debt successively, then each guarantees the other for the entire amount. The result is that the debt is considered equally shared between them, so neither has preference. Therefore, if one pays, he can only recover from his partner any amount exceeding half of the total paid. This avoids circularity (dawr) because both are equally liable. The text then discusses variations: if each guarantees half initially and then each guarantees the other for half, it is like the first case. If they guarantee the whole jointly, the debt is split equally. The commentator (sharih) mistakenly thought Al-Hidayah's statement was an exception to a different case, but the correct understanding is that it merely clarifies the branch (tafri'). The author of the hashiya supports the view that the exception is valid if we consider the first suretyship as principal relative to the second. However, he ultimately accepts the explanation that the purpose is to correct the branch, not to make an exception. The key principle is that when both sureties are equal in obligation, payment by one is treated as shared, so each bears half unless there is a preference from the start.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where a person (kafeel) guarantees the debt or obligation of another (asil).
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Recourse; the right of the surety who paid to recover the amount from the principal debtor or co-surety.
الدور (al-dawr): Circularity; a logical fallacy where a consequence leads back to its premise, making the reasoning invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Equal sureties in mutual kafalah share liability equally; recourse is only for the excess over half to maintain equality and prevent circularity.

Release of One Joint Surety: Liability of the Remaining Surety
Ruling with proof and critical refinement of a commentator's error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the remaining surety's liability after one surety is released, distinguish between the cross-guarantee scenario and the split-debt scenario, and explain the proof for the full liability.

Core Idea
Release of one joint surety does not reduce the other's liability; the remaining surety remains liable for the full debt.

This lesson examines a nuanced point in Hanafi suretyship (kafalah): when two sureties each guarantee the full debt and also guarantee each other, and the creditor releases one, what is the remaining surety's obligation? The Hidayah rules that the remaining surety is taken for the whole amount. However, a commentator misunderstood this and thought the liability should be halved. We will clarify the correct ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two scenarios
Identify the two possible arrangements: (a) each surety guarantees the full debt and also guarantees the other's liability (cross-guarantees), and (b) each surety guarantees only half of the debt (split scenario). The ruling differs based on which scenario applies.
The cross-guarantee scenario is the subject of the Hidayah's ruling.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for the cross-guarantee scenario
When each surety guarantees the whole debt and also guarantees the other, release of one leaves the other liable for the entire debt. The creditor may claim the full amount from the remaining surety.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Trace the proof
The release of a surety does not release the original debtor (asil). The remaining surety is still a surety for the original debtor for the full amount. The cross-guarantee does not affect the primary liability; it is an additional obligation that does not split the debt.
The proof is based on the independence of the primary kafalah.
4
Identify the mistaken view
Some Hanafi commentators thought that the cross-guarantee makes the liability shared, so release of one halves the remaining liability. They analogized to the split-debt scenario, but this analogy is invalid because in the cross-guarantee arrangement, each surety is primarily liable for the full debt.
The mistaken view is based on a false qiyas.
5
Apply the distinction
In the split-debt scenario (each surety guarantees only half), release of one leaves the other liable only for half. The key is whether each surety has guaranteed the full debt independently.
The practical consequence hinges on the exact wording of the guarantee.

Source Text

Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a detailed super-commentary (hashiya) on the commentary of al-Hidayah regarding joint suretyship. The author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya clarifies the correct interpretation of the Hidayah's ruling: if two sureties each guarantee the entire debt of the original debtor, and then each guarantees the other (i.e., each becomes surety for the other's liability), and the creditor releases one of the sureties, the remaining surety is liable for the full debt. The commentator (sharh) had mistakenly thought that the Hidayah's phrase 'in the correct view' was an exception to exclude a scenario where the debt is split equally between the sureties. The author refutes this, explaining that the Hidayah's intention is to correct the branching (tafri') and the ruling remains that the remaining surety is taken for the whole. The proof is that the release of a surety does not release the original debtor, and the other surety remains a surety for the original debtor for the entire amount. The analogy with the split-debt scenario (where each is initially surety for half) is invalid because there the liability was originally divided. The passage also discusses the scenario where both sureties guarantee the same debt without cross-guarantees, in which case payment by one gives him half recourse against the other. The key distinction is whether each surety is also a surety for the other's liability. If yes, release of one leaves the other fully liable; if not, the liability is shared.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a contract in which a person (kafil) guarantees the debt or presence of another, making himself liable to the creditor (talib).
الأصيل (al-asil): The original debtor; the person on whose behalf the surety is given.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety; the person who guarantees the debt or presence of the original debtor.
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee; often used interchangeably with kafalah in Hanafi fiqh, though technically daman is a specific type of guarantee for debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men, Zayd and Amr, jointly guarantee a debt of 1000 dirhams owed by Bakr. Zayd and Amr each also guarantee the other's liability. The creditor releases Zayd. How much can the creditor claim from Amr?
A) 0 dirhams
B) 500 dirhams
C) 1000 dirhams
D) 2000 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In a joint suretyship with cross-guarantees, release of one surety does not halve the other's liability; the remaining surety is liable for the entire debt.

Two Mutual Guarantors: Effect of Releasing One
Intra-Hanafi dispute on release of mutual guarantor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a creditor releases one of two mutual guarantors and explain the reasoning based on independent guarantees.

Core Idea
Releasing one mutual guarantor leaves the other liable for the full debt if each guaranteed independently.

This passage from a commentary on Al-Hidayah deals with a nuanced scenario of two guarantors who each guarantee the entire debt of the principal and then guarantee each other. It resolves an intra-Hanafi dispute between the author of Al-Hidayah and the commentator regarding the effect of releasing one guarantor. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the structure of multiple guarantees and the principle that release of a guarantor does not affect the principal's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two guarantors each guarantee the entire debt (1000) for the principal, then each guarantees the other. The creditor releases one guarantor. Determine the liability of the remaining guarantor.
The scenario is given in the passage: 'each guaranteed the thousand for the principal, then each guaranteed the thousand for his partner.'
2
State the supported ruling
According to the commentator, the remaining guarantor is liable for the whole amount (1000) because each guarantee was independent. The release of one does not affect the other's separate guarantee.
The passage says: 'if he releases one, the guarantee of the other for the whole thousand remains.'
3
Contrast with Al-Hidayah's view
Al-Hidayah considered it 'sound' that the debt is split between the two guarantors, so releasing one leaves the other liable for only half (500).
The commentator explicitly distinguishes his position from that of 'the author of al-Hidayah'.
4
Understand the reasoning
The commentator argues that release of a guarantor does not affect the principal's debt. Since each guarantor independently guaranteed the full amount, the remaining guarantor's liability for the full debt remains intact.
The illa is the independence of each guarantee. This is a qiyas-based reasoning.

Source Text
