Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof discussion from the commentary on al-Hidayah. It addresses a specific scenario involving two guarantors who each guarantee the entire debt of the principal, and then each guarantees the other. The ruling clarifies the effect of the creditor releasing one of them. The author distinguishes between two cases: the one he supports and the one al-Hidayah considered 'sound.' In the supported view, if one guarantor is released, the other remains liable for the full debt because each guarantee was independent. In al-Hidayah's view, the debt is split between them, so release of one leaves the other liable for only half. The sharh then addresses an objection about the priority of the original liability over the secondary guarantee. It argues that when a guarantor pays, he first satisfies his own direct liability, and only the excess beyond his share can be reclaimed from his co-guarantor. The discussion is consistent with Hanafi principles of kafala, where each guarantee is a separate obligation.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafala): Suretyship: undertaking to fulfill an obligation owed by another.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Release or discharge of a debt or obligation by the creditor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is liable for 500.
B) He is liable for the full 1000.
C) He is discharged from all liability.
D) He is liable for 250.

Key Takeaway
The release of one mutual guarantor does not reduce the other's liability if each guaranteed the whole debt independently; the debt remains whole on the remaining guarantor.

Dissolution of Mufawada and Creditor's Rights
Explains creditor's rights after dissolution of a mufawada partnership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on creditor's rights after dissolution of a mufawada partnership and explain the reasoning based on the principle of principalship prevailing over suretyship.

Core Idea
After dissolution of mufawada, the creditor may claim from either partner; the paying partner reclaims only the excess over half.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the dissolution of a mufawada partnership. The passage clarifies that despite dissolution, the mutual suretyship (kafala) inherent in mufawada continues for debts incurred during the partnership. The key principle is that each partner is principal in half and surety in half, and the principal obligation takes precedence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
State the two-part ruling: the creditor may demand the full debt from either partner, and a paying partner can reclaim only the amount exceeding his half. Note the condition: the debt must have arisen during the partnership, not before.
2
Understand the nature of mufawada
Mufawada is a partnership of equals that inherently includes kafala (suretyship) and agency. This mutual suretyship is established by the contract and continues even after dissolution for debts incurred during the partnership.
See the definition in the commentary: شركة متساويين مالاً وحرية وعقلاً وديناً.
3
Analyze the proof: principalship outweighs suretyship
Each partner is principal in half of the debt and surety for the other half. When a partner pays, the payment first satisfies his principal obligation. Only the excess over his half was paid as a surety, which he can reclaim.
The Arabic phrase: جهة الأصالة راجحة على جهة الكفالة.
4
Handle the objection regarding individual purchase
If one partner buys something individually (not for the partnership), the debt is not joint. After dissolution, the buyer is principal in his own half and agent for the partner in the other half. He can reclaim half from the partner, not just the excess, because the debt was never joint.
This is distinguished from the original ruling; the original applies only to debts incurred during the partnership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو فسخت المفاوضة أخذ ربُّ الدِّينِ آيَاً شاءَ من شريكيها بكل دينه، ولم يرجع أحدهما على صاحبه إلا بما أدَّى زائداً على النصف [٢] (ولو فسخت المفاوضةُ ) أخذ ربُّ الدِّينِ أيّاً شاء من شريكيها بكل دينه) ؛ لما عُرف أن شركة المفاوضة تتضمن الكفالة ) ، (ولم يرجع أحدهما على صاحبه إلا بما أدَّى زائداً على النصف) ؛ لِمَا عرفت أن جهة الأصالة راجحة على جهة الكفالة. أقول : في هذه المسألة إشكال وهو أن أحد المفاوضين إذ اشترى شيئاً ، ثمّ فسخا المفاوضة ، فالبائع إن طَلَبَ الثَّمن من مشتريه ، فلا تعلق لهذه المسألة بمسألة الكفالة ، بل المشتري في النصف أصيل، وفي النصف وكيل ، فكلُّ ما أَدَّى ينبغي أن يرجع بنصفِهِ على الشريك ؛ لأنه اشترى العبد صفقة واحدة ، فصارَ الثَّمنُ دينا عليه ، ولا يُمْكِنُ قسمته، فكل ما يؤديه الأصيل، يؤدِّيه منه ومن شريكه ، فيرجع عليه بالنصف. أبرأ الطالب أحديهما يبقى الكفالة للآخر بخمسمئه. فافهم. [٢]قوله : تتضمن الكفالة ؛ فكل واحد من المتفاوضين كفيل عن الآخر، ولَمَّا ثبتت الكفالة بعقد المفاوضة قبل الافتراق لم تبطل بالافتراق. [٣] قوله : لما عرفت... الخ ؛ يعني لما عرفت فيما تقدم من أنه أصيل في النصف وكفيل في الآخر، وجهة الأصالة ترجّحُ على جهةِ الكفالة ، فما أدى يصرفُ إلى ما عليه بحق الأصالة ، فإن زاد على النصف كان الزائد عن الكفالة، فيرجع به عليه.

Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof concerning the dissolution of a mufawada partnership. The ruling states that after dissolution, the creditor may demand the full debt from either partner, and a partner who pays more than his half can reclaim the excess from the other. The reasoning is that mufawada inherently includes kafala (suretyship) and agency, so each partner is liable for the other's debts incurred during the partnership. However, there is an objection: if one partner made a purchase individually, the seller's claim is against him alone, and the partnership's dissolution does not create a joint liability; the buyer is principal in his own half and agent for the partner in the other half. The commentator reconciles by noting that the original ruling applies only to debts that arose during the partnership, not prior ones. The passage also defines mufawada as a partnership of equals that includes mutual agency and surety.

Key Terms
المفاوضة (al-mufawada): A partnership of equals in capital, freedom, religion, and legal capacity, involving mutual agency and suretyship.
جهة الأصالة (jihhat al-asala): The basis of being a principal, which takes precedence over being a surety.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire amount.
B) Half of what he paid.
C) The amount that exceeds his half share.
D) Nothing, because the partnership is dissolved.

Key Takeaway
After dissolution of a mufawada partnership, mutual suretyship continues for debts incurred during the partnership; the creditor may claim from either partner, and the paying partner recovers only the amount exceeding his own share.

Joint Kitaba of Two Slaves with Mutual Suretyship
Ruling on joint kitaba with mutual suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on recovery of payments in a joint kitaba with mutual suretyship and identify the condition that the contract must be a single one.

Core Idea
In a single kitaba contract with mutual suretyship, each slave recovers half of what he paid from the other.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh concerning joint kitaba (contract of manumission by payment) where two slaves are contracted together and each guarantees the other. The ruling clarifies that when they pay, each can claim half back from the co-debtor. The key condition is that the contract must be a single one; if separate contracts, the suretyship is invalid. We will unpack the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is joint kitaba with mutual suretyship. The ruling: each slave recovers half of what he paid from the other.
2
Spot the essential condition
The contract must be a single contract (ʿaqd wāḥid). If there are two separate contracts, the suretyship is invalid from the outset.
This condition prevents the problem of dividing the debt before receipt.
3
Understand the qiyas objection
By strict analogy (qiyas), the suretyship might not be valid even in a single contract, but the ruling is upheld due to a specific scenario: the two slaves jointly purchasing a single transaction (ṣafqa wāḥida).
The commentary resolves potential difficulties by carrying the matn on this form.
4
Resolve three potential difficulties
The commentary raises three points: (1) joint purchase avoids division of debt; (2) dividing debt before receipt is problematic; (3) if the debt is shared, suretyship would imply being surety for oneself. The resolution is to interpret the case as a joint purchase.
The phrase 'فليحمل مسألة المتن على هذه الصورة' indicates how to interpret the matn.
5
Conclude the operative ruling
The mufta bihi position: each slave has recourse against the other for half of what he paid, and this applies only when the kitaba is a single contract with mutual suretyship.
This is the relied-upon ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبدان كوتبا بعقدٍ واحدٍ ، وكَفِلَ كلّ عن صاحبه، رجع كلِّ على الآخرِ : أي عبدان قال لهما المولى : كاتبتكما بالألف إلى سنة، وقبلا، وكَفِلَ كُلَّ عن صاحبه، فكل ما أدَّاهُ أحدهما رجع على الآخر بنصف ما أدى. وإنما قيد بعقدٍ واحدٍ ؛ حتَّى لو كاتبهما بعقدين فالكفالة لا تصح أصلاً ، أما إذا كانت بعقد واحد لا تصح قياساً. الأول: أنه يجوز أن يشتريا معاً صفقة واحدة وحينئذ لا إشكال كما لا يخفي على المتدبر، فليحمل مسألة المتن على هذه الصورة. الثاني : أنه يلزم قسمة الدين قبل القبض في الصورة الثانية ؛ لأن غير العاقد كَفَلَ جميع الدين الذي على العاقد، فعندما أدى يكون المؤدَّى على العاقد، وهو مشترك بين الشريكين على مقتضى تقديره، كما لا يخفي. الثالث : إن الدين الذي على العاقدِ إما أن يكون مشتركاً أو خاصة له، فعلى الثاني لا يصح قوله : فكلُّ ما يؤدى يؤديه منه ومن شريكه، وعلى الأَوَّل: لا يصح اعتبار الكفالة ؛ لأنها إما أن يصح مع الشركة، فليزم أن يكون كفيلاً عن نفسه، وإما مع القسمة فليزم قسمة الدين قبل القبض. فليتأمل. انتهى.
[١] قوله : عبدان...الخ ; المسألة على ثلاثة أوجه : الأول : أن يكاتبهما كتابة واحدة ، وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما كَفل عن صاحبه، فحكمه ما ذكره المصنف له من أن كل واحد منهما يرجعُ على الآخر بنصف ما أدّاه.
Two slaves who were jointly contracted as mukatab in one contract, and each was surety for his companion: each has recourse against the other for half of what he paid. That is, two slaves whose master said to them: 'I contract you both as mukatab for one thousand until a year,' and they accepted, and each became surety for his companion. So whatever one of them pays, he has recourse against the other for half of what he paid. This is restricted to a single contract; because if he contracted them in two contracts, the suretyship is invalid originally. But if it is in one contract, it is not valid by analogy. First: it is permissible for them to together purchase a single transaction, and then there is no difficulty, as is not hidden from the thoughtful one. So the issue of the matn should be carried on this form. Second: it necessitates division of the debt before receipt in the second form, because the non-contracting party guaranteed the entire debt that is upon the contractor, so when he pays, what is paid is upon the contractor, and it is shared between the two partners according to its valuation, as is not hidden. Third: the debt upon the contractor is either shared or specific to him. In the second case, his saying 'whatever he pays, he pays from himself and from his partner' is incorrect. In the first case, the consideration of suretyship is incorrect, because if it is valid with partnership, then he would be surety for himself, and if with division, then it necessitates division of the debt before receipt. So let it be contemplated. End. [1] He said: 'Two slaves...' etc. The issue has three aspects: first: he contracts them as mukatab in a single kitaba, and each of them is surety for his companion, so its ruling is what the author mentioned: that each of them has recourse against the other for half of what he paid.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement concerning the joint kitaba (contract of manumission by payment) of two slaves with mutual suretyship. The ruling is that if two slaves are contracted together in a single kitaba contract, and each becomes surety for the other, then whatever one pays, he can recover half from the other. The text explains that this applies only when the contract is a single contract; if there are two separate contracts, the suretyship is invalid. The commentary then discusses potential difficulties and resolves them by suggesting that the scenario should be understood as the two slaves jointly purchasing a single transaction (e.g., buying something together), which eliminates the issue of dividing the debt before receipt. Three points are raised: first, that the joint purchase resolves the difficulty; second, that dividing the debt before receipt would be problematic; third, that the debt must be shared to make the ruling consistent. The commentary concludes with a note that the issue has three aspects, and the first aspect is what the author mentioned.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitaba): A contract of manumission where a slave agrees to pay the master a sum of money in installments, becoming free upon completion.
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship; in this context, each slave guarantees the payment of the other.
عقد واحد (ʿaqd wāḥid): A single contract; the condition for the validity of mutual suretyship in joint kitaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for two slaves to have mutual recourse after paying in a joint kitaba?
A) The kitaba must be for a large sum.
B) The master must approve the suretyship separately.
C) The contract must be a single contract.
D) The slaves must be from the same household.

Key Takeaway
In a single kitaba contract with mutual suretyship, each slave recovers half of what he paid from the other, but this does not apply if the contracts are separate.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Kafalah of Two Mukatabs in One Contract: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Ruling on mutual kafalah between two mukatabs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutual kafalah between two mukatabs in a single kitabah contract and explain why it is valid by istihsan despite being invalid by qiyas.

Core Idea
A mutual guarantee between two mukatabs is valid by istihsan, not qiyas, because each is treated as both principal and surety.

This passage examines a nuanced scenario where a master grants a kitabah contract to two slaves in a single contract, and each guarantees the other's payment. The author explains that while strict analogy (qiyas) would invalidate such a guarantee, Hanafi jurists uphold it through istihsan (juristic preference). The lesson will unpack the three scenarios and the dispute with Zufar regarding the third case.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic scenario and the ruling from the matn
The matn states that if two mukatabs are contracted together with mutual kafalah, each has recourse against the other for half of what he paid. This is the first scenario.
The kafalah is mentioned explicitly.
2
Identify why qiyas would invalidate the guarantee
Two reasons: (1) A mukatab is not fit for gratuitous contracts (kafalah is tabarru'), and (2) the consideration of kitabah is not a valid debt for guarantee purposes.
Each reason alone would invalidate the kafalah; combined it is even more invalid by qiyas.
3
Grasp the istihsan reasoning that validates the contract
By istihsan, each mukatab is considered a principal (asil) regarding his own portion and a surety (kafil) for his companion. Thus the contract is valid, and the master can demand the full amount from either.
This overrides the qiyas objection.
4
Distinguish the three scenarios and the dispute with Zufar
Scenario 1: kafalah mentioned – each has recourse. Scenario 2: no kafalah, no condition – each liable only for his share. Scenario 3: condition that both pay in full for freedom – Abu Hanifa says neither freed until full payment; Zufar says each freed upon paying his share.
The third scenario is the point of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Hanafi view, one slave's payment does not free him until the other also pays; under Zufar's view, the paying slave becomes free immediately.
This difference affects the timing of freedom and the master's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبدان كوتبا بعقدٍ واحدٍ، وكَفِلَ كلّ عن صاحبه، رجع كلّ على الآخر بنصف ما أدى. وإنما قيد بعقدٍ واحدٍ؛ حتى لو كاتبهما بعقدين فالكفالة لا تصح أصلاً، أما إذا كانت بعقد واحد لا تصح قياساً لأنها كفالة ببدل الكتابة، وتصح استحساناً بأن يجعل كل منهما أصيلاً في حق وجوب الألف عليه.
والمسألة على ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: أن يكاتبهما كتابة واحدة، وكل واحد منهما كفل عن صاحبه، فحكمه ما ذكره المصنف له من أن كل واحد منهما يرجع على الآخر بنصف ما أدّاه.
الثاني: أن يكاتبهما كتابة واحدة ولم يزد على ذلك، فحكمه أن كلا منهما يلزمه حصته.
والثالث: أن يكاتبهما كتابة واحدة على أنهما إن أديا عتقا، وإن عجزا ردا في الرق، ولم يذكر كفالة كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه، فعندنا لا يعتق واحد منهما ما لم يصل جميع المال إلى المولى؛ لأن شرط المولى في العقد يجب مراعاته إذا كان صحيحاً شرعاً، وقد شرط العتق عند أدائهما جميع المال نصاً، فلو أعتق أحدهما بأداء حصته كان مخالفاً لشرطه. وعند زفر: يعتق بأداء حصته؛ لأن كل واحد منهما لم يلتزم بالقبول إلا حصته؛ ولهذا ليس للمولى أن يطالب أحدهما بجميع البدل، ولو أدى أحدهما الجميع لم يرجع على صاحبه بشيء، بخلاف ما إذا اشترط كفالة كل واحد منهما.
قال العلامة الزيلعي: ما استدل به زفر له ممنوع، فإن هذا عندنا كالفصل الأول في جميع ما ذكرنا؛ فلهذا قلنا ما لم يصل جميع المال إلى المولى لا يعتق واحد منهما، ذكره في «المبسوط». انتهى.
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: قوله بعقد واحد؛ احتراز عما إذا كانت كتابتهما بعقدين، فإن الكفالة حينئذ لا تصح كما نبه عليه الشارح له البارع.
وقوله: كفل كل عن صاحبه؛ احتراز عن الوجه الثاني والثالث المذكورين.
قوله: لأنه... الخ؛ تقريره: أنه شرط فيه كفالة المكاتب وكفالة بدل الكتابة، وكل واحد منهما بانفراده باطل، وعند الاجتماع أولى، فصار كما إذا اختلفت كتابتهما: أما كفالة المكاتب فوجه بطلانها أن الكفالة تبرع، والمكاتب لا يصلح للتبرع، وأما الكفالة ببدل الكتابة فوجه بطلانها أنها تقتضي ديناً صحيحاً، وبدل الكتابة ليس بدين صحيح. كما لا يخفى.
قوله: استحساناً بأن يجعل... الخ؛ تحريره: أن تصرف الإنسان يجب تصحيحه
Two slaves were given a kitabah contract in one single contract, and each one guaranteed on behalf of his companion: each one will recourse against the other for half of what he paid. It is qualified by 'in one contract' because if he gave them kitabah in two contracts, the guaranty is not valid at all. As for when it is in one contract, it is not valid by analogy because it is a guaranty for the consideration of kitabah, but it is valid by juristic preference (istihsan) by making each of them a principal in respect of the obligation of the thousand [dirhams] upon him. The case has three aspects: First: He gives them a single kitabah, and each one of them guarantees on behalf of his companion; its ruling is what the author mentioned, that each one of them will recourse against the other for half of what he paid. Second: He gives them a single kitabah without adding anything beyond that; its ruling is that each of them is obligated for his share. Third: He gives them a single kitabah on condition that if they pay they become free, and if they fail they are returned to slavery, without mentioning that each one guarantees on behalf of his companion. According to us, neither of them becomes free until all the wealth reaches the master, because the condition of the master in the contract must be observed if it is legally valid, and he explicitly conditioned freedom upon both of them paying all the wealth; so if one of them is freed by paying his share, it would violate his condition. According to Zufar: he becomes free by paying his share, because each one of them only committed himself by acceptance to his own share; therefore the master cannot demand the entire consideration from one of them, and if one pays the entire amount, he cannot recourse against his companion for anything, unlike if the guaranty of each one was stipulated. The great scholar al-Zayla'i said: What Zufar used as evidence for his view is objectionable, because this is according to us like the first chapter in all that we mentioned; therefore we say that neither of them becomes free until all the wealth reaches the master, as mentioned in al-Mabsut. End. When you know this, we say: His statement 'in one contract' is a limitation against when their kitabah is in two contracts, for then the guaranty is not valid as the capable commentator pointed out. And his statement 'each guaranteed on behalf of his companion' is a limitation against the second and third aspects mentioned. His statement 'because... etc.': its exposition: he included in it the guaranty of a mukatab and the guaranty for the consideration of kitabah; each one individually is invalid, and when combined it is more so, so it becomes like when their kitabah differs. As for the guaranty of the mukatab: the aspect of its invalidity is that guaranty is a gratuitous act, and a mukatab is not fit for a gratuitous act. As for the guaranty for the consideration of kitabah: the aspect of its invalidity is that it requires a valid debt, and the consideration of kitabah is not a valid debt. As is not hidden. His statement 'by istihsan by making... etc.': its analysis: that a person's action must be validated...
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a master gives a kitabah (contract of manumission by payment) to two slaves in a single contract, and each slave guarantees payment on behalf of the other. The matn states that each mukatab will have recourse against the other for half of what he paid. The commentary explains that this ruling is valid by istihsan (juristic preference) despite being invalid by qiyas (analogy). The reason qiyas would make it invalid is twofold: first, a mukatab is not fit for gratuitous contracts like kafalah (guaranty), and second, the consideration for kitabah is not a valid debt for guarantee purposes. However, istihsan validates it by treating each mukatab as a principal (asil) regarding his own portion and a guarantor (kafil) for his companion's portion. The master can then demand the full amount from either, and when one pays, he can recover half from the other. Three scenarios are distinguished: (1) The slaves are contracted together and each guarantees the other – ruling as per matn. (2) They are contracted together without mention of guarantee – each is only liable for his own share. (3) They are contracted together with the condition that freedom is contingent upon both paying in full – here, according to Abu Hanifa and the majority, neither is freed until the entire amount is paid, because the master's condition must be observed. Zufar dissents, arguing that each slave only accepted his own share, so paying his own share frees him. The commentator, citing al-Zayla'i, rejects Zufar's evidence and affirms the majority view. The guarantee phrase in the matn is a limitation: without it, the third scenario applies. The passage then refines the explanation of why the guarantee is invalid by qiyas but valid by istihsan, emphasizing that the kafalah includes two aspects, each individually invalid, but their combination is considered valid by istihsan because the overall transaction can be interpreted as making each a principal and surety.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of suretyship/guarantee, where one person (kafil) assumes responsibility for a debt or obligation of another.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitabah contract with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified amount.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The consideration or payment agreed upon in the kitabah contract for the slave's freedom.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling based on a stronger evidence or custom.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; deriving a ruling for a new case by comparing it to an existing case with a common effective cause ('illah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if two slaves are given a single kitabah contract without any mention of mutual kafalah, what is the ruling on their freedom?
A) Each becomes free upon paying his own share.
B) Neither becomes free until both pay the entire amount.
C) The first to pay becomes free immediately.
D) They both become free immediately upon signing the contract.

Key Takeaway
A mutual guarantee between two mukatabs in a single kitabah is valid by istihsan; in the absence of explicit guarantee, a condition of full payment creates joint liability for freedom.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Two Mukatib Slaves as Mutual Sureties
Ruling on mutual kafalah between two mukatib slaves with istihsan reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutual kafalah between two mukatib slaves and explain the istihsan reasoning that validates the recovery of half the payment.

Core Idea
Mutual kafalah between two mukatib slaves is valid: if one pays the entire kitabah, he recovers half from the other, preserving the unity of the contract.

This passage from the Hanafi chapter on Kafalah examines a specific case: two mukatib (kitabah-contract) slaves mutually guaranteeing each other's entire payment to the master. The ruling may appear counterintuitive because each slave initially owes half, yet the one who pays the full amount can recover half from the other. The text provides the reasoning based on istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid splitting the single transaction of the master. Through this lesson, you will understand the legal mechanism and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the contract
We have two mukatib slaves and a master. They are mutual sureties for the entire kitabah payment, meaning each guarantees the full amount to the master.
The kafalah is mutual: each is surety for the other.
2
Determine the initial obligation
In reality, the kitabah amount is counterbalanced by both slaves, so each initially owes half of the total sum.
The master, however, demands the full amount from each by way of principality (asālah), not by way of kafalah.
3
Examine the ruling on payment
If one slave pays the entire amount, he is freed immediately, and the other is freed as a follower (as in the case of the child of a mukatib). The paying slave then recovers half of what he paid from the other.
Recovery is exactly half because of equality in obligation.
4
Understand the istihsan reasoning
The objection: each owes half, so the payer should have no right to recover unless he pays more than his share. The Hanafi response: to avoid splitting the transaction (tafriq al-safqah) for the master, the payment is considered as falling for the entire substitute (kull al-badal), so half goes for the payer and half for his companion. This is istihsan overriding qiyas.
The equality (istiwā') refers to equality in the cause (kitabah) making the entire debt guaranteed on each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة الضمان وفالة الرجلين وكفالة العبد
۳۹۱
ويكون عتقهما معلقاً بأدائه، ويُجْعَلُ كفيلاً بالألف في حق صاحبه، فما أداه أحدهما رجع بنصفه " على الآخر لاستوائهما".
بقدر الإمكان، وقد أمكن تصحيح هذه الكفالة بأن يجعل المال كله على كلِّ واحدٍ منهما في حق المولى وفي حق نفسيه، وعتق الآخر يتعلق بأدائه، فيطالب المولى كل واحد منهما بجميع المال بحكم الأصالة لا بحكم الكفالة.
وأيهما أدى عتق، وعتق الآخر تبعاً له كما في ولد المكاتب، لكن كل واحدٍ منهما كفيل في حق صاحبه ؛ لأن المال في الحقيقةِ مقابل بهما حتى انقسم عليهما ، فصارت كفالة بما عليه أصالة، وكفالة المكاتب بما عليه أصالة جائزة، فكان كل واحد منهما كفيلاً عن صاحبه بالكل. ولا تظهر الكفالة إلا في حق صاحبه ؛ لأنها ضرورية فتقدر بقدرها، حتى ليكون مطالبة المولى كل واحد بجميع المال بحكم الأصالة لا يحكم الكفالة، فإذا أدى أحدهما شيئاً وقع عن كل البدل، فيقع نصف ذلك عن صاحبه لاستوائهما فيرجع به عليه، ولو رجع بالكل لا تتحقق المساواة بخلاف ما إذا اختلفت كتابتهما، فإن عتق كل واحد منهما تعلق بأداء المال على حدة، وهو صحيح في نفسه فلا حاجة لتصحيحه بهذا الطريق.
[١] قوله : رجع بنصفه ؛ فإن قيل : كيف يرجع على صاحبه بنصف ما أدى والمال في الحقيقة مقابل بهما، فيكون على كل واحد منهما نصفه، فيجب أن لا يكون له حق الرجوع ما لم يزد المؤدى على ما يخصه كما في مسألة الدور.
قلنا : إنما يرجع على صاحبه بنصف ما أدّى تحرزاً عن تفريق الصفقة على المولى؛ لأن المؤدى اسم مفعول، لو وقع عن المؤدِّي اسم فاعل على الخصوص يبرو بأدائه عن نصيبه فيعتق ؛ لأن المكاتب إذا برأ عمَّا عليه من البدل يعتق، والمولى شرط عليهما أن يؤديا جميعاً ويعتقا جميعاً. هكذا في «الكفاية»)، وغيرها.
وهي
[٢] قوله : لاستوائهما ؛ يعني في الوجوب لكونهما مستويين في العلـة ، الكتابة، فصار كل البدلِ مضموناً على كل واحد منهما ؛ ولهذا [لا] يعتق كلُّ واحدٍ منهما ما لم يؤد جميع المال.
Book of Kafalah, Suretyship, and the Kafalah of Two Men and the Kafalah of a Slave. 391. And their emancipation is suspended upon his payment, and he is made a surety for the thousand in the right of his companion. So whichever of them pays something, he recovers half of it from the other due to their equality. To the extent possible, and it is possible to validate this kafalah by making the entire wealth upon each one of them in the right of the master and in their own rights, and the emancipation of the other is attached to his payment. So the master demands from each one of them the entire wealth by way of principality, not by way of kafalah. Whichever of them pays, he is emancipated, and the other is emancipated as a follower, as in the case of the child of a mukatib. But each one of them is a surety in the right of his companion, because the wealth in reality is counterbalanced by both of them, such that it is divided between them. Thus it became a kafalah for what is upon him originally, and the kafalah of a mukatib for what is upon him originally is permissible. So each one of them was a surety for his companion for the whole. And the kafalah does not appear except in the right of his companion, because it is necessary, so it is measured by its measure. So that the master's demand of each one for the entire wealth is by way of principality, not by way of kafalah. So when one of them pays something, it falls for the entire substitute, so half of that falls for his companion due to their equality, and he recovers it from him. And if he recovers the whole, equality would not be realized, unlike the case where their kitabahs differ. For then the emancipation of each one is attached to the payment of the wealth separately, and that is valid in itself, so there is no need to validate it by this method. [1] His saying: 'He recovers half of it.' For if it is said: How does he recover from his companion half of what he paid, while the wealth in reality is counterbalanced by both, so each one owes half of it? So he should have no right of recovery unless what he paid exceeds his share, as in the case of the rotating debt. We say: He only recovers from his companion half of what he paid to avoid splitting the transaction for the master. Because the thing paid (al-mu'adda) is a passive participle; if it were to fall specifically for the payer (al-mu'addi) as an active participle, he would be discharged by his payment from his share and be emancipated, because when a mukatib is discharged from what is upon him of the substitute, he is emancipated. And the master stipulated that they both pay and both be emancipated. Thus in al-Kifayah and others. And it is [2] His saying: 'Due to their equality.' Meaning in obligation because they are equal in the cause, kitabah. So the entire substitute became guaranteed upon each one of them. Therefore, none of them is emancipated unless he pays all the wealth.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Kafalah discusses the ruling of two mukatib slaves who are made mutual sureties for the entire kitabah payment. The author first states the ruling: the payment is divided between them, and if one pays, he can recover half from the other. The commentary then addresses an objection: since each initially owes half, why should one recover half from the other? The answer is based on istihsan (juristic preference) to preserve the unity of the contract (i.e., to avoid splitting the transaction for the master). The payment is considered as being made for the entire substitute, so half goes for the payer and half for his companion. The second commentary explains that 'due to their equality' refers to equality in the obligation because both are equal in the cause of kitabah, so the whole debt is guaranteed on each.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafālah): A contract of suretyship where one person guarantees the debt or obligation of another.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to purchase his freedom by paying a specified sum.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitābah): The payment agreed upon in the kitabah contract for the slave's freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two mukatib slaves who are mutual sureties for the entire kitabah, if one pays the full amount, how much does he recover from the other?
A) He recovers the entire amount.
B) He recovers half the amount.
C) He recovers nothing.
D) He recovers only if the other also pays.

Key Takeaway
Mutual kafalah between two mukatib slaves is valid; if one pays the full kitabah, he recovers half from the other, based on istihsan to avoid splitting the master's transaction.

Kafalah — Surety

Master Frees One Mukatib Before Payment: Effects on Kafalah Obligations
Ruling with proof on kafalah of mukatib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master frees one of two jointly-surrounded mukatib slaves, and explain the master's options for collecting the kitabah payment and the freed slave's right of recourse.

Core Idea
Freeing a mukatib cancels his personal share but not his kafalah; the master can collect from either as principal or surety.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition addresses a nuanced scenario in the law of kafalah (suretyship) involving two mukatib slaves who are each other's sureties. The master chooses to free one before any payment of the kitabah. The ruling clarifies how the remaining financial obligations are distributed and recovered. Understanding this case deepens your grasp of the interplay between emancipation, debt, and suretyship in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two mukatib slaves (A and B) are jointly liable for the kitabah, each serving as surety for the other. The master frees A before any payment is made.
This is the factual premise: joint kitabah with mutual kafalah.
2
State the ruling on validity and options
The master's act of freeing A is valid. The master may collect A's share (half the kitabah) either from B as principal (since B remains personally liable for that share) or from A as surety (since A is still bound by kafalah).
The master has a choice: proceed against the other slave directly or against the freed slave as guarantor.
3
Trace the reasoning
The wealth (kitabah) is in reality counterbalanced by the bodies of both slaves. Freeing A cancels his personal obligation because the means to emancipation is gone, but his kafalah remains because it was a means to secure the whole payment. The obligation is distributed between them.
The rationale: the master's right to the whole kitabah is not extinguished by freeing one.
4
Analyze the right of recourse
If the master collects from A (the freed slave) as surety, A can recover what he paid from B because A paid on B's behalf. If the master collects from B, B cannot recover from A because B paid his own share.
This follows general kafalah rules: the surety who pays has recourse against the principal.
5
Address the objection
An objection: collecting from the freed slave by kafalah validates a kafalah with a kitabah debt, which is allegedly invalid. The answer: originally, each was liable for the entire amount; the remaining liability is part of that original whole, so it retains the same character.
The objection is refuted by noting that the obligation is a continuing one.

Section on the Kafalah of the Slave and Concerning It. If the master frees one of them before payment, it is valid, and he may take the share of the one he did not free from him as principal and from the other as surety. And the freed one recovers from his companion what he paid on his behalf, but his companion does not recover from him what he paid on his own behalf. Section on the Kafalah of the Slave and Concerning It. [3] [Y] (If the master frees one of them before payment, it is valid). And he may take the share of the one he did not free from him as principal and from the other as surety. And the freed one recovers from his companion what he paid on his behalf, but his companion does not recover from him what he paid on his own behalf; because the wealth in reality is counterbalanced by their bodies, and it was only placed upon each of them to validate the kafalah. [1] His saying: 'If the master frees one of them' i.e., one of the two mukatib slaves in the case where he made a kitabah with both and stipulated kafalah of each for his companion. [2] His saying: 'It is valid'; his emancipation due to the presence of a valid cause for emancipation, which is ownership over the body. And he is discharged from his share of the kitabah payment; because he did not consent to undertaking the wealth except as a means to emancipation, and the means no longer exists, so his share falls away, and his share remains upon his companion; because the wealth in reality is counterbalanced by their bodies. It was only placed entirely upon each one of them as a choice to validate the guarantee. And when he attains emancipation, he no longer needs it, so it is considered counterbalanced by their bodies, thus it is distributed between them necessarily. (3) His saying: 'And he recovers...' i.e., if the master takes the share of the other from the freed one (by way of kafalah), the freed one recovers from the other what he paid; because it is paid on his behalf by his order. But if the other takes (from the freed one), he does not recover anything from the freed one; because he paid on his own behalf. For if it is said: The taking from the freed one by way of kafalah is a validation of kafalah with the kitabah payment, and that is invalid. It is answered: Because each one of them was demanded for the entire thousand, and the remainder is part of that, so it remains on that character; because remaining is based on the initial establishment. Mentioned in al-Durar.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This section deals with the specific case where the master frees one of the two mukatib slaves before any payment of the kitabah. The ruling is that the master can recover the freed slave's share (the half that was due from him) either from the other slave as principal (since that slave still owes that share) or from the freed slave as surety (since he is still a surety for his companion). However, if the master collects from the freed slave, the freed slave can recover that amount from the other slave because he paid on the other's behalf. But if the master collects from the other slave, that other slave cannot recover from the freed slave because he paid his own share. The commentary explains that the master's freeing of one slave cancels that slave's personal obligation for his share of the kitabah payment, but his kafalah obligation remains because it was a means to secure the whole payment. An objection is raised that collecting from the freed slave by kafalah is invalid because it validates kafalah with a kitabah debt, but the answer is that since both were originally liable for the whole, the remaining liability is a part of that original whole.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave by the master, which in this context cancels the freed slave's personal obligation for his share of the kitabah.
حصة (hiṣṣah): Share or portion; here it refers to the freed slave's share of the kitabah payment that he was obligated to pay.
ضماناً (ḍamānan): As surety; the master can take the share from the freed slave in his capacity as surety for the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the master frees one of two mukatib slaves before payment, from whom can the master recover the freed slave's share?
A) Only from the freed slave.
B) Only from the other slave.
C) From either the other slave as principal or from the freed slave as surety.
D) The master cannot recover the share because the freed slave is no longer obligated.

Key Takeaway
The master's freeing of one mukatib before payment does not extinguish his right to collect the freed slave's share from either the other slave or the freed slave as surety, and the freed slave can recover from the other if he pays as surety.

Manumission of a Joint Slave Surety
Ruling when master frees one of two joint slave sureties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master frees one of two joint slave sureties before payment, and explain how liability is distributed between them.

Core Idea
A freed slave's kitaba share drops, but he remains surety for the other, because the debt is on both necks.

This passage explores a nuanced scenario from kitaba (contract of manumission) where two slaves jointly guarantee each other's payment. When the master frees one slave before the debt is paid, the manumission is valid, but the liability does not simply vanish. Let's see how the Hanafi school balances the rights of the master and the freed slave through careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Two mukatab slaves (mukataban) have a kitaba agreement with their master, and each is a surety (kafil) for the other. The debt is a fixed amount (e.g., 1000 dirhams).
The suretyship is stipulated in the kitaba contract.
2
State the rule for manumission
If the master frees one slave before the debt is paid, the manumission is valid (sahih) because the master owns the slave and the right to free him.
Manumission is an act of piety; its validity depends on ownership of the neck.
3
Determine liability distribution
The freed slave's own share of the kitaba debt drops (he is no longer indebted for it), but the other slave remains liable for his share. The master may claim the other slave's share directly from him or, via the suretyship, from the freed slave.
The freed slave's suretyship obligation persists.
4
Analyse the reclaim rights
If the master takes the other slave's share from the freed slave (as surety), the freed slave can reclaim that amount from the other slave. However, the other slave cannot reclaim from the freed slave what he himself paid.
This is because the freed slave only paid on behalf of the other, not on his own behalf.
5
Understand the legal reasoning (ta‘lil)
The kitaba payment is considered to be in exchange for the slaves' bodies (neck). Therefore, the debt is distributed between them. The suretyship validates the guarantee; once freedom is obtained, the means (payment) for that freedom is dropped.
This is a qiyas (analogical reasoning) based on the nature of kitaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كفالة العبد وعنه فإن أعتق السيد أحدهما قبل الأداء صح ، وله أن يأخذ حصةَ مَن لم يعتقه منه أصالة ومن الآخر ضماناً، ورجع المعتق على صاحبه بما أدى عنه لا صاحبَهُ عليه بما أدى عن نفسه ؛ لأنَّ المالَ في الحقيقةِ مقابل برقبتهما ، وإنما جُعِلَ على كل منهما تصحيحا للكفالة. قوله : فإن أعتق السيد أحدهما ؛ أي أحد العبدين المكاتبين فيما إذا كاتبهما وشرط كفالة كل منهما عن صاحبه. قوله : صح ؛ اعتاقه لوجود الصحيح للعتق ، وهو الملك في الرقبة، وبرئ عن حصته من بدل الكتابة ؛ لأنه لم يرضَ بالتزام المال إلا ليكون وسيلة إلى العتق، ولم يبق وسيلة فتسقط حصته، وتبقى على صاحبه حصته ؛ لأن المال في الحقيقة مقابل برقبتهما. وإنما جعل كل على كلِّ واحدٍ منهما كله اختياراً لتصحيح الضمان، وإذا حصل له العتق استغنى عنه ، فاعتبر مقابلاً برقبتهما فيتوزع عليهما ضرورة. قوله : ورجع ...الخ ؛ يعني أن المولى إن أخذ حصة الآخر من العتق رجع المعتق بما يؤدي على الآخر؛ لأنه مؤدى عنه بأمره ، فإن أخذ الآخر لم يرجع على المعتق بشيء ؛ لأنه أدى على نفسه. فان قيل أخذ المعتق بالكفالة تصحيح للكفالة ببدل الكتابة، وهي باطلة. أجيب : بأن كل واحدٍ منهما كان مطالباً بجميع الألف، والباقي بعض ذلك ، فيبقى على تلك الصفة ؛ لأن البقاء يكون على وفق الثبوت ذكره في «الدرر»
Chapter on suretyship of the slave and from him. If the master frees one of them [the two slaves] before payment, it is valid, and he has the right to take the share of the one he did not free from him as principal, and from the other as guarantee. The freed slave reclaims from his partner what he paid on his behalf, but his partner does not reclaim from him what he paid on his own behalf, because the wealth in reality is in exchange for their necks [the slaves], and it was only placed on each of them to validate the suretyship. His statement: 'If the master frees one of them' meaning one of the two mukatab slaves in the case where he made a contract of kitaba with both and stipulated that each is a surety for his partner. His statement: 'it is valid' his manumission due to the existence of the valid cause for manumission, which is ownership of the neck, and he is released from his share of the kitaba payment because he did not consent to assume the wealth except as a means to freedom, and when the means no longer exists, his share falls away, and his partner's share remains, because the wealth in reality is in exchange for their necks. It was only placed on each of them entirely by choice to validate the guarantee, and when he attains freedom, he becomes independent of it, so it is considered as being in exchange for their necks, so it is necessarily distributed between them. His statement: 'and he reclaims...' etc. means that if the master takes the share of the other from the freed slave, the freed slave reclaims what he paid from the other, because it was paid on his behalf by his order; if the other takes it, he does not reclaim anything from the freed slave because he paid on his own behalf. If it is said: taking from the freed slave through the suretyship validates the suretyship with the kitaba payment, and it is invalid. The answer is that each of them was demanded for the entire thousand, and the remaining part is some of that, so it remains in that condition because continuation is according to establishment, as mentioned in al-Durar.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This is an expansion_probe passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. The author explains a ruling from the matn about freeing one of two slaves who are joint sureties in a kitaba contract. The main ruling states that if the master frees one slave before payment, the manumission is valid. The freed slave is only liable for his share of the kitaba debt; the master may collect the share from the other slave either as principal (directly) or from the freed slave via the guarantee (if he chooses). However, the freed slave can reclaim from the other slave what he paid on the other's behalf, but not vice versa. The reasoning is that the kitaba payment is essentially in exchange for both slaves' necks (bodies), so it is distributed between them. The commentary clarifies that the manumission is valid because the master owns the slave. The freed slave's share of the debt is dropped because he only agreed to the payment as a means to freedom, which is now achieved. The suretyship is validated to make the guarantee effective. The objection about the suretyship being invalid if the freed slave pays is answered: each slave was originally liable for the entire amount, so the remaining payment is just part of that original liability.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafāla): Suretyship; here used for a slave guaranteeing payment of another's kitaba debt.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted with his master for manumission in exchange for a set payment (kitaba).
ضمان (ḍamān): Guarantee; the obligation to pay the debt if the principal fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a master frees one of two joint slave sureties (mukatab) before payment, why does the freed slave's share of the kitaba debt drop?
A) Because the freed slave is now free and cannot be held liable for any debt.
B) Because he only consented to the payment as a means to freedom, which is now achieved.
C) Because the master forfeits his right to collect from the freed slave.
D) Because the other slave becomes solely liable for the entire amount.

Key Takeaway
A freed slave-surety's own share of the kitaba debt drops, but he remains a surety for the other's share, and can reclaim if he pays on the other's behalf.

Kafalah — Surety

Free Person's Suretyship for a Slave's Deferred Debt
Expansion of a matn ruling using qiyas khafi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on absolute suretyship for a slave's deferred debt, apply the analogy of the absent/bankrupt principal, and identify the effective cause (illah) that makes the surety immediately liable.

Core Idea
A free surety for a slave's deferred debt must pay immediately because the slave's insolvency is personal, not a valid term.

When a free person gives an absolute suretyship (kafala mutlaqa) for a debt that a restricted slave (mahjoor) will only owe after manumission, does the surety have to pay now or wait? The Hanafi school rules that the surety is immediately liable. This lesson unpacks the reasoning: the debt is certain, but the slave's deferment is due only to his insolvency (his earnings belong to his master), which does not affect the solvent surety. The commentator draws a powerful analogy with suretyship for an absent or bankrupt person, showing that the cause of liability (the debt and absolute suretyship) outweighs the personal impediment of the principal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is a free person's absolute suretyship for a debt that the slave principal owes but is not obligated to pay until after manumission. The ruling is that the surety is liable immediately (hukm: wajib).
The slave's debt is itself not yet due because of his restricted status and insolvency.
2
Locate the analogy (asl)
The author compares this to suretyship for an absent person (gha'ib) or a bankrupt (muflis). In those cases, the surety is liable immediately even though the principal is not.
The analogy is based on the common illah: the debt exists, but a personal impediment delays the principal's liability; the surety has no such impediment.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (effective cause)
The effective cause (illah) is the combination of a confirmed debt on the principal and an absolute suretyship without a fixed term. The principal's insolvency is a personal impediment that does not transfer to the surety. Manumission cannot be a valid term because its time is uncertain and it may never occur.
This is a khafiyyah (hidden) analogy because the impediment is not evident from the contract itself.
4
Note the objection and Hanafi response
Some might argue that manumission itself is a term, so the surety's liability should also be deferred. The Hanafi response: manumission is not a valid term due to uncertainty (jahala) – it has no fixed time and might not happen.
A fixed postponement (e.g., one year) would be a valid term, but manumission is not.
5
Apply the ruling to other cases
The passage also clarifies exceptions: debts that are immediately binding on the slave (e.g., from obvious consumption or trade with the master's permission) can be guaranteed without this complication. The surety may reclaim from the slave after manumission.
If the surety pays, he has recourse against the slave after the slave is freed and solvent.

ومال لا يجب على عبدٍ حَتَّى يُعتقَ حال على مَن كَفِلَ به مطلقةً، ولو أدَّى رجع عليه بعد عتقه: أقرَّ عبد محجور بمال، فالمال لا يجب عليه إلا بعد العتقِ ، وإِن كَفِلَ به حُرِّ كفالة مطلقة : أي لم يتعرض للحلولِ والتَّأجيلِ يَجِبُ عليه حالاً ؛ لأنَّ المانع من الحلول في ذمّة العبد أنه معسر ؛ لأنَّ جميع ما في يده لمولاه، ولا مانع في الكفيل. قوله : مال ؛ مبتدأ موصوف. وقوله : لا يجب على عبد ؛ أي العبد متعلق بقوله : لا يجب. وقوله : حال ؛ خبر. وقوله : على من كفل به مطلقة ؛ أي كفالة مطلقة متعلق بقوله : حال. فمعناه أن المال الذي لا يجب على العبد إلا بعد عتقه، وهو دين لم يظهر في حق مولاه، بل هي في حقه يؤاخذ بعد عتقه، كما إذا أقرَّ باستهلاك مال وكذَّبه المولى، أو أقرضه أحدٌ أو باعه، وهو محجور، فإن ذلك كله لا يجب بعد عتقه. وكذا إذا أودع شيئاً، فإن كَفل حرّ بذلك المال مطلقة عن قيد الحلول والتأجيل لزمه حالاً ؛ لأن المال مالٌ على العبد لوجود السبب وقبوله الذمة وعدم الأجل، وكيف والعتق لا يصلح أجلاً لجهالة وقت وقوعه، وقد لا يقع أصلاً، والمطالبة إنّما تأخرة عنه بعسرته ؛ لأن هذه الديون لا تتعلق برقبته لعدم ظهورها في حق المولى، والكفيل غيرُ معسر. فالمانع الذي تحقق في الأصيل منتف عن الكفيل مع وجود المقتضى، وهوا الكفالة المطلقة بمال غير مؤجل، كما إذا تكفّل عن غائب أو مفلس يلزمه في الحال، مع أن الأصيل لا يلزمه، بخلاف ما إذا تكفل بدين مؤجل ؛ لأنه التزم المطالبة بدين مؤجل، وليس للمطالب أن يطالب بالدين المؤجل في الحال. فقوله : لا يجب على عبد حتى يعتق ؛ احتراز عمَّا يجب عليه في الحال مثل دين الاستهلاك عياناً ، أو دين لزمه بالتجارة بإذن المولى، فإنه تجوز به الكفالة بلا شبهة.
And wealth that is not obligatory upon a slave until he is freed is due immediately upon the one who guarantees it with an absolute suretyship, and if he pays, he reclaims (the amount) from him after his manumission. A restricted slave acknowledges a debt of wealth: the wealth is not obligatory upon him except after manumission. If a free person guarantees it with an absolute suretyship - i.e., he does not specify postponement or term - it becomes due immediately, because the impediment to it being due in the slave's liability is that he is insolvent, as everything in his hand belongs to his master, and there is no impediment in the surety. His statement: 'wealth' is a qualified subject. His statement: 'is not obligatory upon a slave' i.e., the slave is related to 'not obligatory'. His statement: 'is due' is the predicate. His statement: 'upon the one who guarantees it with an absolute suretyship' i.e., an absolute suretyship relates to 'is due'. So its meaning is that the wealth that is not obligatory upon the slave except after his manumission, which is a debt that has not appeared in the right of his master, but rather he is held liable for it after his manumission, like if he acknowledges consuming wealth and the master denies it, or someone lends him or sells to him while he is restricted, all of that is not obligatory after his manumission. Similarly, if he deposits something, a free person who guarantees that wealth with an absolute suretyship without the condition of postponement or term becomes liable immediately, because the wealth is a liability upon the slave due to the existence of the cause and his liability's acceptance, and there is no term. Moreover, manumission cannot serve as a term due to the ignorance of its occurrence time, and it may not occur at all. The demand of payment is only delayed from him because of his insolvency, because these debts are not attached to his neck due to their non-appearance in the right of the master, and the surety is not insolvent. Therefore, the impediment that existed in the principal is absent from the surety, while the effective cause exists, and that is the absolute suretyship for an unpostponed debt, like if he guarantees for an absent person or a bankrupt, it becomes binding immediately, even though it does not bind the principal. This is unlike if he guarantees a postponed debt, because he has committed to demanding a postponed debt, and the claimant cannot claim a postponed debt immediately. So his statement: 'is not obligatory upon a slave until he is freed' excludes what is obligatory immediately, like the debt of obvious consumption, or a debt that bound him through trade by the master's permission, for then it is permissible to guarantee it without doubt.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is an expansion_probe that explains a ruling from the matn about a free person acting as surety for a debt that a slave will only owe after manumission. The main ruling states that such a debt becomes immediately due upon the free surety if the suretyship is absolute (not tied to a term). The slave's debt is deferred only because of his insolvency (since his earnings belong to his master), not because of a valid future term like manumission, which is uncertain. The commentator draws an analogy with suretyship for an absent or bankrupt person: the surety is liable immediately even though the principal is not. The key point is that the impediment (the slave's insolvency) does not apply to the free surety, and the cause of liability (the debt itself and the absolute suretyship) is present. The passage also clarifies exceptions: debts that are immediately binding on the slave (e.g., from obvious consumption or trade with the master's permission) can be guaranteed without such complication. If the surety pays, he can claim reimbursement from the slave after the slave is freed.

Key Terms
كفالة مطلقة (kafāla muṭlaqa): An absolute suretyship without specifying a term or condition of postponement.
محجور (maḥjūr): A restricted slave (or minor) whose dealings are limited by law; here a slave whose master has imposed restrictions on his financial transactions.
معسر (muʿsir): Insolvent; one who does not have assets to pay a debt. Here the slave is considered insolvent because his earnings belong to his master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a free surety immediately liable for a debt that a slave will only owe after manumission?
A) Because manumission is a fixed future event.
B) Because the surety's contract implicitly includes a term equal to manumission.
C) Because the slave's insolvency is a personal impediment that does not affect the surety.
D) Because the master guarantees the surety's payment.

Key Takeaway
A surety's immediate liability for a slave's deferred debt hinges on the personal nature of the slave's insolvency; the surety must pay now and can reclaim after manumission.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Absolute Guarantee for a Restricted Slave's Deferred Debt
Two rulings on kafalah with proof and detailed conditions for recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an absolute guarantee (kafalah mutlaqah) for a restricted slave's deferred debt is immediately binding on the guarantor, and identify the conditions for recourse based on whose order the guarantee was given.

Core Idea
An absolute guarantee for a deferred debt on a restricted slave is immediately binding because the obstacle (insolvency) affects only the slave, not the guarantor.

This lesson examines two interconnected rulings from the Hanafi school on kafalah (suretyship) involving restricted slaves. The passage clarifies a critical distinction: although the slave's debt is deferred until emancipation, a free guarantor's absolute guarantee becomes due immediately. The proof uses an analogy (qiyas) with guaranteeing for an absentee or bankrupt person. We will also explore the detailed conditions for the guarantor's recourse after payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Context
Read the matn lines: 'And a debt that is not obligatory upon a slave until he is freed is immediately due upon the one who guaranteed it absolutely, and if he pays, he recovers from him after his emancipation.' Recognize that we have a restricted slave (abd mahjur) and a free guarantor giving a kafalah mutlaqah.
The slave's debt is valid but not due until emancipation due to his legal incapacity.
2
Understand the Obstacle to Immediate Liability
The slave's immediate liability is prevented by his insolvency (i'sar) – all his property belongs to the master. The guarantor is not insolvent, so the obstacle is absent for him.
The obstacle (man') is the slave's inability to pay, not a term (ajal) set on the debt.
3
Apply the Hanafi Principle: Guarantor's Independent Liability
The guarantor's liability is independent of the principal's ability to pay. The effective cause (illa) from the analogy: the obstacle (i'sar) is specific to the principal and does not transfer to the guarantor.
Contrast with a deferred debt: if a term is specified, the guarantor is also not immediately liable because he committed to a term.
4
Examine the Dalil – Qiyas with Absentee/Bankrupt
The text cites analogy: just as guaranteeing for an absentee (gha'ib) or bankrupt (muflis) is immediately binding on the guarantor even though the principal is not, so too here. Identify the common illa: the obstacle is personal to the principal.
The dalil is zanni in thubut and dalala, but sufficient for a masa'alah.
5
Distinguish Recourse Scenarios
Two situations: if the guarantee was by the slave's order, recourse is after emancipation; if by the master's order, recourse is immediate against the master. Also distinguish debts that are immediately due (e.g., consumption) vs. deferred.
This nuance comes from later commentary and is the mufta bihi position.

كتاب الكفالة الضمان وكفالة الرجلين وكفالة العبد
ومال لا يجب على عبدٍ حَتَّى يُعتَقَ حال على مَن كَفِلَ به مطلقةً، ولو أدَّى رجع عليه بعد عتقه
شرح: "ومال لا يجب على عبدٍ حَتَّى يُعتَقَ حال على مَن كَفِلَ به مطلقة)" أقرَّ عبد محجور بمال، فالمال لا يجب عليه إلا بعد العتقِ، وإن كَفِلَ به حُرّ كفالة مطلقة: أي لم يتعرض للحلولِ والتَّأجيلِ يَجِبُ عليه حالاً؛ لأنَّ المانع من الحلول في ذمّة العبد أنه معسر؛ لأنَّ جميع ما في يده لمولاه، ولا مانع في الكفيل. (ولو أدى رجع عليه بعد عتقه) صفة. وحتى يعتق: قوله: مال؛ مبتدأ موصوف. وقوله: لا يجب على عبد؛ أي العبد متعلق بقوله: لا يجب. وقوله: حال؛ خبر. وقوله: على من كفل به مطلقة؛ أي كفالة مطلقة متعلق بقوله: حال. فمعناه أن المال الذي لا يجب على العبد إلا بعد عتقه، وهو دين لم يظهر في حق مولاه، بل هو في حقه يؤاخذ بعد عتقه، كما إذا أقرَّ باستهلاك مال وكذَّبه المولى، أو أقرضه أحدٌ أو باعه، وهو محجور، فإن ذلك كله لا يجب بعد عتقه. وكذا إذا أودع شيئاً، فإن كَفَل حرّ بذلك المال مطلقة عن قيد الحلول والتأجيل لزمه حالاً؛ لأن المال مالٌ على العبد لوجود السبب وقبوله الذمة وعدم الأجل، وكيف والعتق لا يصلح أجلاً لجهالة وقت وقوعه، وقد لا يقع أصلاً، والمطالبة إنما تأخره عنه بعسرته؛ لأن هذه الديون لا تتعلق برقبته لعدم ظهورها في حق المولى، والكفيل غير معسر. فالمانع الذي تحقق في الأصيل منتف عن الكفيل مع وجود المقتضى، وهو الكفالة المطلقة بمال غير مؤجل، كما إذا تكفّل عن غائب أو مفلس يلزمه في الحال، مع أن الأصيل لا يلزمه، بخلاف ما إذا تكفل بدين مؤجل؛ لأنه التزم المطالبة بدين مؤجل، وليس للمطالب أن يطالب بالدين المؤجل في الحال. فقوله: لا يجب على عبد حتى يعتق؛ احتراز عمَّا يجب عليه في الحال مثل دين الاستهلاك عياناً، أو دين لزمه بالتجارة بإذن المولى، فإنه تجوز به الكفالة بلا شبهة. هذا ما أفاده الزَّيْلَعِي، لكن قال في «البحر»: وهو سهو، وأفاد أن حكم ما يظهر، أي إن أدى الكفيل، وكانت الكفالة بأمرِ العبدِ عليه بعد عتقه.
(ولو مات عبد مكفول برقبته، وأقيم بينةٌ أَنَّه لمدَّعيهِ ضَمِنَ كفيلُهُ قيمته) وهو ما يؤاخذ به للحال أولى كدين الاستهلاك عياناً، وما لزمه بالتجارة بإذن المولى. انتهى.
قوله: بأمر العبد؛ بقي ما لو كفل بدين الاستهلاك المعاين، قال في «الفتح»: ينبغي أن يرجع قبل العتق إذا أدى؛ لأنه دين غير مؤخَّر إلى العتق، فيطلبُ السيد بتسليمه رقبته أو القضاء عنه، وبحث أهل الدرس: هل المعتبر في هذا الرجوع الأمر بالكفالة من العبد أو السيد، وقوي عندي الثاني؛ لأن الرجوع في الحقيقة على السيد. انتهى. وقال في «النهر»: ورأيت مقيّداً عندي إنما قوي عنده هو المذكور في «البدائع»، قال: ظاهر؛ فلو كانت بأمر العبد لا يرجع عليه إلا بعد العتق. فالحاصل أن ضمان العبدِ فيما لا يؤاخذه حالاً صحيح، والرجوع عليه بعد العتق إن كان بأمره وضمانه فيما يؤاخذ به حالاً إن كان بأمر السيد صح، ورجع به حالاً عليه، وإن كان بأمر العبد صح ورجَعَ به عليه بعد العتق، كذا يؤخذ من كلامهم. انتهى. كذا في رد المختار. قوله: رجع عليه؛ لو أعتقه؛ لأن الكفيل بالأداء ملك الدين، وقام مقام الطالب، فلا يطالبه قبل الحرية. قوله: ولو مات، بأن ثبت موتُه ببرهان ذي اليد أو بتصديق المدعي، فلو لم يكن ثمة برهان ولا تصديق لم يقبل قول ذي اليد: إنه مات، بل يحبس هو والكفيل، فإن طال الحبس ضَمِن القيمة، وكذا الوديعة المحجورة. كذا في «النهاية». قوله: وأقيم؛ أي أقام المدعي بينةً أن ذلك العبد ملكه فاحترز به عمَّا إذا ثبت ملكه بإقرار ذي اليد، أو نكوله عن اليمين حيث يقضى بقيمة العبد الميت على المدعى عليه.
Book of Kafalah, Guarantee, Kafalah of two men, and Kafalah of the slave. And a debt that is not obligatory upon a slave until he is freed is immediately due upon the one who guaranteed it absolutely, and if he pays, he recovers from him after his emancipation. Explanation: "And a debt that is not obligatory upon a slave until he is freed is immediately due upon the one who guaranteed it absolutely)" A restricted slave acknowledged a debt, so the debt is not obligatory upon him except after emancipation, but if a free man guaranteed it absolutely—i.e., without specifying immediacy or deferment—it becomes obligatory upon him immediately because the obstacle to immediacy in the slave's liability is his insolvency, since everything in his hand belongs to his master, and there is no obstacle in the guarantor. (And if he pays, he recovers from him after his emancipation) as a description. Until he is freed: His statement: 'debt' is a modified subject. His statement: 'is not obligatory upon a slave' i.e., the slave is attached to his statement 'is not obligatory'. His statement: 'immediately' is the predicate. His statement: 'upon the one who guaranteed it absolutely' i.e., absolute guarantee attached to his statement 'immediately'. So its meaning is that the debt which is not obligatory upon the slave except after his emancipation, and it is a debt that did not appear in the right of his master, but rather in his right he will be taken to task after his emancipation, just as if he acknowledged consumption of property and the master denied it, or someone lent him or sold to him while he was restricted, then all of that is not obligatory except after his emancipation. Likewise, if he deposited something, then a free man guarantees that property absolutely without the restriction of immediacy or deferment, it is binding upon him immediately because the property is a debt upon the slave due to existence of cause and acceptance of liability, and absence of a term, and how can emancipation serve as a term when its time of occurrence is unknown, and it may not occur at all, and the claim is only delayed from him due to his insolvency; because these debts do not attach to his person due to their not appearing in the right of the master, and the guarantor is not insolvent. So the obstacle that existed in the principal is absent from the guarantor with the existence of the effective cause, which is the absolute guarantee of non-deferred property, just as if he guarantees for a missing or bankrupt person it is binding upon him immediately, even though the principal is not bound by it, unlike if he guarantees a deferred debt because he has committed to claim a deferred debt, and the claimant cannot demand the deferred debt immediately. So his statement: 'it is not obligatory upon a slave until he is freed' is a precaution against what is obligatory upon him immediately like the debt of explicit consumption or a debt incurred by trade with the master's permission, for then the guarantee is valid without doubt. This is what al-Zayla'i said, but he said in 'al-Bahr': it is an oversight, and he stated that the ruling of what appears—i.e., if the guarantor pays and the guarantee was by order of the slave—it is upon him after his emancipation. (And if a slave guaranteed with his person dies, and evidence is established that he belongs to the claimant, his guarantor is liable for his value) which is what is taken to account immediately, even more so like the debt of explicit consumption and what is incurred by trade with the master's permission. End. His statement: 'by order of the slave'; there remains the case if he guaranteed a debt of explicit consumption. He said in 'al-Fath': one should recover before emancipation if he pays because it is a debt not deferred until emancipation, so he requests the master to hand over his person or pay on his behalf. The people of study debated: in this recourse, is the considered factor the order for the guarantee from the slave or the master? And the second is strengthened in my view because the recourse is actually against the master. End. And he said in 'al-Nahr': I saw a restriction with me that what he strengthened is the one mentioned in 'al-Bada’i', he said: apparent; so if it was by order of the slave, he does not recover from him except after emancipation. So the summary is that the guarantee of the slave regarding what he is not immediately liable for is valid, and recourse is after emancipation if it was by his order; and his guarantee regarding what he is immediately liable for, if it was by order of the master, is valid and he recovers immediately from him; and if it was by order of the slave, it is valid and he recovers from him after emancipation. This is as understood from their statements. End. Likewise in Radd al-Mukhtar. His statement: 'he recovers from him'; if he emancipates him, because the guarantor by payment owns the debt and stands in the place of the claimant, so he cannot claim him before freedom. His statement: 'and if he dies' i.e., his death is established by proof of the possessor of the hand or by the claimant's confirmation; if there is no proof nor confirmation, the statement of the possessor that he died is not accepted, but rather he and the guarantor are imprisoned; if the imprisonment becomes long, he guarantees the value. Likewise the restricted deposit. As in 'al-Nihayah'. His statement: 'and evidence is established' i.e., the claimant presented evidence that that slave is his property; this avoids the case where ownership is established by the possessor's admission or his refusal of oath, where the value of the dead slave is adjudged against the defendant.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Wiqayah, dealing with kafalah (suretyship) involving slaves. Two main rulings are expounded. First Ruling (lines 1-2 of matn): If a restricted slave (abd mahjur) incurs a debt that is not due until his emancipation (e.g., by acknowledgement, borrowing, or purchase without permission), and a free person gives an absolute guarantee (kafalah mutlaqah) for that debt—i.e., without specifying a due date—the guarantee becomes immediately binding (hāl) on the guarantor. This is because the obstacle preventing the slave's immediate liability is his insolvency (all his property belongs to his master), but the guarantor is not insolvent. The guarantor's recourse against the slave is only after emancipation, because the debt itself is deferred until then. The text supports this by analogy: guaranteeing for an absentee or bankrupt person is also immediately binding on the guarantor even though the principal is not immediately liable. An important exception: if the slave's debt is immediately due (e.g., for explicit consumption or trade with the master's permission), then the guarantee is valid without any issue, and the guarantor can recourse immediately. Second Ruling (later matn): If a slave who is the subject of a kafalah (i.e., guaranteed as a person) dies, and the claimant proves with evidence (bayyinah) that the slave is his property, then the guarantor is liable for the slave's value. This is even more applicable to debts that are immediately due. The commentary then discusses detailed conditions for recourse: whether the guarantee was by order of the slave or the master. The relied-upon position, as transmitted from later authorities (e.g., al-Bada’i', al-Nahr), is that if the guarantee was by the master's order, the guarantor can recourse immediately against the master; if by the slave's order, recourse is only after the slave's emancipation. The text also clarifies procedural points: the death must be proven by evidence or the claimant's confirmation; otherwise, the possessor and guarantor are imprisoned until the matter is resolved.

Key Terms
كفالة مطلقة (kafalah mutlaqah): An unconditional guarantee without specifying the time of payment (neither immediate nor deferred).
عبد محجور (abd mahjur): A slave legally restricted from financial transactions because ownership vests in the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A restricted slave incurs a debt by acknowledging a loan without his master's permission. A free man gives an absolute guarantee (kafalah mutlaqah) for this debt. When is the guarantor's payment due?
A) Only after the slave is emancipated.
B) Immediately, because the guarantee is absolute.
C) At a time agreed upon by the guarantor and the slave.
D) Only if the master approves the guarantee.

Key Takeaway
The absolute guarantee of a restricted slave's deferred debt becomes immediately binding on the guarantor due to the absence of the obstacle of insolvency, and recourse depends on whose order the guarantee was given.
